The body of each chapter is its author’s own account. Notes marked the Curator reconcile the voices; the shaded margins of the Veiled Hand are for the Game Master alone.

Preface of the Curator

I am the Magister-Compiler of the Crystal Seat, and this book is not mine. Not a word of it, save these. What follows was written by many hands across many centuries and many tongues — by a priest of Xebech who has outlived empires, by a friar of Solanthya who believes his Church could do no wrong, by a sea-navigator who trusted his ledger more than his gods, by a Dark-Elf loremaster who holds the rest of us in gentle contempt. I have only gathered them, set them in order, and here and there added a line where two of them call the same thing by different names, or swear to events that cannot both be true.

Do not mistake this for confusion. It is the truest map of Ymir I could make. A world is not what a single scholar declares it to be; it is the quarrel of everyone who has ever tried to declare it. I have let them quarrel.

We stand now near three thousand five hundred and eighteen years after the Maelstrom — deep in the calm the histories call the New Equilibrium, the long exhale after the age of storms. From this quiet shore the narrators look back, and so shall you.

How to read this book

  • The body of each chapter is its author's own account — their beliefs, the knowledge of their age, and their prejudices, left intact. Trust it as far as you would trust the one who wrote it.
  • Where I have something to add, you will find it marked Curator's note. I try to be brief.
  • And — a warning I give once — there are margins in this book that I did not write, and cannot account for. They appear in a hand that the copyists could not reproduce and the eye does not like to rest upon. They contradict the text they sit beside. I have left them, because I have come to fear they are the only wholly honest thing between these covers.

The Order of This Book

Set down at the binders' insistence, and against my own preference for letting a reader be ambushed. The attributions are the narrators' own; the small judgements are mine. — the Curator

  • I · Cosmogony — Xochiyayotl, Xattva Loremaster of Xuthal, Star-Priest of the Cult of the Stars. The making of all things, from the one tongue old enough to have been told it.
  • II · Of the Gods — Fra' Teodabir, torch-priest of Hokhmah, of the Brotherhood of the Torch. Every creed on Ymir, reconciled. Very nearly.
  • III · Of Magic — Sir Drusilde, Archmage of the Seventh Circle of Armora; dated 3027. The Essence weighed, measured, and — her word, never mine — governed.
  • IV · Of the Ages — the Annalist of Solanthya, chronicler-friar of the Clericate of Mithra. Every year in its order. Trust his dates; audit his judgements.
  • V · The Lay of the Land — the sea-diary of Ramiro Estioca of Cartosa. The world from a masthead, which is how it actually looks.
  • VI · Letia — the Annalist, returning. His mother's house. He counts the windows.
  • (Here follows an unnumbered leaf: A Leaf Passed by the Censors of Solanthya — a facsimile, struck through where the censors earned their bread. I bind what I am given.)
  • VII · The Young Kingdoms — "The Armora Crier," a broadsheet-man. Progress, at volume, with advertisements.
  • VIII · Helgedad — Hrafnkel Ognirsson, skald of the Nortmaar. Cold country, long memory, no apologies.
  • IX · Qi-Long — the Pilgrim of the Thundering Gate. Six kingdoms, six dragons, one road walked slowly.
  • IX·B · A Letter to a Dead Pupil — Xebech — Xochiyayotl, returning: a letter addressed to Sir Drusilde, five centuries dead, deposited unasked at the Crystal Seat. I did not solicit it, and I could not have refused it.
  • X · Cartosa — Ramiro Estioca, returning, with a Blue Mantles factor's ledgers. Home waters. The diary ends where the diarist did.
  • X·B · The Sounding-Report of Ssas'Kher — delivered to the Council of Peoples; rendered from the trade-pidgin by a Grey-elf broker. Numbered soundings, no adjectives, and one measurement the whole world turns on.
  • X·C · The Silver Isles & the Council — the Curator, in body-text at last. My own ground. The reader will find that the margins have opinions about me. That seems only fair.
  • XI · Gaerwath & the Elves — Aelryn Vhaeloth i-Celeblyar, Dark-Elf Loremaster of Morannagul the Eternal. The rest of us, ranked from above.
  • XII · The Sadhir Deserts — Adhima the Rakhama'i, Sarradhi lore-keeper; with a human caravan-scribe's rebuttals. The Wheel, and one man who declines to turn with it.
  • XII·B · Ophiur — Nyema of Ga'Namesh, Keeper of the Thousand Songs of the Ivory Coalition. She crossed three seas to correct my blank map. The map deserved it.
  • XII·C · The Book of the Sleeping Shore — the Dreamlands — a retired dream-pilot of a licensed Armorite parlour, thirty years in the trade. Where the sleeper goes; what the deeper shores cost; who keeps the doors.
  • XII·D · The Speaker of the Tally — the Belligerent Kingdoms — an envoy of a Khan's tribute-court, her words taken down at the Crystal Seat through two interpreters, who do not agree. The captors' ledger, in the captors' words. It was owed a hearing.
  • XIII · Of the Peoples — Pips Quondam, a Tal-Tanoth traveller. Everyone, observed from knee height, which misses nothing.
  • XIV · Of Beasts — a huntsman's field-book; a ranger of Therìos. Nineteen names in the old lists, their tracks, and their prices.
  • XIV·B · Figures of Renown — an Inquisition record — a dossier of the Clericate of Mithra, obtained rather than gifted. The heroes and horrors of the late ages, as the Church files them: alphabetically, and under suspicion.
  • XV · Behind the Veil (GM) — set down by the hand that wrote the margins. I did not commission this chapter and cannot account for it. Players: your book ends at XIV·B.
  • XV·B · The Veiled Hand's Workbook (GM) — the same hand, in a working temper. For the one who runs the world; unfinished business, sorted by region.
  • (Here follows an unnumbered closing leaf: A Note Left Unanswered. It is mine, and I will say nothing else about it.)
  • Appendices A–K — the Curator's own. Of the years (A); of the calendar (B); of monies (C); a register of persons (D); a glossary (E); of the tongues (F); of herbs, draughts & dusts (G); a register of instruments & materials (H); doors into Ymir, for the one who runs the world (I — GM); of the plates, and an index of places (J); and of the making of this book (K).
In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

You who read the margins: read them alone. Everything the bright text tells is believed by those who tell it, and almost all of it is a lie told to the tellers. The gods are real. So are the things that wear the gods. Turn the page, Curator's guest, and I will show you the seam.

Herewith, then, the Compendium — beginning where all things begin, with the making of the world.

Colophon. Framing device for the World Bible; establishes the anthology conceit, the ~3518 d.M. present (canon/history.md), and the two reading-layers (player body-text vs. GM margins). [INVENTED]: the Curator's one-line judgements in the contents (framing colour only).

I · THE COSMOGONY

Of the Silence, the Sundering, and the Making of the World

As set down by Xochiyayotl, Xattva Loremaster of Xuthal, Star-Priest of the Cult of the Stars, eldest of the reckoners of Xebech.

The Curator

Of all who might tell the world's beginning I chose the coldest voice. The Xebechani are the eldest people still drawing breath, and Xochiyayotl is old even among them; it has watched the red star rise and set more times than Letia has crowned kings. It does not tell the tale as the warm peoples tell it at their hearths. It tells it as a thing it remembers. Where its account and theirs diverge, I have let both stand. — the Curator

Be still, and I will tell it as it was told to the first of us, in the drowned halls, before your grandmothers' grandmothers were warm mud.

In the beginning was not fire, and not dark, but the One. We name it Ech, which in the old tongue is less a name than a held breath — the whole of all-that-is, undivided, neither awake nor asleep. From Ech flowed the Spirits, numberless as motes in a shaft of light, and where the Spirits went there was the possibility of change; and change is the mother of magic, and of time, and of grief. Understand this first, warm reader, for your priests forget it: the Spirits are older than the gods. The gods are only the largest of the changes.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The priest calls it Ech and calls it the One. It is neither one nor a person. It is the Essence (l'Essenza, in the old tongue) — the raw substance that fills and underlies creation, impersonal as the sea. The Spirits are its foam. Every creed on Ymir has drawn a face on the sea and knelt to the face. That much of the cosmogony is true: there is a substrate, and it is real, and no god made it. Remember that. It is the one honest sentence in the chapter.

Then Ech was sundered. Why, none of the warm faiths agree, and we of Xebech do not think the question well-formed; ask instead how the sea learns to have a near shore and a far one. From the sundering came two, forever turned against each other and forever unable to be rid of each other: Mithra, whom we name Quetzalcoatl, the Feathered Serpent — the making-force, the light, the breath the warm peoples call Aether; and Ahriman, whom we name Azatzotech, the unmaking-force, the rot, the cold undertow they call the Nether. Do not imagine a good lord and a wicked one. Imagine your own two hands. Neither hand is evil. But set them against each other and you have made war, and war was the first true thing after the light.

From the friction of those two great contraries, six were struck off, as sparks are struck from flint — the Six, who are twinned to the elements and preside over them:

  • Diis, the Lady of Flames, of fire and of passion, who gives life and takes it in the same breath;
  • Hudemia, the Just Mother of the Waters, who holds law as one holds a level, and weighs;
  • Varshnak, the Beast, of ice and of the winter and of the cruelty that is in weather and means nothing by it;
  • Heis, the Mad God, of the air and of chance, who laughs and the laugh is a storm;
  • Therìos, the Hunter, of the earth and the forests and the beasts and the roads between them;
  • Dingir, the Twofold, of light and its shadow both, of logic, and — mark this — of Magic itself.

With the Six to labour beside it, the making-force shaped the world, and hung it about with the elemental deeps, and out of clay and light and cold water called forth the peoples to walk upon it. The unmaking-force could not bear it. Ahriman set its hand against the work — could not undo it, the sundering having made them equals and not masters — and so only spoiled it, here and there, the way frost spoils fruit; and then, in something the warm tongues have no better word for than sulking, it withdrew beyond the edge of creation, into the Outer Dark, and there, from Spirits it had soured past saving, it bred the first of the Demons. This is why your exorcists are right to say a demon is a Spirit gone wrong. They simply do not know how early the wrongness began.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Here the honest sentences end and the beautiful lie begins — and the priest does not know it is lying. Mithra and Ahriman and the Six are real enough; they are the great motions of the Essence, and they will answer a prayer. But they are not the eldest powers, and they did not make this world. Older than all of them slept the true gods — the ones this very priest and all its kind worship, and call the Outer Gods — until a people of tool-and-star, the Adepts, found them sleeping, sealed them beyond the elemental walls, drank their power, and set themselves up in the empty thrones wearing the old names. Much of what the warm faiths adore is the Adepts in a mask. The Ancient Gods are not dead. They are locked out. And in this present age the lock is failing — which is why, when the Tide broke, a red eye opened in the night sky and kept its watch there for two hundred years before withdrawing. But of that, the priest will speak only sideways, and only because its people never forgot whom they truly serve.

Now hear the part the warm peoples do not keep, for it is ours, and older. Before the sundering, before Ech learned to have two hands — there were already watchers. We do not say they made Ech; we say they were there, as the deep is there before the diver. We name them the Outer Gods, the Elder Ones who slept in the Dark past the last wall of the world: the Observer, who is the eye and the red star; the Judge, who is three things at once, Good and Evil and Nothing; the Silent; the First; the Mother; and the Risen. The warm faiths think our worship strange, and call our temples fearful. Let them. We are the eldest, and we remember that the gods you kneel to are young. When the Observer's eye is fully open, you will remember it too.

The Curator

The reader from Letia or Cartosa will find much here familiar and much of it upside-down. In the common telling — the one the Church of Mithra prints — it is Mithra who is Creator and eldest, and Ech only His first name; the Six are called the Divine Twins, said to be born of Mithra and Ahriman rather than struck from their strife; and the Outer Gods are not mentioned at all, or are named devils. I set Xochiyayotl's account first not because it is the majority faith — it is very far from it — but because it is the oldest, and because, on the matter of the Spirits and the Essence, even the theologians of Solanthya privately concede the priest is right. The gods of the common creed are treated in their own chapter, at length, and with the respect a living faith is owed. — the Curator

One thing more, and I have done. You will ask where the great wound in the sea came from — the Maelstrom, the turning storm at the heart of the world, out of which no ship returns and into which all the compasses lean. The warm peoples tell three tales of it and cannot choose, and I will not choose for them; their historians may quarrel over it in their own pages. I will say only what we of Xebech hold: it is not a natural thing, and it is not old. The world turned for ages before it opened. Something was done — a working past the strength of any who should have dared it — and the sea has not healed since, and the walls of the world were cracked by it, and through those cracks, slowly, patiently, the Elder Ones are waking. We do not fear this. We have waited for it. You perhaps should fear it a little.

That is the making of the world. Sleep now, warm reader. You are very new, and the night is long.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The "something done" was the Adepts' own ritual — the theft that sealed the Ancient Gods, drank their fire, and tore the Essence so badly that a second, curdled Essence bled out and became the demons; the backlash drowned their shining capital and left the Maelstrom over its grave. Priest, historian, and doom-preacher each saw one face of the single act and named it a different catastrophe. It was one catastrophe. The Xebechani "waiting" is not serenity — it is the patience of a jailer's servant who has always known the prisoner would get out. See secret-the-great-lie.md.

Colophon. Chapter I. Narrator: Xochiyayotl of Xebech (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md). Drawn from canon/cosmology.md and canon/pantheon.md (the public cosmogony: Ech → Mithra/Ahriman → the Six Twins → the shaping, the Void, the demons; the Spirits/Essence; the Maelstrom as an unnatural wound) and, in the margins, canon/secret-the-great-lie.md (the Adepts, the sealed Ancient/Outer Gods, Ech = the Essence, the one-event Catastrophe, the waking / the red moon). II · THE GODS & THE SACRED

Of the One and the Many; the Faiths of Ymir Set Side by Side; and the Orders of the Unseen

As set down by Fra' Teodabir, torch-priest of Hokhmah, of the Brotherhood of the Torch at Armora — called by his brothers, not always kindly, the Collector of Gods.

The Curator

After the cold of Xochiyayotl I wanted a warm room, and Fra' Teodabir keeps the warmest in Armora: a priest of Wisdom who has spent forty years praying, courteously and in rotation, at every altar that would admit him, forever fitting the creeds of Ymir into a single Concordance he has never finished and, I think, never will. Devout, learned, incapable of contempt — the rarest kind of theologian. Where his tidy tables disagree with the peoples who own the gods, I have said so. — the Curator

To the honest reader, greetings, and good light to read by.

I am asked to set down the gods of Ymir, all of them, in order — a task, my Abbess observed, best given to a man too cheerful to know it is impossible. I accept the charge and the insult together. Understand first the custom that makes my science possible: on Ymir we practise what the schoolmen call monolatry. No one doubts the many gods exist; but each soul worships one alone, honouring that god as the truest face of the Absolute which contains all the others. The fisherman does not deny Thurms; he prays to Orum, because Orum is the face the sea shows him. No cleric here is asked to work wonders to prove the gods real; nobody needs proving to. Ymir's quarrel has never been whether the gods are, but which face of the One a decent person ought to kneel to. I have spent my life suspecting the answer is: any of them, honestly knelt to. You will see how far the suspicion carries, and where it stumbles.

Begin, as the common creed begins, with the Two. In the telling printed by the Church of Mithra — and told, with small furniture moved about, from Letia to Cartosa — Mithra the Creator is eldest and first, Lord of the Sun and Light, and Ech, the One merely His oldest name, worn before time. His the golden sun; His the Aether, the bright breath of creation; His justice, peace, healing, and the patient work of making. Against Him — and, the creed insists, from Him, as shadow is from light — stands Ahriman the Corruptor: the black sun, Supreme God of Evil, bearer of the 999 Plagues, lord of the Nether, of war, disease, and unmaking. Neither can end the other. Solanthya's theologians have written libraries on why the Creator permits His adversary; the best answer I ever heard came from a midwife of the Seamark: you cannot have an inhale without an exhale.

The Curator

The reader who began at the beginning will notice that Xochiyayotl, in Chapter I, tells it the other way about: Ech first and impersonal, the Two struck from its sundering, Mithra no one's creator-eldest. The Church calls that a venerable error of a venerable people. The Xebechani do not call the Church's account anything at all, which is somehow worse. — the Curator

From the contrast of the Two were born the Six — the Divine Twins, the elemental gods who helped Mithra shape the world, each presiding over one element:

  • Diis, the Lady of Flames — fire and passion, giver of life and destruction in one breath. Sign: a heart wreathed in flame.
  • Hudemia, the Just Mother of the Waters — Law and justice; she weighs. Sign: a spear holding up the pans of a balance.
  • Varshnak, the Beast — ice, winter, the dark side of nature, the fury of the elements. Sign: an axe sprouting tree-roots from its haft.
  • Heis, the Mad God — wind, chance, fury, madness. Sign: a snarling, one-horned monstrous face. Sailors love him and will not say so.
  • Therìos, the Hunter — earth, forest and beast, protector of travellers and hunters. Sign: a wolf's head.
  • Dingir, the Twofold — light and shadow both, logic, and — mark it — Magic itself. Sign: a star.

Six elements, six temperaments, six ways the world can touch you. My Concordance was easy going, this far. It always is, this far.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Here is the seam the friar will spend the chapter almost finding. Mithra and Ahriman are real — the true polar principles of the Essence — and the Six are real elemental divinities, old and potent. But not everything that answers to their names is them. The Adepts, when they made themselves gods, co-opted names, masks, and whole cults of the true powers, and seated themselves inside the liturgy like cuckoos. Part of the worship catalogued here reaches real divinity; part feeds usurpers wearing its face — and no priest on Ymir can tell from inside which is which. See secret-the-great-lie.md.

Beneath the Two and the Six the schoolmen rank the Lesser Gods — and never say "lesser" inside their temples, for several are greater in cult than half the Six; the taxonomy measures cosmic seniority, not the length of the processions. As my tables hold them:

  • Ishtar, the Mother Goddess — Moon, Love, and also War. Wife of Mithra. Sign: the silver moon — the faithful moon, I must now say, since a redder one took to wandering our sky (of which, patience).
  • Kur, the Dark Lady — death and the underworld, giver of oracles and prophecy; wife of Ahriman, yet no enemy of the living. The dead need a housekeeper. Sign: a skull.
  • Thurms, Lord of Thunder — war and storms; he leads the just to victory and is understandably vague about the unjust. Sign: a thunderbolt.
  • Gog, the Serpent — God of the Damned, of Vengeance, Lies, and Corruption; ancient enemy of the good, and of Thurms especially. Yes, he is worshipped: read his domains again and tell me you have never wanted anything on the list. Sign: a serpent coiled upon itself.
  • Aisen, the Liberator — Freedom, and the salvation of the oppressed. Sign: a broken chain.
  • Tifereth — Love and Beauty. Sign: a red rose.
  • Hokhmah — Wisdom, and Knowledge turned to Good. Sign: a torch. She is mine, and I will not pretend to weigh her as I weigh the others. I was a boy who asked too many questions in a town that had run out of answers, and hers was the one house where "I do not know" was spoken like the beginning of something rather than the end. Forty years I have held my torch up to other peoples' gods, and she has never once scorched my hand for it. That is my whole theology. It has been enough.
  • Tuchulcha, the Darkener — Eternal Night, and Knowledge turned to Evil. My Lady's shadow: the torch and the raven are one hunger fed differently. Sign: a raven.
  • Orum — the Sea, protector of sailors and fishermen; chief god of Cartosa. Sign: a shell.
  • Ranin, the Merchant — transactions, artisans, commerce; the god most often paid and least often prayed to. Sign: a hammer.

Now to the Church of Mithra, and its two great tearings. The old rupture is political: nearly five centuries gone, the Grand Duke of Armora declared Solanthya's High Theocrat corrupt — accusing the Letian Church of secretly worshipping Ahriman behind the golden sun — and raised the Armorite Church of Mithra, still the only licensed clergy of my city, in open schism — and, for its first bloody generation, open war — with Solanthya. The guns have been quiet since the Peacemaker's treaty; the schism has not. On the truth of the accusation I say nothing, chiefly because I would like to keep saying things in general.

The younger quarrel is doctrinal and cuts across both obediences: the schism of the Inclusivists and the Populars, and its flashpoint is the neutral gods — Kur, Ranin, Heis, my own Hokhmah, and the rest who serve neither sun. May they be honoured within the faith of Mithra? The Inclusivist party says yes: all lawful worship is one worship, every god a window on the same Absolute. The Popular party says the windows are a wall with holes in it, and a church that admits every god will shortly be a market. I am, you will have guessed, an Inclusivist in all but membership. And what I notice, as a collector, is this: the deeper I file the faiths of this world, the more they fit. Names translate. Symbols rhyme. Domains interlock like a mason's course. It is as though the pantheon were not a crowd but a design — and my Concordance keeps almost closing, one god short of a circle. Almost. Hold that word; I will owe it to you again.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The Inclusivists are accidentally right, and righter than they would enjoy. The faiths of Ymir reconcile because they were reconciled — one cover-story, drafted by the Adepts, who standardised the masks and let the local names vary. "Every god a window on one Absolute" is very nearly true: the Absolute is the Essence, real and impersonal, and the windows were glazed by the same guild. The friar's Concordance keeps almost closing because one hand drew it; it will never quite close, because that hand left out everything it had buried. Where his tables jam, dig.

Across the western ocean the Xebechani keep a pantheon of their own, and here is the joy of my whole misspent vocation. Set the lists side by side, as I have on one enormous much-mended sheet, and watch: Quetzalcoatl, the Feathered Serpent, Life and Birth, the Great Benefactor — our Mithra, feather for ray. Azatzotech, the Great Putrefier — Ahriman, to the last plague. Xiuhtecuhtli, Lord of the Land of Ash — Diis. Tamoanchan, the Incorruptible — Hudemia. Ehecatl, the Inconstant — Heis, chameleon for horn. Mictlantecuhtli, Lord of the Underworld, of earth and beasts — Therìos. And my favourite correspondence in all the world's liturgies: Omotecuthli, Lord of Duality, Abundance and Wisdom, whose sign is a star — our Dingir, the Twofold, whose sign is a star. Two peoples an ocean and an age apart, and the same god signing both registers with the same mark. On days my faith wavers, I take out that sheet.

But honesty is my Lady's trade, so here is the column that does not map. Tezcatlipoca, Lord of the Night Sky, protector of warriors, the jaguar: I have tried him against Thurms and against Ishtar-in-arms, and my Xebechani correspondents reply, patiently, that he is himself. Yogh, the Spider-God, Poison and Assassination, the Creeping Death — and here my science rewards and unsettles me in one stroke, for the same Yogh, same spider, same whispered offices, is culted at Ulan-Tang on our own continent, among folk who never traded a missionary with Xebech. One god, two hemispheres, no bridge; my Concordance files him, and my hand hesitates over the drawer. And Xhulhu, He Who Sleeps and Dreams, god of the Abysses, Wrecker and Storm-Bringer, whose sign is a tentacle; and Heichpyel, the Observer, scribe of the gods, keeper of secrets that must not be revealed, whose sign is an owl — for these two I have no column at all. They are not translations of anything we pray to. They are, I think, translations of something else.

Which brings me, reluctantly, to the eldest cult, and the one I understand least. The Xebechani — openly, and certain quieter congregations less openly — worship the Outer Gods, said to sleep in the dark beyond the last walls of the world: the Observer; the Judge, a trichotomy of Good and Evil and Nothing; the Silent; the First; the Mother; and the Risen. Their own priests say that to worship one of them, once, wrongly, caused the Disaster. I have prayed in forty-one temples, honest reader; I entered this one, asked my questions, wrote down the answers, and I will not pretend the answers fit my tables. Every other god on Ymir, however strange, is a face of something I can love. These are not faces. The Xebechani say they are what was there before — and when I ask, gently, before what, they look at me the way I look at my novices. And there is this: for two centuries after the Tide a second moon wandered where no moon was — red, unscheduled, answering no calendar — and the star-priests name it the Gaze-upon-Nothing, and say it was the Observer, stirring; and that around the year 3260 it did not set, but withdrew, which they insist is a different thing. The Church called it vulgar superstition about an errant heavenly body. The Church also, I observe, has never restored the night processions it suspended while that moon stood overhead.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The friar's instincts are a compass needle: watch where they tremble. The Outer Gods are the Ancient Gods — the true, eldest divinities, whom the Adepts found sleeping, sealed beyond the elemental walls, and robbed to fill their own new thrones. They do not fit his Concordance because the Concordance is the cover-story, and they are what it covers. The red moon is exactly what the star-priests say: the Gaze-upon-Nothing is Tuulhak-Xanar, the Observer, the seal's first failure — it walked the sky from the Tide until it receded, two and a half centuries past: withdrawn, not set — and Xhulhu and Heichpyel, the two "gods" the friar could not file, are that one Observer's exoteric masks: the dreaming face and the recording face of a single opened eye. He is right about the spider, too — one Yogh, two continents, no missionaries.

The provinces of faith are no less rich. In Helgedad's Seamark, at the temple called the Shining Tower, they honour a triad of mothers: Hudemia, Diis, and a third lady, Thal'Khal, whom the locals hold to be theirs alone. I sat with her priestesses, heard the offices, and will state that Thal'Khal is our Kur — the skull beneath the sea-veil: death, the deep, and above all the oracles, which are the whole trade of her tower. The priestesses, told this, poured me more tea and agreed the way one agrees with weather. It remains the cleanest triumph of my method, and it changed nothing whatsoever in the Seamark. — Among the Dark Elves of the Gaerwath the great gods are Valrin, the warrior god who leads the holy war, and Myethel, goddess of magic and oracles; but the heart of their faith is a place, not a person: Niethel, the Source, the paradise where the elves were deathless until a grave sin — a revolt against the gods, their doctors say — cast them out as exiles. Theirs is a theology of banishment, and much that you fear in them follows from it. (Their bound servants, also called Valrin — the god's Forces, sworn to his war — I defer to the foot of this chapter, where such beings belong.) — In the deserts of the Sadhir the Sarradhi, Those Who Walk the Cycle, keep the creed of the Wheel of Life: the eternal return of souls, with priestess and sultana one office, for the Wheel does not distinguish the realm from the rite. — And in the far forests the Nkishi Bokkor serve the ancestor-spirits and the dark forest gods, the Orisha, fiercest among them the Mayombe — a faith without temples, which taught me more about reverence than several faiths with very good ones.

The Curator

The friar's identification of Thal'Khal with Kur is his own, and the Seamark does not thank him for it. The Dark Elves' exile-creed is given whole in Chapter XI, by one of their own loremasters; the Sadhir's Wheel turns at proper length in Chapter XII. — the Curator

Last, the orders of the unseen — for the sacred does not end at the gods, and a survey that omitted the servants would be a palace with no staff.

The Spirits are first and eldest: immaterial dwellers of the Ethereal Plane, and the wellspring of all magic — older, the Xebechani insist, than the gods themselves. Lingering too long in our material world corrupts them into Raging Spirits, furious and past reason: a wisdom that stayed too long at the fair.

The Elementals issue from the elemental planes in a long ladder of dignities — from the littlest wisplings through servitors, mounts, and guardians, past the giants and the true and higher elementals, to the Dragons, mightiest of the hybrid kind — and, above all, the six Elemental Lords, one throne to an element, semi-divine, honoured by the elemental cults just below the Six Twins. Where a Lord Elemental ends and a small god begins, my tables decline to say.

The Djinn are the artisan's answer to the Spirit's frailty: a Spirit given fixed form and bound to a place, an object, or a single mission — which is why every djinn-story turns on the terms of the binding. Their ranks, rising: Jann, Jinn, Shaitan, Ifrit, Marid.

The Angels are Spirits of the good god's service, made wholly of beneficent energy, in three Spheres: the Third, nearest us — Angels, Archangels, Principalities; the Second — Dominions, Powers, Virtues; the First, about the throne itself — Seraphim, Cherubim, Thrones. I have met men who saw one. They do not describe it; they describe what the room was like afterward.

And the Demons — Spirits wholly corrupted and enslaved to the god of Evil, whose distorted true forms can scarcely be hidden on our plane; the mightier the demon, the worse the disguise sits. They are ranked in six Circles, weakest to strongest; beyond the sixth, unclassifiable, stand the Sovereign Demons, Beyond the Circles — near-gods bent solely on our unmaking, of whom the chroniclers now count most, Ech be thanked, destroyed, and still whisper three names: Asmodeus, Xardas, Masak. And at the ladder's base, its strangest annex: the bound Forces of the Dark Elves, leashed and sworn under Valrin's name to Valrin's war. One ladder, honest reader, from the Sovereigns down through the six Circles to a demon on an elf's leash. Even Hell files neatly. I no longer know whether to be comforted.

So ends the survey, and I owe you the word I held. My Concordance almost closes. Forty years, and every year the circle wants one god of being whole — and every year a different god will not sit. I have decided this is not the failure of the work; it is the finding. Either the Absolute is a circle too wide for any table of mine — which my Lady's torch can live with — or somewhere in my tidy pages there is a seam where two things have been sewn together and called one cloth. On bright mornings I believe the first. On dark nights — the sort the red moon once owned — I take out the sheet, and look at the gods that do not map, and trim the torch, and keep asking. She has never once scorched my hand for it.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Give the old man his due: "a seam where two things have been sewn together and called one cloth" is the truest sentence a priest utters in this whole book, and he does not know it. The cloth is the pantheon — real gods and the Adepts' masks stitched into one liturgy. The seam runs exactly where his tables jam: through the unmapped gods, through the eldest cult, under the red moon. His Concordance will close on the day the Lie finishes coming apart, and he would not survive the closing. Pray, if you pray, that he never finishes it.

Colophon. Chapter II. Narrator: Fra' Teodabir of the Brotherhood of the Torch, Armora (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md). III · OF THE ESSENCE

a Treatise on Magic: its Substance, its Realms, its Corruption, and its Control

As set down by Sir Drusilde, Archmage and Directress of the College of the Seventh Circle of Armora, in the year 3027 d.M. — the second of the Age of the Tide — being the doctrinal preface to her Red Book of Magical Control.

The Curator

Five centuries separate us from this treatise, and no one has replaced it. Drusilde wrote it as the opening of her Red Book — the "Vademecum of Practical Anti-Magic" that Armora's Section for Magical Control still issues to every recruit — and it remains the standard work on the Essence. Read her with two cautions. First, she wrote a bare two years after the Tide broke: what is settled history to us was, to her, an emergency in progress. Second, she was trained by Xochiyayotl of Xuthal — the priest whose cosmogony opens this Compendium — and spent her life politely refusing to believe it. Where the teacher offered a mystery, the pupil reached for a scale and weights. Both, I have come to think, were holding one end of the same animal. — the Curator

The reader will forgive me a definition before a single wonder. Wonders mislead; definitions do not. This preface exists because the Grand Duke's commission asked my College for a book to let an ordinary soldier survive an extraordinary enemy, and a soldier cannot counter what his officers cannot define. Neither, I have found, can most wizards.

I. Of the Substance

Observation the first: magic is a substance before it is an art. The learned call it the Essence; the vulgar say mana, and I permit the word in my lecture-halls because it is short. The Essence fills and underlies creation as water fills a sponge — impersonal, inexhaustible on any scale we can measure, indifferent to the uses we make of it. The Spirits, those immaterial dwellers of the Ethereal, move through it and are in some manner of it; every working of magic, from a hedge-witch's charm to the drowned engines of the Adepts, is a displacement of this one substance.

Observation the second: the substance precipitates. Where the Maelstrom's Resonance beats against the world, the Essence settles out as a fine physical sediment called Arvo — powdered magic, as silt is powdered mountain. The Dark Elves prize it beyond gold. My College keeps a dram in a case lined with Orihalcum, and I have watched it for twenty years the way other women watch the sea.

My first teacher — Xochiyayotl of Xuthal, to whom I owe my method and one persistent headache — would tell you that the Essence is Ech, the One, a held breath older than the gods, and would say it in a manner that made the candles feel cold. I honour that being too much to flatter it: this is poetry. What I can attest is that the substance exists; that it can be detected (my College's Essence-meter does nothing else), stored, conducted, amplified, nullified, and — the burden of this whole book — denied to those who would misuse it. Whether it is also a god I leave to the priests, who at least are paid for it.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The teacher told her the truth and she filed it under poetry. The Essence IS what the old priest calls Ech — the substrate beneath the whole cosmogony of Chapter I — and Drusilde's instinct to meter it rather than kneel to it is not new. It is the oldest instinct on Ymir. The Adepts worked the Essence with machines, precisely as she dreams of doing, and it made them gods in fact if not in right. Her College is not founding a science. It is excavating one.

II. Of the Three Realms, and of the Arcane

Practitioners of every stripe we call Channelers; the word is deliberately unromantic. What separates them is not the substance, which is one, but the conduit through which they draw it. There are three base realms, and a fourth thing that is not a fourth realm.

Essence — the realm named for the substance itself, because it draws the Essence raw from the world: the arcane and elemental tradition, the fireball and the ward, the bread and butter of Cartosa's Blue Mantles and above all of their Elementalists of Water and Wind, who are genuinely excellent and will tell you so unprompted. Its strength is force — no other realm matches it for elemental violence. Its weaknesses are practical and beautiful: it wants the spoken word and the free gesture, and it despises armour. The Red Book's chapters on gags, manacles, and wet sand are the least glamorous pages I have ever written, and have saved more lives than any battle-mage in the Grand Duke's pay.

Mentalism — magic channelled through the mind, the mind serving as catalyst rather than source. This is the subtle realm: the Mentalist casts without gesture and without voice, and the only outward sign is a faint reddening of the eyes — which is why the Red Book teaches every checkpoint sergeant to look travellers in the face. A Mentalist fights the way a stiletto fights: illusion, assault upon the mind, the bending of passions, the quiet strengthening of the self, levitation and short translocations. He does not fight the way a regiment fights, and that is how you beat him — his arts favour himself and take one victim at a time, and they are poor cousins at elemental force. Watch the eyes; do not parley; come in numbers. A crossbow at eighty paces is entirely indifferent to a stare.

The Flow — the divine realm: power not learned but granted, by the gods, to their priests, on terms no mortal audits. I state as doctrine what my colleagues only mutter: this is the realm the regulator cannot reach. Cartosa registers and taxes every Essence-user in the Principality, but — as the Red Book has it — no one has yet dared ask the priests to register. The gods answer to no guild, and their servants answer to the gods. The Section's guidance on hostile clergy is accordingly the most careful writing in this book, and I summarise it in one line: you are not fighting the priest.

The Arcane — and here let the lesser colleges attend, for most print this wrongly — is not a fourth source. It is the union of the three base realms: the highest, rarest, most versatile discipline, in which one Channeler weds the raw draw of Essence, the silent conduit of Mentalism, and something uncomfortably like the granted grace of the Flow. I have met four true Arcanists in my life. Two frightened me. The Arcane Academy of Cartosa advertises a curriculum in it, which is charming.

III. Of Corruption, which is not a School

The gravest error in the popular understanding — and in three textbooks I could name — is to speak of the "dark arts" as a fourth realm with its own students and syllabus. Corruption is not a school. It is a taint, and it can afflict any realm as rot can afflict any timber. The necromancer channelling Dark Flows, the demon-binder drawing on Dark Essence, the Mentalist who has bargained his dreams away — these Corrupted Channelers did not matriculate somewhere; they were contaminated, by the substance scholars call the Corrupted Essence, whose wellspring is Ahriman's and whose handling marks the handler. My College grades that contamination on a ledger the profane have shortened to the CEO — Corruzione da Essenza Oscura, in the old tongue: Dark-Essence Corruption — and we grade it in anyone, of any realm, of any rank, because it respects none. It accrues the way soot accrues: from demon-wrought gear, from pacts, from sustained work with the dark derivations of the elements. In the civilised south its two ripest expressions, necromancy and demon-summoning, are capital matters — whispered of all the same in certain great houses, and practised openly and with terrible refinement by the Dark Elves of the Gaerwath.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

She is righter than her Church and righter than her Grand Duke. The Corrupted Essence is not Ahriman's malice; it is spoilage — the portion of the Essence that curdled when the Adepts' theft-ritual tore the flow of it at Ragash. The demons poured out of that curdling like flies out of a wound. Every point on Drusilde's tidy ledger measures the leakage of a single ancient crime, and the "capital matter" her state executes people for is trafficking in the by-product of the theft her own gods committed.

IV. Of the Elements and their Derivations

The Essence, drawn raw, expresses itself along six Elements — Fire, Water, Ice, Air, Earth, and Light — each presided over by one of the Six of the cosmogony and each crowned, below the gods, by a semi-divine Elemental Lord: Diis in the flame, Hudemia in the water, Varshnak in the ice, Heis in the wind, Therìos in the earth, and Dingir in the light and its shadow, for the sixth element carries its own negation within it. Elementalism — the disciplined working of one element — is the strong right arm of the Essence realm and the most easily licensed, because its effects are at least visible.

Beyond the six lie the derivations: hybrid and negative expressions that the six produce as surely as two notes produce a chord. The field officer must know four — Storm, the violent marriage of air and water; Nether, the cold undertow of the Essence itself, Ahriman's charge upon the substance; Spectral, where the Essence touches the Ethereal and the dead; and Umbra, which some colleges call simply the Dark, light's devouring negative. These are not seventh elements, whatever the Helgedad skalds sing of storm-wyrms; they are compounds and inversions, and they answer to the same counters as their parents, only less politely.

Separate from all of these stands the Void — the domain of Topramesk the Yellow, that most singular and least worshipped of powers. The Void is not an element and not the substance: it is the absence into which substance can be unmade. I insist on the distinction because the Academy of Cartosa still prints "mana" for both Topramesk's domain and the common stuff of magic, and I have written to them twice. Reserve mana for the substance. What Topramesk keeps is the hole.

V. Of the Maelstrom's Grip

All doctrine of magic on Ymir is doctrine under the Maelstrom, and the officer who forgets it will die surprised. The great vortex lays four hands on our art:

1. The Outflow. Free translocation — the teleport of the storybooks — decays with distance. Within a town it is merely dangerous; beyond a modest range the failures multiply upon each other until no sane Channeler attempts it, which is why no power on Ymir moves armies or cargo by spell, and why my harbour-masters still have employment. 2. The Obscuring. Divination is clouded. No scrying pierces the Maelstrom itself, and visions grow unreliable as they reach into the far past or future. Trust no seer who claims otherwise; bill any who insist. 3. The Arcane Storms. The Central Ocean is periodically wracked by storms of raw magical energy channelling forces from beyond the world. The Section's standing order in an Arcane Storm is a sailor's: sea-room, and none of your cleverness. 4. The Mark. The Maelstrom's energy sometimes touches the unborn, and the Marked are the result — twisted in body, gifted in ways that fit no realm cleanly. Most do not survive their villages. Those who reach my College are studied, paid, and — I record it as policy — protected.

To this grim list the present age has added the exception that has upended everything: the portals. Understand the distinction, for it is the hinge of our era. The ancient gates — the Adepts' own works, and the True Ways by which the Xebechani revived their network fifteen centuries ago — are fixed-gate constructions, and the Outflow does not touch them: the warp that ruins free teleportation was never their prison. What held them shut was a seal — the isolation the chroniclers call the bell — and two years ago that seal broke, and every gate on Ymir woke at once. Free translocation remains as ruined as ever; the fixed doors stand open. I have therefore recommended to the Grand Duke that warders be posted at every known gate, and I commend the same to whatever governments read this: a door that cannot be moved can be watched, and had the ancients watched theirs, this book might not have been necessary.

The Curator

It was necessary. Five hundred years on, the picture is exactly as she drew it: the Outflow holds, the seers still cannot pierce the vortex, and the gates of the True Ways stand open and garrisoned from Elendor to Qi-Long, carrying the trade of the New Equilibrium. Her "gate-warders," proposed as an emergency measure, are now so universal an institution that most readers will be startled to learn a single woman had to argue for them. — the Curator

VI. Of the Regulation of Magic

Magic in the civilised south is practised openly and mistrusted thoroughly, which is the correct arrangement. Its lawful economy is fourfold — protection (wards, golems, the bodyguard-mage), travel (the navigation-arts that keep Cartosa rich), healing (temple and lay, costly and rationed), and entertainment (licensed, lest the illusionists discover politics). Its governance takes two great forms:

  • The Cartosan model. Every user of Essence, Mentalism, or the Arcane in the Principality must register with the Hermetic Order of the Blue Mantles and pay the annual dues, or stand outlaw. Priests are exempt (see the Flow, above) and nobles are exempt (see human nature). A tax collected by wizards from wizards, and it works better than it deserves to.
  • The Young Kingdoms' model — the permit, Levels A through D. We license the act, not the person: A for lawful practice without guild membership; B for temporary leave to work arts normally forbidden, or to bring a summoned creature within city walls; C for the same at greater breadth and duration; D — near-total licence — which only a prince of the Young Kingdoms may grant, and which I have countersigned exactly three times.

Outside both models there hunts a blunter remedy: the Brotherhood, the mage-hunters Tholaer the Valiant raised under the sign of the Falcon, who register nothing, license nothing, and bill no one — I name them so that no recruit of mine mistakes their trade for ours.

And beneath both stands my own house: the Section for Magical Control, Armora's anti-magic corps, which takes ordinary soldiers — not one caster among them, by design — and trains them to recognise, disrupt, and survive each realm in turn. The S.C.M. is the reason the Red Book exists; my College supplies the theory, the Section the bruises. Lesser colleges call the enterprise unsporting. They are correct, and that is the point: sport is for duellists, and the Essence is not a duel. It is weather, and we are building roofs.

VII. Of Materials

The quartermaster's page. Commit these to memory; the examinations are not gentle.

  • Shald — violet volcanic glass; a living reservoir of Essence that refills itself and can be drained by a working mage. Its refined form, Actorios, is the summoner's stone.
  • Orihalcum — nullifies all magic, without appeal. An Orihalcum-lined cell is worth forty interrogators; the Section's manacles have it at the wrist.
  • Thalaras — the opposite number: amplifier and conductor of the Essence, priced accordingly.
  • Umbra — a light-devouring material from another world, come to us through the Night-Ones. It corrupts toward the Dark. We do not stock it. Do not bring me any.
  • Demon Steel — which taints its bearer toward Ahriman; and Dream Steel — mined beneath the Dream World, whose virtue waxes with the bearer's conviction. The one corrupts the faithful and the other rewards them.
  • Black Powder — the dwarves' secret, whose making no other race has reconstructed. Not, strictly, magical — and the most closely guarded substance on this list even so.
  • Alchemical Coal — char of Urakron wood, which drives an engine three times as long as the common sort. The unglamorous foundation of half of Armora's marvels, and of every tram my critics ride to their lectures against magitech.

VIII. Of Famous Instruments

I end with the instruments the age will be remembered by, because two years ago six of them remade the world.

The six Relics — Ignus, Saralis, Meletskar (which the easterners call the Masamune), Tassalung, Natharien the Celestial Diadem, and Oedring — are not, as the ballads already have it, six holy treasures. They are six seals upon four Magic Runes: four engines of the Adepts, housed from time immemorial in the Xebechani fortress-city of Xanadu, and keyed — I record the correspondence with a scholar's shiver — one to each realm of magic, the Arcane included. Four Runes; six locks. The Adepts did not build half-measures. When the Relics were gathered and the Runes unsealed at the dawn of this era, that single act was what shattered the Maelstrom's isolation — the unsealing and the breaking of the bell are one event, whatever the pamphleteers print. I have handled exactly one of the six: Meletskar, for the better part of an afternoon, with its bearer's leave and both my hands shaking. It is the finest made thing I have ever touched, and I include my own Essence-meters in the comparison, which costs me something to write.

Of the Door of Despair — with its Forgotten Scrolls and its terrible Key — I say only that it is a separate dread, no kin to the Runes, and that I decline to write more of it in a book meant for soldiers.

Of the named blades the reader will meet in the histories — Soul-Reaper (Mietianime), forged they say by the death-god himself and carried by Morannagul of the Gaerwath; the Masamune above; and Oblivion, the axe of Tofango — I note only the last as doctrine. Within Oblivion a mighty demon is imbued and trapped, and its will gnawed its wielder's mind until the greatest axeman of his age ended his days a mad hermit on a mountaintop. The case of Tofango is the whole of my third chapter in a single life: corruption is a property of the instrument and the handling, not of the man's realm or his virtue — which is why my Section registers artefacts as diligently as it registers wizards.

The Curator

The unsealing that was two years old when Drusilde wrote is five centuries old as I copy her, and the world it opened is the one we live in. Where the four Runes stand now, and in whose keeping, is matter for the historical chapters. Her "case of Tofango" is still taught, in her exact words, in every college from Armora to the Academy she teased. They forgave her. It took a while. — the Curator

Magic, then, is not a mystery. It is a substance; substances can be measured; and what can be measured can be governed. That conviction built my College, armed my Section, and wrote this book. Yet I close with the honesty I demand of my students. My old teacher called the Essence a held breath, and after forty years of weighing it I cannot prove the old priest wrong — only unhelpful. So let the doctrine stand, and one line of poetry beside it: if the Essence should prove after all to be a breath, then let us learn, before it exhales, who is breathing.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Nearer than she knows, at the last. Her beloved instruments — the meters, the gates, the Runes she venerates as the summit of "magic control" — are the toys of the usurpers: the four Runes are the Adepts' anti-demon weapons, built to scour the spoilage of their own theft, and the "isolation bell" she mourns as a broken protection was their seal — the lid the thieves screwed down over the wound, the demons, the gates, and the sleeping gods alike. What the heroes of the Tide performed at Xanadu was not a liberation or a catastrophe but a key turning, ages late, in a door the owners never meant to stay shut. Drusilde spent her life perfecting the discipline of watching doors. She never learned that the finest were built by the burglars.

Colophon. Chapter III.
