Decisions applied: HIS-1 (present ≈ 3518), HIS-2 (Leonius → Vespertina succession; present ruler a later VonGryffen), HIS-12 (the war cascade), MAG-7 (fixed gates exempt from the teleport-warp; free teleport still unreliable), META-2 (region-specific money: marks & scrip), GEO-2 (regional-tone context: the Young Kingdoms as Ymir's magitech register). [INVENTED] flags: the present Grand Duchess Serafina VonGryffen II "the Magnificent" (name and epithet invented to fill the HIS-2 present-day gap); Galenia's and Tydriell's one-line flavours (realm names canon, descriptions invented); the Crier's masthead, mottos, advertisements (VonHaller & Sons; Dextrius & Quill), letter-writers, the eight-legged cargo-walker, the annual Palace denial as a civic ritual, the "eighty-five million souls" census figure (invented order-of-magnitude), and the Crier's whisper-column treatment of the Conspiracy of the Five (the conspiracy is canon; the "never printed, never proved" framing is invented).

VIII · HELGEDAD, THE FROZEN HELL

Of the Six Marches, the Deep Halls, and the Wolf-Winter

As sung by Hrafnkel Ognirsson, skald of the Nortmaar, before the hearths of the six Marches; taken down from recitation at Nortcastle, the skald refusing to write.

The Curator

What follows was not written but caught — three clerks working in relays across two nights while Hrafnkel Ognirsson sang, drank, and sang on. Asked to set his own hand to the page he replied that a song nailed to parchment is a hawk nailed to a barn door, and would hear no more of it. The prose between the verses he supplied himself, grudgingly, "so the southern reader does not drown." He is a Vorskgen of the Nortmaar, of Seamark blood on his father's side, and he is exactly as reliable as pride is. — the Curator

Put down your wine, southlander, and pull your bench nearer the fire. You will want it. I am to sing you Helgedad, and a man cannot hear Helgedad warm.

Hail, hall-sitters — Hrafnkel sings you
the ice-anvil, isle of iron,
last land north where the whale-road ends,
where the wind whets winter for a weapon
and frost is father of all our folk.
Southrons named it the frozen hell.
We name it home, and hold it dearer.

Your mapmakers write the Frozen Hell beside our island and think it wit. The wit is older than they are, and it is ours: a hell should burn, and ours froze, and we stayed anyway. That is my whole people in one sentence, free of charge. Helgedad is the coldest land of Ymir, and the cold is not weather. Weather changes. Hard the land; the land's folk harder. What the ice takes, the ice keeps.

Six Marches the old Empire cut us into, and six we remain, each with its house, its city, and its own way of not dying. I sing in all six halls, winter by winter — a skald who cannot praise six rival hearths starves in the seventh — so trust a man who has eaten everyone's bread.

Kelmark, in the south-west, is House Rudstar's march, and its city Kelmar the greatest of ours — which a Tallvar man will dispute, from behind his counting-table. Kelmark breeds horse-knights sworn to Thurms, the Thunder-Lord who leads the just to victory: the Order of the White Mountain, white cloaks on winter horses, the only chivalry in the north worth the iron it rusts in. And Kelmark keeps the Wall. The Wall of Aslund runs four hundred thousand strides against the north, stone from sea to sea, raised in the Empire's day by Aslund the Swordless of the Tenth Legion. They say he broke his blade and buried it beneath the first stone — let the wall be my sword — and never bore steel after; the knights swear he walks the rampart still when the north-lights burn.

Aslund set stone upon stone in the storm-dark,
gave his sword to the first stone's grave.
Blade in the bedrock, oath in the mortar —
four hundred thousand strides of steel.
Wall against wolf-kind, wall against winter:
the Swordless keeps his watch unsleeping.

Tallvar, south-east, is House Talvain's: the first city the Empire ever raised on our shore, and the first thing it raised there was a customs-house. Tallvar is our gate to the southern continent — whatever comes up the whale-road in a fat hull pays in Tallvar and is lied about in Tallvar. Thurms is honoured there, but the crowds are at the shrines of Heis, the Mad God of wind and chance, for Tallvar men will dice on anything, including the honesty of their own guilds. Of those there are three — merchants, warriors, and thieves — and you may ask which is richest only if you already know the answer.

Nortislong, on the west coast, is House Geidmund's, and the eldest of us, for there live the Norsi, first folk of this island, whose fathers hunted the Winter Forest before any keel found us. Every Norsi line keeps its totem, a beast-spirit of the Forest that walks with the family, wakes in its dreams, and grieves at its graves; a Norsi will forgive an insult to his face sooner than a joke about his wolf, his elk, his white owl. Nortislong was our capital once, and taken once, in a single day — but that shame comes in its place, and not before.

Grudgemark, centre-west, is House Tannermar's, seated at Grudgeburg, and its saga is written in stone flat on the ground: the roads. Tannermar's folk drove roads through country that eats armies, and keep them clear in winters that close the sea itself. They are the dwarves' oldest friends among us, which tells you their quality, and they keep the Healing Houses open to any hurt thing that crawls in. Mock the leech-halls, southlander; you will crawl to one someday.

Nortmaar is mine, and it is no house's. Free land, hill land, land of the Vorskgen clans — the old blood, first-made men, ancient when the Empire was an ambitious village. We were a quarrelling of clans until some fifty winters before the Wolves came, when the Ulding — the high-chief line at Nortcastle — bound us under one shield-ring. Our temple is the barrow-field and our scripture is the blade: every clan keeps its sacred weapon, never reforged, never sold, never surrendered, in which the honour of the dead is housed. Our mad-brave, the bear-shirted, you have heard of; pray you never see them.

Ask a Vorskgen the names of his fathers:
he hands you the haft and bids you count.
Thirty names notched in the ash of the axe-shaft,
thirty winters of wound-sea weathered.
The blade is the book of us. Break the blade
and the line lies beheaded with it.

Seamark, last and to me first, is House Ognir's — my father's fathers — and its port is called Harbor, plainly, for we spend our poetry on ships and not on towns. Seamark men are the sailors of Helgedad, and above their mast-forest stands the Shining Tower, temple of the triple goddess: Hudemia the Just Mother of Waters, who weighs; Diis the Lady of Flames, who warms and takes; and third, the Veiled One, Thal'Khal, who receives the drowned. The wise whisper, quietly, in the tower's lee, that Thal'Khal is Kur, the Dark Lady of death and oracles, wearing a kinder face for us — and why not? A sailor would rather be gathered by a mother than judged by a queen of skulls. Our keels are three: the drakkar, the wave-wolf, for war; the deep-bellied knarr, for trade; the great oar-heavy dromen, for whatever needs a hundred backs. We have gone farther in them than your charts admit.

Now the dwarves, and here I will be fair, for they have earned it and few else have. Under the Aestyr Mountains shine the Golden Halls; under the Mountains of Mist stands Stonebridge, old beyond old, dwarf-work from before men wrote. Deep roads join hall to hall, and by them the Marches have been fed through siege and armed through war. Dwarves sell iron honestly and keep oaths better than kings. But mark this, for even the dwarves say it low: below their deepest mines run passages that no dwarf ever dug. The delvers find them, sound them, and brick them shut, and will not sing of them at any price. When a dwarf refuses payment for a story, the story is true.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The under-passages are the outer coils of a labyrinth, and the labyrinth is a prison. The Lord of Oblivion did not die and did not leave: he is sealed in the Aestyr's roots, in a maze of the Adepts' workmanship that holds its prisoner not with doors but with forgetting — walk it, and the way out unravels behind you in the mind. The Lord of Oblivion is locked in oblivion. Miners who enter the coils come back not knowing their own names — or knowing his.

Of the elves of Caelebawn I can tell you little, which is exactly what they intend. Sylvan folk, hidden in the Caelebawn Wood; you will not find them, though they will assuredly find you. Twice in our history their grey bows have come out of the wood unasked. Both times we needed them. Neither time did they stay for the feast.

North of the Wall lies the Frozen Waste, and there my wit fails me. There are ruins in that ice, southlander — towers in no style any mason can name, streets of black stone under blue glass. Evil things walk there and worse things wait, and in the Empire's twilight a sorcerer men called the Signore dell'Oblio dwelt in the Aestyr and reached his hand northward. His name means Lord of Oblivion, and it is the only thing about him not forgotten. We do not go north of the Wall. Write that plainly.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The ruins in the ice were a watch-city, and what it watched is still there. Under the Frozen Waste sleeps Nihoggr, the Ice Dragon — one of the twenty True Dragons the Adepts forged as living weapons — coiled about the island's root since the Catastrophe. Helgedad is not cold by chance: the Frozen Hell is literal — the cold is the dragon's breath in sleep. The Lord of Oblivion's crime, the one the labyrinth answers, was this: he sought to wake it.

Now the wars, in their order, the shames with the glories. In the year the southrons count 1555, at the Empire's greediest girth, Letia annexed us. Hear it straight: before the eagle-standards came we were a kingdom already, a kingdom of two crowns, stone and flesh — the dwarf-throne in the Golden Halls, the man-throne at Nortislong — and we were bought, bullied, and blessed into a province. A thousand years of tithes and governors followed, and the Wall got built; I will not say we gained nothing, only that we were not asked. In 2600 we walked out. Call it secession; there is no battle in it worth a stanza. The Empire had grown too poor to keep us, and we too proud to be kept, and the parting was as loveless as the wedding.

The Curator

The skald is precise where it stings: Helgedad before 1555 was indeed a dwarf-and-human kingdom (seats: the Golden Halls and Nortislong), and its road from annexation through the 2600 secession to the Wolves is one continuous arc of decline. The Annalist of Solanthya (Ch. VI) sings the annexation as a gift of civilisation to grateful barbarians. The two accounts agree on the date. — the Curator

Then the Winter of Unrest, in 3015, and the coming of the Wolves. An army out of nowhere and nothing — no herald, no demand, no homeland behind it — and at its head five generals whose faces no living captive ever described. In the Siege of One Day — one day, southlander; write one day — they took Nortislong, our eldest city and capital. And then, at the hour of their triumph, the five vanished — no bodies, no graves, no ransom — and the host broke like ice off a roof, and we threw the leavings back.

Then came the wolf-winter, worst of winters:
not wolves of the wood but wolves of war,
a host without herald, hunger-bannered,
five at its head with frost in their footprints.
One day they dined in Geidmund's high-seat —
gone by the grey dawn, graveless, nameless.

Berthrant Vaelaer would not let the leavings live. From 3015 to 3020 he hunted the Wolves' remnants across every march, sworn to scour Evil from Helgedad root and stone, and his muster is the great lay of my grandsires' grandsires: thirteen thousand and five hundred answered him — rangers of the Winter Forest, dwarves in ring-mail out of both deep halls, the White-Mountain knights of Kelmark, archers out of Caelebawn who had spoken to no man in living memory, and older friends still: the walking woods that southrons call Ents, and the Great Eagles off the high Aestyr. Against them: orc-bands and trolls of the waste, frost-giants down from the Frozen Waste, and the dead who would not lie down.

Thirteen thousand and half a thousand:
rime-wood rangers, ring-mailed delvers,
white cloaks whetted on winter horses,
Caelebawn's bowmen, unseen, unnumbered,
the walking woods and the wide-winged eagles.
Never since has the north so mustered —
never again may it need to. So we pray.

He broke them, and we breathed — one winter. In 3021 the Wolves came again in full strength, and no single day decided it: three years the War of the Wolves ground on, near half the isle lay under their terror, and what was done in the taken marches is sung only at funerals. Then, as before — as if a hand had picked its pieces off the board — the generals were gone, the packs tore themselves apart, and by 3024 we stood in the wreckage, victors of a war whose enemy we never once understood.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Understand it here. The five generals served the Lord of Oblivion, whose will still seeps out of the labyrinth in dreams. The Wolves' wars were never conquest — they were excavations: Nortislong, the Wall, and the deep roads all stand over ways down to the maze's heart or the dragon's grave, and the terror was a spade. The generals "vanished" because the labyrinth reclaims what its prisoner sends out — his reach ends where his forgetting begins — and five half-forgotten shapes now wander the coils, still obedient, still searching. The prison holds. So far. See secret-the-great-lie.md.

The Curator

The skald folds 3015 and 3021–24 into one long "wolf-winter"; the chronicles keep them distinct, and set them in a cascade — Meridian's second War of the Waves (3018–3023) burning alongside, the Young Kingdoms' war of machines catching from 3025: overlapping fires, not one blaze. On the dark generals, though, the driest annalist and the proudest skald record the same three words: no one knows. — the Curator

And then the Tide, which you southrons date your whole age by, and which we remember as the winter the dead walked out of the Frozen Waste under the red moon, and the harbours burned, and Seamark buried more sailors than the sea ever took. Since those years — near five hundred now — the tale of Helgedad is the long dwindling: fewer keels in Harbor, fewer hearths in Kelmar, marches thinner, barrows fuller. The Empire's censors would weep at our numbers; counting was always their genius, not ours. We are harder than we were, emptier than we were, and prouder than we have ever been — and if you ask how pride feeds a people, you have never wintered north of the Wall.

Emptier now are the ale-benches,
prouder for that the few that fill them.
The Wall still stands. The Tower still shines.
The ice does not ask if we are weary.
Hard the land; the land's folk harder.
What the ice takes, the ice keeps —
and the ice has not taken Helgedad.

That is the song, southlander. Pay the singer.

Colophon. Chapter VIII. Narrator: Hrafnkel Ognirsson of the Nortmaar (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md), taken down from recitation. Drawn from canon/geography.md (the isle, the six Marches, the Wall of Aslund, the dwarf-holds and non-dwarf passages, Caelebawn, the Frozen Waste, the ship types), canon/factions.md (the Marcher houses, the Order of the White Mountain, Tallvar's guilds, the Army of the Wolves), canon/history.md (annexation 1555; secession 2600; Winter of Unrest and Siege of One Day 3015; Berthrant Vaelaer 3015–3020; War of the Wolves 3021–3024; the Tide), canon/pantheon.md and canon/peoples.md (Thurms, Heis, the Shining Tower triad; Norsi, Vorskgen); margins built on canon/secret-the-great-lie.md (the Adepts' substrate; the True Dragons as engineered weapons). Decisions applied: HIS-11 (pre-annexation dwarf-and-human kingdom; one continuous decline arc), HIS-12 (the Wolves/Waves/Machines cascade), COS-7 (Thal'Khal = the Seamark mask of Kur). [INVENTED] and flagged: the Aslund buried-sword legend; the Vorskgen name-notching rite; the "fifty winters before the Wolves" dating of the Ulding's unification; the five generals' number as fixed lore; the post-Tide sack of the harbours and the ~3518 "harder, emptier, prouder" portrait; and the GM margins entire — the labyrinth-prison, the under-passages as its coils, the generals' allegiance, and Nihoggr the Ice Dragon (proposed as one of the twenty True Dragons) — offered as new GM canon.

IX · THE QI-LONG, WHERE GODS WEAR SCALES

Being the walking-book of a monk of the Path of the Enlightened: six kingdoms, eleven stages, and no kneeling

As set down by the Pilgrim of the Thundering Gate, once an initiate of the School of the Thundering Gate, now a mendicant of the Path of the Enlightened, who walked the six kingdoms and paid rent in none.

The Curator

This text reached Elendor sewn into a tea-merchant's coat, copied from a walking-book the pilgrim left behind, as his order leaves everything. The Path of the Enlightened is a mendicant way of the East that bows to no Dragon-God; its monks write of themselves in the third person, holding the self to be luggage. Of the pilgrim I know only what his sandals tell: he has stood in all six kingdoms, and returned, which fewer manage. Read him slowly. He wrote slowly. — the Curator

The First Stage — the Wound in the Mountains

A border is a wound that two lands agree to keep open.

The pilgrim came up out of the Sadhir with salt in his teeth, through high passes where winged shapes ride the dusk and the careless are lifted screaming into it. The bridge at the top of the pass asked him nothing. Bridges never do; that is why they outlast kingdoms.

Beyond lies the Qi-Long: six kingdoms, forever at war for a supremacy none has won. Each is ruled with an iron fist by a Dragon-God — a god who walks in the shape of a man or a woman and puts that shape off like a coat; matchless in war, in sorcery, in every art of fist and blade. Each names itself strongest of the six. For sixteen hundred years the other five have disagreed. The war does not tire.

The Second Stage — What the Old Men Remember

Ask a stone about the flood. It was there, and says nothing; that is how you know it was there.

In every kingdom the old men tell it the same way, door shut. In the deep past one True Dragon-God reigned alone from the Capital of Light, standing yet in the north. Then he was gone; no tale agrees where. Men filled the silence, built the empire of Mah-Dhol, and for six centuries the Qi-Long was one. When Mah-Dhol cracked and fell, the six came. Mark the word: the old men do not say the six were born. They say the six woke. What wakes has slept; what has slept has dreamed; what has dreamed, remembers. No man can say what the six remember. Many have died of it.

Some whisper the six are not even deathless — that when one falls, a successor takes the name, and the name does not grieve.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The old men are exact. The six woke — roughly 1900–1920 d.M., as Mah-Dhol came down — and were not born: they were made long before, six of the twenty True Dragons, living superweapons the Adepts engineered to stand warden over their makers' buried secrets. The six that hold the Qi-Long kept their post. So the "resistance to the dragons" is mortals — themselves the Adepts' crafted children — at war with their makers' watchdogs, neither side knowing whose yard they fight in. See secret-the-great-lie.md.

The Third Stage — the Spires of Weeping, Where the Lie Lives Underground

The mountain does not lie. The lie lives under the mountain.

First of the kingdoms: the Spires of Weeping, a desert of stone teeth. Its god is Qiángdà Shéshān, the Mighty Serpent of the Mountain — earth is his element, and his nature the earth's: dormant, unfathomable, patient past all human patience. His true realm is not the spires but the dark beneath them, cities under stone, whose people are taught from the cradle that the surface kills. Goblin muleteers led the pilgrim by ways they would not name — goblins are common folk in all six kingdoms, and lied to like everyone else. Above, the Tengu keep the god's order: crow-headed, cruel, fond of heights and of dropping things from them.

Against him stand the Black Scorpions, whose mark is the scorpion's tail: assassins who hide even from those they die for, since the buried people, raised on the god's lie, spit at their name. Their couriers drink a poison from their master's hand, and must return for the next cup or die with their secrets. Above them sits the Black Sting, whom no one has seen grow old. The pilgrim asked a Scorpion why they protect a people who curse them. "Does the rain ask?"

The Fourth Stage — the Province of Ash, Where the Ground Smokes

Ash remembers the forest. That is why it burns the feet.

South lies the Province of Ash: sulfur flats, open mines, canyons, mountains that have not finished burning. Its god is Nanfang Huolong, dragon of fire, despotic and wrathful; his priests count their sermons in brandings. His servants are the Oni — orc-blooded demon-things steeped in elemental fire, driving half-orc and human miners alike; the whip does not check ancestry. The people of the ash are blunt as hammers and as hard to offend twice.

Their shield is the Red Hounds, whose mark is a snarling jaw. They run in packs, hunt Oni as Oni hunt men, and hold that a rebellion is kept alive the way a fire is: by feeding it. Their head is the Scarlet Mastiff, a title that has outlived every neck it sat on. Of their fellow societies the Hounds trust only the Tigers — "the only ones who actually fight."

The Fifth Stage — the Sea of Shipwreck, Where the Landlord Is the Sea

The sea owns every ship. Sailors are tenants; drowning is the eviction.

West is a kingdom with almost no land: the Sea of Shipwreck, a scatter of islands in a water that answers to one will. Its god is Tatsumoto Ryūdō, dragon of water, haughty and megalomaniac; he does not rule the islands so much as permit them. His tax-men are the Kappa, armored amphibians who climb the tide to collect. The islanders paid the pilgrim's passage in strung cash-coins and settled quarrels in weighed jade; paper promises do not survive salt.

Their defenders are the Celestial Tigers, whose mark is a tiger's claw: ambush-fighters who harvest the great turtle-beasts and the Kappa themselves for armor. Under the Great Tiger they fight as the sea fights — suddenly, then not at all. A Tiger told the pilgrim: "We cannot beat the sea. We can beat what crawls out of it."

The Sixth Stage — the Dead Forest, Where the Innkeepers Are Dead

Accept no roof at night from a house you did not see at noon.

The Dead Forest is a poisoned swamp the size of a nation, and its god is dead and reigns anyway: Hēi-yīng Fēngxié, the Black Eagle of the Malign Wind, the undead dragon of the air — mellifluous, Machiavellian, the one god of the six who prefers the soft word to the fist, because his words rot. His court is the Yurei: ghosts of the great warriors who challenged him, now his property. By night the drowned forest grows temples and lit inns that were not there by day; those who enter are eaten by the hospitality. The living are few, stilted above the water, kind in the way of people who have buried many guests.

Their guardians are the Grey Herons, whose mark is a heron's wing — the loosest of the six societies, wanderers and jokers who gather but once in five years and fight only as they must, the civilians' lives ranked above the victory. Their art is the Style of the Divine Winds. The other societies find them unserious; the Herons count that the graver wound.

The Seventh Stage — the Frozen Plain, Where Faith Is the Only Fire

Faith is the one coin that grows heavier the more it is spent.

North of everything human lies the Frozen Plain, a polar desert under mountains of ice. Its goddess is Mò-hán Lóng, Dragon of the Frozen Desert — savage and sadistic. Her servants are Rakshasa, beast-bloods crossed with devils, who hunt the herders for tribute and for sport. The people endure, which on the ice is a politics.

Their society is the strangest of the six: the White Mongooses, whose mark is a white lotus. Alone of the societies they fight for a god — not the ice-queen, but an absent one. At a shrine of cut ice their master told the pilgrim what he tells every despairing brother: "We serve a Dragon-God who is not of this world — not yet. Awaiting his return, we must guard his realm with the greatest weapon he left us: our faith." The other societies mock this as superstition; the Golden Falcons whisper darker, that the Mongooses are some enemy's unwitting tools. The pilgrim notes only that of six creeds, theirs alone requires the dawn.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The six wardens have an elder — apex of all the True Dragons, above loyal and rogue alike: Ouroboros, the True Dragon God, who has woken once already, in the Age of the Tide, and Alfheim burned for it. If the "god not yet of this world" is the lost True Dragon-God, the Mongooses keep a vigil. If it is Ouroboros — or if those were ever two names for one appetite — they are kneeling on a fuse. The Falcons' suspicion is the sanest sentence in the Qi-Long.

The Eighth Stage — the Capital of Light, and What Is Under It

In a city of light, count the shadows. The count will come out wrong. Count again.

Last, the Kingdom of the North, scarcely a kingdom at all: one city so vast the pilgrim walked nine days inside it — Chéngshì, the Capital, the Capital of Light the True Dragon-God founded and ruled. Its mistress now is Léilong Lìnbei, dragon of light and of light's shadow, thunder in her voice — paranoid, divided against herself. Her courtiers are the Kitsune, fox-people of many faces, so that no one is certain who anyone is, including the Kitsune. Its people are the most refined in the six kingdoms — half-elf poets, scholar-clerks, silk-brokers, and the little cat-folk Tal-Tanoth (there are no halfling folk east of the Sadhir; the Tal-Tanoth fill their place, and your pockets) — and every one a spy, if only on their neighbors.

Fittingly, the local society is spycraft perfected: the Golden Falcons, whose mark is a raptor's talon — cells that never meet, messages carried as verses by the Master-Singers, spymasters who never fight, and at the summit a Red Phoenix so secret some Falcons doubt it exists. And beneath it all, the old men say, lies a second city, entire and inhabited, ruled by the twin of the goddess above; which sister holds the throne of light and which the shadow, no tale dares settle. The pilgrim, who counts shadows, believes the old men.

The Curator

Two name-sets circulate for these gods: older itineraries write Huolongnan, Seishima Tatsumoto, Weichong-Shanxuan, Kuangshuang Long, Jiu-Yaofeng, Qiulei-Senbei for the pilgrim's six, and call the Spires of Weeping the Plateau of the Clouds. The beings are the same; the tongues render them differently — unless, as some hold, the successors differ. Western map-houses meanwhile still print "Tianqi" across the whole Qi-Long: an empire sixteen centuries dead. Buy newer maps. — the Curator

The Ninth Stage — the Gathering of Heroes

A tournament is a war that has learned table manners.

Every seventh year comes the Gathering of Heroes. Under Mah-Dhol it was held once, at the capital, before one throne; now six times, before six, each Dragon-God crowning champions into the Great Sects — the orders that governed provinces for an empire and now serve each the god of its own kingdom: the Way of the Undying Flame, the Temple of the Frozen Peak, the School of the Golden Pavilion, and their peers, with countless lesser schools beneath — the pilgrim's own School of the Thundering Gate among the least. The resistance societies — men call the six together the Triad; do not ask why six are called three, the name is older than the counting — seed their finest into the lists, for a champion's robe passes doors rags cannot. So each god hosts, every seventh year, a festival of its own future assassins, and cannot stop: to cancel the games is to admit fear before the other five. The pilgrim watched the finals and applauded with everyone else. Manners.

The Tenth Stage — the Spiral, Which Is Not on Any Map

Climb a spiral long enough and you will meet your own back.

Of his own order's way the pilgrim will say what novices are told. That Fate is the road and not the walker: it does not compel your feet, it only outlasts your refusals. That the Essence is water and every god a jar, and the jars quarrel over which of them is wet. That there are Three Realms, and with them the most Ancient, who shine with a Wisdom not their own. And that magic is the seventh — older than the six, older than their madness, glowing with its own enchantment, borrowed by all and owned by none.

There is a walk the perfected make, the Path of the Enlightened itself, and the pilgrim has made it: through the Darkness of Nightmare and the Doubt of Illusion, through the last Veil; past the faceless judge with the broken scale, whose unjust judgment you must accept to pass. Then the spiral: six sanctuaries, one to each elemental divinity, each bearing a confession. Life itself we enslaved, and it bowed at our feet, says the obsidian of Therìos. Drawing on the Astral fire, in our Forge we recreated the Essence, says the ruby of Diis. Only when every altar is turned toward a mirror that is not there does the seventh sanctuary open, whose confession is entire: we created empty simulacra, pretending they were alive; cracking the walls of the World, we were possessed by the elements; in our immense Arrogance we defiled the perfection of the Essence. Beyond waits the Great Library of forbidden truth, whose guardian says: better to live in the darkness of ignorance than perish in the flame of a terrible knowledge. The pilgrim read one page. He walks more slowly since.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Set the spiral's six divinities beside the six Dragon-Gods: the match is exact, element for element — Diis to the fire-throne of the Ash Province, Hudemia to the drowned kingdom, Therìos to the serpent under the Spires, Varshnak to the ice-queen, Heis to the dead wind of the Forest, Dingir — light and shadow both, mark the twin — to the Capital. The "confession" is no parable: it is the Adepts' indictment in their own plural voice. The monks of the Path recite the truth of the Great Lie nightly and have footnoted it into harmlessness. The pilgrim read one page. Watch what he does next.

The Last Stage — Leaving by the Same Wound

Between six fires, do not warm yourself, and do not curse the burning. Walk.

The pilgrim went out as he came in. Behind him the stalemate ground on as it has for sixteen hundred years: six gods, each certain, none sufficient; six societies dying by inches so that others may die more slowly; nine hundred millions planting rice between the battle-lines, because rice does not care who is divine. The Qi-Long is not a war. A war ends. The Qi-Long is a weather. The pilgrim came to the bridge; the bridge asked nothing; and that, of everything east of the mountains, was the only mercy that asked no tribute in return.

The Curator

One matter I record without judgment. A colleague versed in eastern ciphers observes that the pilgrim's maxims scan, every one, in the courier-meter of the Master-Singers — the verse-code of the Golden Falcons. A monk owing allegiance to none, walking all six kingdoms, remembering everything. I have counted the shadows in this chapter. The count comes out wrong. — the Curator

Colophon. Chapter IX. Narrator: the Pilgrim of the Thundering Gate (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md). Drawn from canon/geography.md (six-kingdom present — decision GEO-2 = A; the True Dragon-God / the Capital of Light; Tianqi as historical label), canon/factions.md (the Triad; the Great Imperial Sects, the School of the Thundering Gate, the Gathering of Heroes), canon/bestiary.md (the Dragon-Gods woke ~1900–1920 d.M., not born — MAG-14; Ouroboros above the loyal/rogue split — MAG-12), canon/history.md (Mah-Dhol), canon/peoples.md / economy.md (Tal-Tanoth in the Halfling niche; cash-coins, jade), and above all sources/extracted/batch2-digests/east-qilong.md (the Dragon-Gods, realms, servitors, natures; the societies; the Mongoose creed; the twin city; the Path's cosmology and confessions). IX·B · A LETTER TO A DEAD PUPIL

being Xebech, the Land, in the only account of it this book will ever hold

Set down by Xochiyayotl, Xattva Loremaster of Xuthal, Star-Priest of the Cult of the Stars, eldest of the reckoners of Xebech; addressed to Sir Drusilde of Armora, of the College of the Seventh Circle, five centuries dead; deposited unasked at the Crystal Seat.

The Curator

I must account for this chapter, because I cannot. Twice I wrote to Xuthal asking for the land's own leaf — the one great blank in this book — and twice my letter came back unopened, its seal not broken but repaired, which I am told is Xebechani for no. Then, this spring, a Xidhe courier laid this packet on the steps of the Crystal Seat, waited for no receipt, and was gone; and its address no courier alive can serve: Sir Drusilde has been dead five hundred years. To print a private letter is theft; to print a letter that cannot be delivered may be the only delivery left. And it is the only account of Xebech that exists, or ever will: the eldest people has refused every census and every college, and then answered, once, a dead woman's questions. A being that old does not misdirect its mail. The address is exact; the rest of us may stand behind her and read. — the Curator

To Sir Drusilde of Armora, of the Seventh Circle, wherever the warm dead are kept.

You will forgive me a delay before a single answer. Answers mislead; delays do not. You have been dead for most of five hundred years, and the living tell me this makes my letter improper. A letter arrives when it arrives; the only impropriety is inaccuracy, and I have waited exactly as long as the answers required.

The compilers at the Crystal Seat asked me for an account of Xebech. I do not make accounts for the living; the living interrupt. You never interrupted. You did something far worse, which I have missed for five centuries: you waited, visibly, with instruments.

You asked me once what Xuthal stands on. You were the only one who asked on, and not how. I told you the question was not well-formed. That was untrue, and I have kept the untruth the way your College keeps its dram of Arvo — in a lined case, taken out on anniversaries. The answer was ours, and you were not ours. Now you are dead, which is a citizenship of its own, and older than mine. Three times you asked; three times I refused; and a teacher who outlives a pupil inherits the pupil's questions with the estate. I intend to settle.

The first question

Xuthal stands on permission.

The how I give you in one sentence, for your ghost's method: the city rests where a working of the Ancients holds it, as a note is held by a singer who has not yet chosen to breathe in. The on is this: it stands on a word spoken so long ago that no one remembers the shape of the mouth. We do not keep the city up. We keep the silence in which the word can go on being heard. That is the whole of Xebechani engineering, and most of Xebechani religion, and you may now stop underlining.

You would have wanted the streets, so here are the streets. Xuthal has no stairs; it is ramps to the crown, wide spirals cut to the shoulder of a Xudra. Our doorposts are written in warmth — inscriptions laid in veins of slow heat, so that at dusk the streets cool into legibility and the city reads itself. There are no locks: a people who see heat cannot be crept up on, and what cannot be crept up on never invents the lock. On the undersides, where the shadow falls on cloud, hang the rain-gardens that feed us, tended head-down.

And there you would have met the Xudra, whom the world has never met. The world believes my people are the Xidhe — the silk-voiced ones we post abroad the way you post sentries; it has met our ambassadors and believes it has met us. More than half of all Xebechani drawing breath are Xudra, and no Xudra leaves Xebech: huge, heavily scaled, brief-lived, and — record this — the only caste that laughs. They keep the ramps, the gardens, and the shells of the Pools, and they sing once a year, at the Rebirth: a counting-chant with one number in it. The number changes. I will come to that.

Of doors, which are our statecraft

There is no state here as your registries mean it: no throne, no assembly, no tax but duty. Our statecraft is doors.

Eleven centuries before your birth, my predecessors walked the dead network of the Ancients and taught stretches of it to carry again — the True Ways, your books call them, and for once your books are exact. We revived those roads so the peoples of Ymir could be gathered quickly if the demons came again; it was Xebechani feet on them that summoned the First Council of Peoples. Your Church's chronicles write "the peoples were summoned," in the passive voice, and we have never corrected them — correction is a tax the corrected must pay. And when your red book argued that a door which cannot be moved can be watched — that was a Xebechani argument, my pupil. We had made it for two thousand years to anyone who would hold still. You were the first to make it in a language princes fund.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

"Statecraft," it says. Hear it plainly: the Xebechani are the portal-caste — grown in the Pools by the empire whose network the True Ways merely re-lit, gate-keepers by design as a pointer is a pointer by design. They did not adopt a policy of doors; they are the policy. That is why the dead network answered their hands and no one else's, and why the priest, in two millennia of gate-craft, never once names the doors' maker. It knows the name. It is keeping the house.

The second question

Your second question you asked with both hands flat on my table, which among your people is rudeness and among mine is prayer: what is behind the doors of Xanadu?

Walk it with me; you have the time. Xuthal flies; Xanadu refuses to. Three days up from the river-cities, through jungle that thins politely, and then the fortress — not built, you would say, but decided: grey-green stone with the grain of an argument, and no gate where a siege would want one. Inside, the corridors are all of one length; measure any and you have measured them all. At the centre is the room the rolls call the Quiet Room, and in it the four Runes of Power, the great engines of the Ancients, keyed — you recorded the correspondence with a scholar's shiver, and the shiver was the correct instrument — one to each realm of your art. Around them, six sockets, empty, where the six Relics sat as seals for three and a half thousand years. We keep the sockets dusted. It is not hope. It is bookkeeping.

You saw that age begin, so I will tell you the part no chronicle has thought to ask after. Two warm strangers came to Xanadu in the year the world turned, carrying the gathered six. The chronicles keep them nameless, and a chronicle's agreement should be kept by somebody. But ask your own question, meterer: how did two warm bodies cross a fortress garrisoned by a people who see heat, to the one room that matters? They did not. The doors were opened from within. We judged the hour dark enough, and we let the key turn. Whether we judged rightly, the age you did not live to see is still deciding.

Your Curator once promised his readers — I have read every leaf of his book; we read everything, eventually — that where the four Runes now stand, and in whose keeping, was matter for the historical chapters. They never paid. I will. They stand where they have always stood. Unsealed is not the same as spent. In whose keeping? In ours — as a lamp keeps a flame. One change only: Xanadu was the coldest house in Xebech, the one building our children could not read. Since the Tide it is warm. Every eye in the river-cities can see it glowing, three days away, like a word beginning.

The Curator

Chapter III's promise concerning the Runes stands discharged — five hundred years late, and not by me. For Xuthal as the sea meets it, see the navigator's diary in Chapter V. — the Curator

The page you turned

Now a question you asked only once. When I did not answer, you turned the page. Here is the page.

In the year 2020 of your reckoning the dead came up out of our north — not our dead only, but the dead of every field and wreck of that coast, and behind them a will we never named. Attend, you who filed the gods under poetry: we know the six names of the powers beyond the sky, and we never learned the name of the thing that took our north. It showed no face and sent no terms, and what wants nothing cannot be treated with. We fought it for seventy-five years — the time it takes a patient people to accept a fact — and in 2095 we drew the border ourselves and stepped behind it.

Understand the cost, for the chronicles say our numbers were nearly halved and let the sentence rest as if it were about soldiers. Our dead are not buried, pupil. They are continued: the body goes to the Pools, and the Pools give back a child. Every corpse that thing took and raised was a cradle robbed — for three generations we watched our unborn walk toward us with weapons. That is the arithmetic under the chronicle, and it is why we speak of the war only to the dead. We renamed the land as we left it: Loquitchatzotech, which your maps render Isle of Death — close, and wrong. The word means, more nearly, the place where our dead are not ours. Your sailors note that no birds fly there. The birds are wiser than the sailors.

Of the Vigils, which you feared

Of our worship you never asked at all. You were frightened of it, which was the single most intelligent thing about you, and I honoured the fear too much to relieve it.

The Cult of the Stars does not keep your calendar of months and feast-days; we keep Vigils — six watches in each Cycle, one for each of the Elder Ones, timed by the risings of their stars. A Vigil is not a festival. Nothing is eaten, nothing is sung; the Xion take the sky's account, the reckoners enter it, and the entry is the rite. We ask our gods for nothing; we confirm their position, as your harbour-masters confirm a ship still at anchor. For two hundred years after your death the Vigil of the Observer needed no priests: the feast hung in the sky for every warm nation to call an omen. You saw the red moon rise. You did not see it furl, around 3260, withdrawn as an eye is withdrawn from a lens when the observation is complete. We entered that in the rolls too. The entry reads: seen enough.

And there is one of the Six to whom we no longer pray. To that one we perform the Apology: once a Cycle, in silence, every caste, the whole night. What we apologise for you will not learn from me, nor from anyone living; that is what eldest is for.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

In Chapter I, I called their waiting the patience of a jailer's servant. Here it is at home. The Vigils are not adoration; they are shift-work — six watches kept over six sealed prisoners, logged nightly for thirty-five centuries. The Apology is addressed to the one their forebears reached for and botched: the Xebechani strand of the single Catastrophe, told from the temple side. A servant does not mend the household past the master's homecoming — and by their own sky-ledger, the homecoming has begun.

The third question

Your third question was in your last letter, which I did not answer. You offered to come to the Pools with your meters; you had numbered the wagons; you had already chosen the road. I read the letter three times and repaired the seal, and you died — briefly, as your people do everything — before I found the sentence I wanted. This letter is that sentence, grown long.

Here is what you would have seen. The body is carried down at dusk, by Xudra, singing their one song: the counting-chant, revised at every carrying. The Pools take the body as still water takes a stone — without ceremony, which is the ceremony. A season later, if all is well, the water gives back a child, and the tally rises by the number it fell. It has been all is well for longer than your civilisation has existed.

It is no longer all is well. Some children come back wrong now — unfinished, or folded strangely, or perfect and empty. We do not put them away, as your villages do your Marked; we name them, and they hold the softest offices. And some do not come back at all. The chant's number has fallen, Cycle upon Cycle, for two hundred years. You would write: the series converges. We are dying of a lost instruction, in good order, without complaint — the complaint would change nothing, and it would be overheard.

Twice, since you died, they have carried me down in the proper manner, and twice the water has declined me. The attendants dried me and said nothing; there is no rite for it — I checked; I keep the rolls. I am the eldest of the reckoners because the machine that continues us has twice declined to conclude my case. Do not condole. It is instructive, being one's own discrepancy.

In the Chaos, when the dead walked even your tidy streets, we opened the deepest door in Xanadu — the one that opens away — and sent a part of every caste through to the far sanctuary, until the sky's eye furled. Most came home. Some keep the far side still. I will not describe it: you would have wanted to meter it, and there are doors even a dead pupil must be refused. It is the last refusal, little archmage. I am sorry for all of them.

For five hundred years I have wondered whether your meters would have read what our rites cannot; the wondering is the nearest thing to doubt my kind is issued. So take the sentence I could not write while you lived: I do not know that you could have saved the Pools. I know that I did not let you try. Between those sentences lies the whole distance between my people and yours, and I am no longer certain we stand on the better side.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The "far sanctuary" is on no map of Ymir because it is not on Ymir. It is X-01 — the artificial planet the empire built to regulate its Portal network — and Xanadu's deepest door opens onto it. The evacuation was partial and mostly temporary: most returned; Xuthal still flies. But some stayed, and the priest's refusal hides the one fact that would remake this chapter's grief: out there, children are grown in Pools that do not fail, because the switchboard was never out of maintenance. Somewhere past the sky stands a Xebech that is not dying. Ask which is the backup of which — and who decided.

Last

I have read your book. You ended it with a question you called poetry, having spent two hundred pages calling mine poetry: should the Essence prove a breath after all, you wished your kind to learn, before it exhales, who is breathing. I could not have taught you that. But I may correct the question — you always permitted the correction, in front of those candles of yours that hated me. Do not ask who breathes. Ask what a held breath is for. One holds a breath to dive. One holds it to hide. One holds it to listen. The world will learn which, in a while that will feel long to it, and is not.

Three questions; three answers. If the warm peoples read this over your shoulder — they will; they are incapable of not — let them note what the only full account of my country cost: one brief warm woman who asked on and not how, and waited, with instruments, and died waiting. That is the epitaph I would have cut for you, had anyone asked the stone-cutters of Xuthal: SHE ASKED ON.

Sleep on, warm pupil. You were very brief, and the night is long, and I find I mind its length differently now.

Written in Xuthal, in the Cycle your reckoners number 3518, on the ninth night of Tortoise, when nothing watched back.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Who reads letters to the dead? Count. The grieving write them; the archivists bind them; I annotate them. Three readers, and none of us the address. Now mark where the priest deposited this one: in the single book on Ymir written in two layers, knowing these margins would take it in; a being that repairs the seals of letters it declines does not mislay a packet. This letter is also a report, filed where the sleepless would find it. The Cult has prayed for thirty-five centuries to addressees the warm world calls absent and has never once believed an address empty; it does not believe this one is. Decide, for your own table, whether the pupil reads nothing — the Dreamlands are not fussy about what the dying dreamed last.

The Curator

I have had a fair copy sent to the College of the Seventh Circle, where Sir Drusilde's reading-room is kept as she left it; the librarian entered it in the register as Correspondence, incoming — answered late. The original is bound here, where the address, being impossible, is at least in good company. — the Curator

Colophon. Chapter IX·B (late-bound; deposited, not commissioned). X · CARTOSA, MERIDIAN, AND THE SOUTHERN SEAS

The home waters: the Principality, the Republic, and everything a master must know south of the middle sea

From the home-water books of the sea-diary of Ramiro Estioca, licensed navigator of the Hermetic Order of the Blue Mantles (Navigators), master of the brigantine La Bendata; with excerpts from the ledgers of Anselmo Duarte, factor of the Order at Puerto Nuevo. Gathered posthumously at the Crystal Seat.

The Curator

The circumnavigation (Chapter V) made Ramiro's name; these entries, kept between commissions in his last years, make his portrait. I have interleaved them with his factor's ledgers, which arrived in the same tin — a second voice, drier even than the first, that prices what the diary loves. Nothing corrects a romantic like his accountant. — the Curator

Fourth of Boar, 3517. Home water; wind steady off the Twin Coasts; the Bendata knows the way without me. Two islands the world calls by one name. Cartosa, under the Sierra Plata; and the sister isle under the Sierra Grande, where Meridian rides its river. Between and about them run the Twin Coasts, and a scatter of roads and anchorages I could con blindfolded, like my ship's namesake: Garzona, Portejo, Santa Hermana, Puerto Nuevo, La Roca, Punta del Toro where it all began. Jungle inland, dry plains south of the ranges, and weather that gives honest warning, which is more than can be said of the politics.

The Factor's ledger

From the factor's ledger (Puerto Nuevo). Item, refit of the Bendata: cordage, pitch, Meridian oak, 212 doubloons. Item, harbour dues, 9. Item, tariff to the Order on the ship's weather-work, 31 — entered under weather, the Order having taxed the wind these thousand years.

Eleventh of Boar. Landsmen think the Principality old as its rocks. It is a colony grown fat, and I say that with love. Letian keels under Marco Cartaurelius made Punta del Toro; a fort followed, then a province — Cartoris — renamed Cartosa when the captain died aboard; a governor was jumped up to Prince nineteen centuries after the Maelstrom, and the Carrillo have kept the palace since — through plague, three wars with Meridian, and once, the old men say, through a year when the sun itself turned traitor. Say what you like of the Carrillo (quietly): they have never taxed the sea more than it would bear, or not for long.

Twentieth of Salamander. Rain; the Old Lighthouse tavern at Garzona. Old Jacinto — eighty years old for the thirty I have known him — did his winter piece tonight: the Year of the Black Sun. Five hundred years gone now, and every teller's grandfather's grandfather manned a boom-chain in it. The sun came up black as a hanged man's tongue — Jacinto does the voice — and hooded men came out of the cellars with the Black Sun on their breasts: the Black Dawn. Fires in the old city; chains across the harbour mouth; and young Alberto Carrillo V, that was after called the Steady, holding the palace stair until the Navy came up from Santa Hermana with the true dawn behind them. The coup failed; the cult burned; the boys bought Jacinto rum and slept badly. So did I, and I know it for history. Some tales keep their teeth.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The teeth are real. The Black Dawn was no faction of ambitious lords: it was — is — a cult of Ahriman and the Sovereign Demons, its seal the Black Sun, its captain in that year the mage Tobias, its true business the relics of the Adepts (a sceptre; certain maps to a golden city — see below). It burned the way embers burn. A Black Sun, note, is only a sun you cannot see shining; the cult keeps its own calendar, and five hundred years is not long on it.

Second of Stag. Across the strait to Meridian; wind fair, papers in order, flag polite. Say "the enemy" in a Cartosan tavern and you mean the Republic of Meridian; say it aboard and you mean the weather; the weather is more sincere. An island-republic: the city rides natural and made islands in a great river's arms at the jungle's edge, built on water like a wager that paid. The Public Assembly of its noble houses governs, under a President elected each five years — figurehead or tyrant according to the man — and the Wardens guard the Assembly in parade armour and house-masked helms. Its wealth is Urakron, the enchanted wood of the adjacent jungle (my own ship's knees are Urakron); its pride is its artists; its memory is the Wave Wars. The Old Port keeps the scars of our fleet's work; the New Port stands on two razed quarters, one of them the Lighthouse of the Vallerini — the house that sold its own city to Cartosa in the second war and was paid with a governorship. Three wars in all: Meridian won the first, lost the second, and half-won the third, which leaves the score at the most dangerous number a rivalry can stand at. I love the place the way you love a rival: exactly, and armed.

The Curator

The reader will find charts, and one persistent gazetteer, placing Meridian inland. It is an island-republic, as the fathoms and the customs-receipts agree. — the Curator

The Factor's ledger

Factor's ledger. Item, Urakron, quarter-load, against a letter of credit on the Mercantile Society of Meridian, 480 doubloons; discounted 8 in the hundred at Cartosa, the ink being Meridianese.

Ninth of Wyvern. For the young master keeping his first log, the order of things ashore. The Navy is the Principality's pride and its true crown; the Admiral outweighs most ministers, and within the Navy ride the Sea Dragons — four squads of fifty out of Santa Hermana, under their Grand Dragons and the Elder Dragon, who is by tradition the Admiral himself. Sea-saints with sabres; salute them. The Gold Guard is the land army and the peacetime watch, thirteen ranks of gold braid the nobles sneer at — and some of that braid answers to the Shadows, mind. The Shield Company are the mercenaries the Guard hires when the work turns real. My own paymasters, the Blue Mantles: the mages' guild of the realm, wed to the Navy by old treaty, three factions under one blue cloak — the Navigators, who move ships, weather, money, and me; the Cerulean Eye, diviners and letter-readers, the Prince's secret police in all but invoice; and the Drowners, battle-mages whose name is the only honest advertising in the Principality. Every essence-worker in the realm registers with the Order and pays dues, or is outlaw — priests and nobles exempt, naturally; the Order's genius was making wizardry a tariff. I pay mine each Griffon, and each Griffon I say the same word over the receipt. Below all this, the Shadow Society runs the under-trade — "never met them," says every man who has — and at Meridian the Guild of Singers licenses even the singing of spells; so between the Mantles and the Singers, the only free magic in the south is a mother's blessing, and I expect a stamp for that any year now.

The Curator

(Of the Order's other famous work — the Zortos, the fox-headed hunting stock its alchemists breed by a rite they do not publish — the Order's own licensed man writes nothing, and the huntsman of Chapter XIV, who is nobody's man, writes all a reader needs. — the Curator)

Sixteenth of Wyvern. The nine houses, rated as a pilot rates anchorages. Carrillo — the palace; deep water, mind the guns. Contreras — the Admiralty; a Navy house, promptest pay in the realm. Vidal — Navy also, smaller, fair. Escorsa — heads the Blue Mantles always; my owners' owners; polite, and reads your mail. Cadenas — rich as a reef is sharp; they keep an Archmage in the family the way others keep hounds. Vallerini — turncoats ennobled; Merchant Guild; the Meridian governorship, such as remains of it; charming; count your fingers after. Escolar — merchant-risen, feuding with Contreras, and their manifests and their cargoes are cousins at best; the Shadows have hooks in that house. Sail for them once, cash, no signature. Stuba — disgraced in a coup plot; bad credit, worse friends. And Gibraleon — I will write it where their proctors cannot reach: rumour gives them necromancy and demon-traffic, and rumour has been right about smaller things. No Gibraleon charter. I have refused three. The Bendata floats on my superstitions.

Fifth of Serpent. Watering up the Rio Tapajero; boats out, muskets dry. The interior is not empty; it is patient. The Nkishi — the wild orcs — let beasts bite them from boyhood and wear the scars as we wear medals; brave past sense; their Bokkor shamans keep the ancestor-spirits and the dark forest gods, the Orisha, of whom the Mayombe are the ones you pray not to interest. Trade steel and rum at the bank; keep the boats afloat. The M'Bantha goblins stole my second-best kettle at the third bend, as they have stolen something on every watering these thirty years: they hold that our power lives in our things — "Cargo," the traders call the creed — and go decked in buttons, spoons, and one bosun's whistle I still miss. Cunning as bilge-rats, and not stupid; ask the punitive expeditions that never came back. In the highlands sit the Arawak: dwarves who work stone and bone and will not touch metal, gems braided into their hair to count their deeds, and death preferred to capture — remember that before you play at press-gangs. Their mountains hold Shald, the magic glass worth more than emerald, and the uninvited expeditions that go up after it feed the mountains. Last, the Ur-Tanoth, shy as smoke: the finest woodwork, herbs, and furs on Ymir, but they sicken and die if caged, so the trade runs through the Tal-Tanoth — the little cat-folk, who take a broker's tenth and your heart. Sell to all; cheat none; anchor mid-stream.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Deep in that same jungle stands the tale the whole south tells and no one prices honestly: Cumbre de Oro, the golden city — truly Taremorn, a rich city of the Adepts' empire, raised on an Earthnode now curdled. In the Demon War's first days its king was tricked by Ismael, a Sovereign Demon, and a Demon Prince cursed the city for its greed: the gold is real, and whoever takes it joins the garrison — a soulless skeleton bound inside those sad walls. The Black Dawn went looking for it in the Black Sun year. That is the measure of them: they knew what the gold costs, and went anyway.

Eleventh of Hawk. The pearl-mark off Costa Sileña; sea like poured glass. Laid the Bendata at the reef-mark, hoisted the shell-sign, and by the tide's turn they came up: the Sohleugir, the true lords of the southern seas, older than every flag in this book. Their kingdom is below — Ssas'Kher, whose coral walls have never been breached, not in millennia, not by anything. They surface to trade pearls and coral for worked steel and glass: few words, exact weights, and no haggling, because the sea does not haggle. I have dealt with them man and boy and been cheated never, which I can say of no port with a customs-house. Take off your hat to them. It costs nothing, and they notice.

The Factor's ledger

Factor's ledger. Item, pearls of Ssas'Kher, two strings, 620 doubloons. Item, rose-branch coral, 75. Nota: the master again refused to anchor over the drowned ground south of the mark, which the men call the Oyster's water; the delay is charged to piety.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Under the Oyster's water lies what is left of the Cult of the Blue Oyster: the drowned shrine where the Sovereign Demon Ea, Lord of the Abysses, was called up — and unmade, the first of the Sovereigns ever slain. What the cult left below: a shell-temple cracked open like its namesake, and the Blue Oyster Shadows — the cultists' shades, still keeping a service their god cannot hear. The pearls of that ground grow large and lightless, and should not be brought up. The Sohleugir do not breach such places; they contain them. Their patience with Cartosan anchors ends at that mark, and the master's piety kept a peace he never knew was in question.

Third of Spider. The ship's chest, southern issue, for masters new to these waters. Utombe, a jungle-leaf distillate: kills pain outright — a man will fight on past a wound that should have him screaming, and not know he is dying; issue it, and count the vials. Qarando root: fire and gladness in the blood; boarding-courage in a chew — and your best hand diving over the rail after a fleeing prize like a fool. I ration it to fights I mean to win anyway. Subotan, the violet poppy: bliss, torpor, visions, then need; illegal in the Principality this long age and smuggled every year of it; the margins are handsome and the Navy hangs you handsomely — choose. Rud-Tekma berries the wizards chew: spells fly truer, they say, while the chewer sits entranced like a saint on a shelf — which is why my weather-worker chews nothing during weather. As for beasts: the Daretas, six-legged hill-oxen in armour plate, tender eating and hide for shields; the Reptoids, horse-lizards that furnish mounts and most of the realm's leather, my sea-boots included; and the Yarhim, the great blue crabs, platter-sized to nine metres across — Maelstrom-marked beasts, whose shell makes armour kings feud over, and whose taking makes widows.

Nineteenth of Spider. Last, the blood of it all: money. The south runs on the doubloon and the piece-of-eight, and above a chest's weight, on letters of credit of the merchant guilds, good at a shave from Armora's counting-houses to the eastern banners. Prize-money for lawful war; tariffs on everything, including, as noted, the wind. Beyond home waters every land coins its own — church coin in Letia, marks and scrip in Armora, strung cash and jade in the east, hacksilver north, salt and coral in the Sadhir; and in the Gaerwath they pay in Arvo, powdered Essence, and in bound devils, and a master who accepts either deserves the change. Between realms the trade rides on metal by weight, on Shald and mana-salt, and on paper and trust — and paper is only trust with a seal on it. So is a ship's log. So, I suppose, is this.

— of Spider, 3518. Glass falling fast; blue weather on the southern rim; we run for

The Curator

The sentence is not finished. The Twin Coasts returned the tin two winters later; they did not return the ship. From the diary our cartographers drew the world. From the ledger, one line more, and I give the accountant the last word he would have wanted: —

The Factor's ledger

Factor's ledger, final entry. Item, the brigantine Bendata, thirty souls, master R. Estioca: written off. Nota: against this loss, no sum entered.

Colophon. Chapter X. Narrator: Ramiro Estioca (returning from Chapter V) with his factor's ledgers (both invented framing voices; NARRATORS.md). Drawn from canon/geography.md (the two isles, Sierra Plata/Sierra Grande, the Twin Coasts, settlements), canon/factions.md (the Gold Guard, the Navy and Sea Dragons, the Blue Mantles' three factions, the Shadow Society, the Black Dawn, the nine houses), canon/history.md (the colony and Principality; the Wave Wars of 2320–2350 / 2817–2830 / 3018–3023; the Year of the Black Sun of 3017 — all told as history from ≈3518), canon/peoples.md (Nkishi and Bokkor, M'Bantha "Cargo," Arawak and Shald, Ur- and Tal-Tanoth, Sohleugir and Ssas'Kher), canon/bestiary.md (Daretas, Reptoids, Yarhim; the Blue Mantles' Zortos, cross-noted to Chapter XIV), canon/magic.md (registration and dues; the drugs Utombe, Qarando, Subotan, Rud-Tekma), and canon/economy.md (doubloons, letters of credit, magic-tariffs); margins from canon/secret-the-great-lie.md, canon/factions.md, and canon/history.md (the Black Dawn as Ahriman/Sovereign-Demon cult under Tobias; Ea, the Blue Oyster, and its shades; Taremorn/Cumbre de Oro and Ismael).
