XII·B · OPHIUR

the Continent of the Morning, sung by one who keeps its songs

As sung by Nyema of Ga'Namesh, Keeper of the Thousand Songs of the Ivory Coalition, and taken down at Elendor by the Curator's own hand.

The Curator

This leaf reached Elendor after the book was sewn, and I have bound it in where it belongs — south of the Sadhir, east toward the dragon-kingdoms, which is where you will find the place itself. I owe the reader a confession and Nyema an apology: in the chapters already bound, Ophiur is glimpsed only from the sea, and even the fairest of those glimpses only from an anchorage, and my northern narrators pass over its peoples with a phrase — i Popoli Neri, "the Black Peoples," as if a continent were one nation and a thousand songs one song. Nyema crossed three seas to correct me. She dictated; I wrote; where my pen slowed she waited, which is its own kind of correction. — the Curator

Ask me what Ophiur is, and I will answer: it is the morning's country. The sun that reaches you worn-out and pale in your grey north rises out of our sea with its strength still on it. Write that first, Curator. The rest follows.

Your mapmakers call us wild and unexplored. I have seen your maps: the wildness is in them, not in us — a blankness where their authors did not go. Ophiur is jungle, yes, deep and tangled, a green that swallows roads; and it is mountains, the Dark Mountains where the dark stone drinks the light, and the Tortoise Mountains whose backs carry the clouds; and between and beyond it is the great savanna, gold to every horizon, where the grass moves like a sea that decided against water. Three countries in one, and peoples to match. Your Leti and your Cartosans call all of us the Black Peoples and think they have said something. It is as if I called all the north the Pale Kingdoms and claimed thereby to know a Letian friar from a Helgedad skald. I have met both. The difference is considerable.

Hear the coast first, for the coast is mine. We are the Ivory Coalition — free cities allied and independent, sisters who share a table and keep separate houses: Zabhula of the hundred quays, Akhon of the deep bells, and my own Ga'Namesh, where the song-halls stand higher than the warehouses, though the warehouses are not humble. What the rest of Ymir digs for, hoards, and kills over, our land gives with both hands: the precious minerals of the Dark Mountains, orihalcum that drinks magic silent, shald that holds it burning, the lotus in all its colours. The caravans come up from the Sadhir salt and down from the dragon-kingdoms' passes, and the ships come from everywhere ships are made. We sell to all of them. We are rich the way our sun is strong — natively, and without apology.

Hear the savanna, which is not ours and never will be. The gold grass belongs to the Great Kingdom of Tarkur, the kingdom of the Ur-Tanoth, and it is a kingdom without one city, without one throne, and without one day of standing still. The Ur follow what they name the Storm. Do not picture weather. The Storm, as their singers gave it to me — and singer to singer we do not lie — is the power that is in everything made and everything unmade: one energy, creating and destroying, an eternal wheel of birth and death that rolls across the world as wind rolls across grass. All peoples stand in the Storm, they say — you, Curator, are standing in it now, as am I — but only the Ur can feel it pass. So they follow it. That is the whole law of Tarkur and the whole of its religion: the kingdom goes where the Storm goes, and its three castes are three ways of knowing the road — the Seekers, who scent the Storm and lead the migrations; the Priests, who see it and hear it and lay their hands upon it; and the tribes, who trust the other two. I asked a Seeker once what the Storm smelled like. He said: like the world, before it has decided anything. I have carried that answer for thirty years and am not done with it.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Attend to the Storm-faith, reader; it is the rarest thing in this book. Every great creed of Ymir kneels, knowingly or not, to something with a mask on. The Ur of Tarkur kneel to nothing. What their Seekers scent is the Essence itself — its currents, its turning cycle of making and unmaking — perceived raw, without doctrine, without names, without one fingerprint of the usurpers on it. The Adepts built no temple here; there was nothing to steal, because the Ur never wrote their god down. Of all Ymir's faiths, the Storm is the one that was never lied to. What it may notice, now that the seals are broken and the currents shift, is a question worth a campaign.

Hear Gazal, the free city, the old bone. At the feet of the Dark Mountains stands a city older than every kingdom I have named, and it stands the way a skull stands in the grass. Gazal was a capital once — of what empire, even the Thousand Songs do not agree; the songs only agree that it was great, and that it ended. Now it is free the way a ruin is free: no king, many masters. Its streets above are a market for everything and a guarantee of nothing — merchants, adventurers, thieves, and those who have been all three in one afternoon — and below its streets lies the vast undercity, vault under vault under vault, of which no honest map exists. People go down for the old empire's riches. Enough come back up to keep the inns full. Not enough come back up to keep the priests idle.

Hear Horatli, though Horatli would rather you did not. Under Mount Horat, the highest white head of the Tortoise Mountains, in a valley the mountain half-hides with its own shoulder, there is a city of scholars. Mages, arcanists, a conclave — quiet, courteous when found, difficult to find twice. What they study there they do not sell in Zabhula, which among the Coalition is considered the strangest thing about them.

And hear the deep jungle, which I will sing but not enter. In the interior, past where the caravan-songs end, the legends begin, and our legends do not behave like your northern ones — they have witnesses. It is said, and the oldest songs say it plainly, that in the deep green live creatures of the most ancient races, on Ymir since the world's origins, who endured the Wars of the Mages that broke the world and simply — continued. It is known, not merely said, that demons and vampires keep court in the drowned-in-green ruins, and that certain tribes carry them tribute in exchange for protection from the jungle's plainer horrors; do not judge those tribes until you have heard what the plainer horrors are. And there is the song of songs: the hidden Garden of Xiombarg, warded by guardians older than its walls, where grow the plants that cure — the songs are specific — any disease, any affliction. Every generation, someone sells a map to it. Every generation, the map-seller retires wealthy and the map-buyer retires missing. The Garden, I think, is not lost. The Garden is kept. There is a difference, and it has teeth.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Three true things under the songs. The "most ancient races" of the interior are exactly what the singer says: native lines older than the Adepts' gardening, survivors of the first world — the deep jungle is one of the few places the makers' catalogue was never completed, which is why their dragon-wardens circle it and do not land. The tribute-courts are real, and some of what takes tribute there crawled out of the prison-crack at the Tide and walked south looking for a throne no one would contest. And the Garden of Xiombarg is no legend — its guardians predate the Catastrophe, its cures are real, and the interesting question is not where it is but what it is for: a garden is where you keep what you mean to plant again.

Last, hear my own people, the Ophiriti, since your other chapters will tell you of everyone else. We are tall, and we are strong, and our skin is the black the night sky wishes it were, and in our oldest houses runs the oldest blood there is: the blood of the Hyperboreans, the fire-made, the first people — of whom your north keeps only the Vorskgen in their ice and a handful of Letian lords too proud to know what they are proud of. Our nobles do not write their inheritance in books that burn. We sing it. Grandmother to mother to daughter, the wisdom and the craft of the first people, a thousand songs deep — I carry some hundreds of them myself, and I have given your book only the ones we sell. Ask me what Ophiur is, Curator, and I will answer one more time: it is the place where the oldest fire still remembers being lit. Bind that in, south of the salt. It will keep.

The Curator

On the Hyperboreans and their surviving lines — Vorskgen, Ophiriti, the Pureblood Leti — see the Peoples chapter, which Nyema's visit obliged me to amend; and for the continent as the sea meets it, the navigator's diary in Chapter V now carries its Ophiur soundings. The Coalition's wares — orihalcum, shald, the lotus trade — are treated in the Treatise on Magic and the appendix of substances. — the Curator

Colophon. Chapter XII·B (late-bound). Narrator: Nyema of Ga'Namesh (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md). Drawn from sources/raw/Additions.md via canon/geography.md (Ophiur: the Ivory Coalition, Tarkur and the Storm, Gazal, Horatli, the jungle legends, the Garden of Xiombarg), canon/peoples.md (Hyperboreans/Ophiriti; the Ur-Tanoth castes), and in the margins canon/secret-the-great-lie.md. [INVENTED]: the narrator herself; the phrases and anecdotes of her telling (the Seeker's answer, the map-seller aphorism); the margin readings of the Storm (uncorrupted Essence-perception), the interior as uncatalogued native lines, and the Garden's purpose — all consistent with, but extending, the source.

XII·C · THE BOOK OF THE SLEEPING SHORE

the Dreamlands, and Kyria under its veil, set down by one who piloted sleepers for hire

From the retirement memoir of Hesper Vaneck of Armora, thirty years dream-pilot of the licensed house at the sign of the Bell & Candle, Glassharp Lane, the New City; styled on her licence — No. 114 in the registry of the College of the Seventh Circle — Conductor of Assisted Sleep, second grade. Deposited at the Crystal Seat, unasked, beneath fifty-one volumes of descent-logs.

The Curator

This leaf was not commissioned. It arrived two winters after the book was sewn, in a deal crate under the log-volumes of a licensed Armorite dream-house; the arithmetic of their waking-times reconciles to the minute across thirty years, which nothing else in this Compendium can claim. The covering letter asked only that the logs be kept somewhere that does not burn. The memoir lay underneath them, unmentioned. I bind it among the late leaves because the desert chapter ends standing at a door into this same country, and because the late leaves are where this book keeps the accounts that came in after the accounts were closed. The reader will notice the narrator's tenses misbehave. I queried one passage and received her only reply: "It is not a slip. It is the tense the country keeps." — the Curator

I was thirty years on the night-lists of the Bell & Candle, a licensed dream-parlour of the New City, and I made four thousand nine hundred and six logged descents into the country this leaf concerns. The trade calls me a dream-pilot. My licence — I have it before me; it wants dusting — calls me a Conductor of Assisted Sleep, second grade, which is what the Permit Office believes a dream is: a conveyance respectable persons are assisted into and out of, like a tram. Both names are honest. Neither is sufficient. I will keep to the tram-language as long as I am able; it is the language of my log-books, and my log-books are the only part of me that has never lied.

Understand one thing and the rest will sit straight. The Compendium's other chapters describe countries you may decline to visit. Not this one. You go every night. Every sleeper on Ymir — the Theocrat on his cushions, you, reader, some hours from now — goes down into the Dream World as water goes downhill, and comes back at dawn with wet hair and no memory. The learned call the country a province of the Spirit World, folded against ours like silk against a lining; I have felt the fold. Passage, then, is free, universal, and wasted on everyone. What a licensed parlour sells is arrival: going down awake behind your own eyes, with a pilot who knows the way home and a bell already set for your return.

Weigh the rest of my testimony by what a sitting was like. The client takes the couch; I take the chair; between us the Oneiroscope, warm, smelling faintly of rain on brass; at the wall the house wizard watches the Essence-meter. I enter the waking-time in the log — the waking-time first, before the name, before the tariff; a pilot who enters the fee before the bell is a pilot I will not sit with. The lamp is lowered. The bell above the couch is struck once, softly, so the sleeping ear will know its voice at the far end. And then — forgive me; this is the tense the country keeps — then we are on the Stair; the mind will not go down without a metaphor to hold. Below, the water of sleep lies black and warm, lit from underneath, and the first shore shows itself the way it always shows: a lamp you remember from childhood, in a window you cannot possibly remember. We step off. The sand takes the weight of two. I count my fingers — the oldest exercise of the trade — and there are five, and we have arrived, and the whole enormous, kindly treachery of the place opens before my client like a garden.

Then the bell, and tea. Ninety marks the sitting, waking-times observed to the minute. The licence is not a tax but a tether: the Crier counted eleven tourists lost last season to houses promising more than the tariff-board, and all eleven sat at unlicensed couches. In thirty years the Bell & Candle lost nobody. Nobody, I should write, but one; and I will come to her.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The pilot believes the fee ends at the tariff-board. It does not. The Dreamlands are a province, and provinces have rulers: the Outer Gods hold this one. Sealed out of waking creation, the Ancient Gods were never sealed out of sleep — the one border the Adepts could not close — and they keep it as landlords, patient as tide: every night the whole world crosses into their reach and comes back unremembering, and the parlours' little bells ring because something tolerates the ringing. And the road has a keeper: Topramesk the Yellow, last of the seven who broke the world, holds the dream-gate of drowned Ragash and polices every lucid crossing out of Ymir — for the dreamers' safety, the records say, without saying against what. No one has asked what the true toll is, or to whom the door is paid.

Now the geography, as far as a second-grade licence runs. The near country the trade calls the Hem — the shallows just under the surface of sleep, where everything is sewn out of the sleeper. The Crier's advertisement is for once exact: gardens no gardener planted, cities of brass, skies you will not describe at breakfast. The Hem is safe the way a mirror is safe: nothing in it but what you brought. Below it lie the Meadows, and there the trade begins in earnest, for the Meadows are shared. You know a thing is not yours by the way it fails to need you — an inn that goes on standing when no one dreams in it. A pilot learns the fixed landfalls as a coastwise master learns his marks — not by star or needle, for there are none, but by conviction: you steer by what you can hold yourself certain of, which is why the trade takes calm persons and ruins vain ones. Consider Dream Steel, the metal mined, they say, under that country: I have held a knife of it, and it weighed exactly what I believed it weighed.

Below the Meadows — in the plainest tram-language I own — lie the deeper shores, and the deeper shores are not priced in marks. Nothing down there wants your gold. The far country trades in waking things, and names its own price: hours first, for time there runs as it pleases — a forty-minute sitting can cost a day and a half; then the fine grain of memory, so that a man comes home whole but can no longer say his mother's smell; then names. A colleague of mine paid a name once. He is well. He is called something now, the way a shop is called something. That is the tariff of the deep, and no bell rings there softly.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Past the deeper shores, in order: Ragash-below — the drowned capital's shadow, standing whole in the dream with every window lit, the fixed point through which every lucid crossing out of Ymir must pass, which is why the gatekeeper keeps it. Beyond Ragash, the Desert of Lost Dreams, which everyone who has tried it calls infinite. It is not. On its far side hangs Arcadia: a steam-wreathed world of islands suspended in an ocean of air, where the Essence blows in Winds and is bottled in Van Argan Stones — batteries of refined Banite that burn for ever, the engine-heart of a civilisation of gliders and rotor-ships. Its great power is the Crystal Empire of the Kraeva — grey-skinned, bloodlessly courteous, certain they are Arcadia's only rightful people — prosecuting an armed peace under a Prophecy of the Return; their captives "wear the White" and serve unarmed. No dreamer of Ymir has crossed the Desert; almost none suspects a far side. Topramesk alone certainly knows Arcadia exists — and a Return, in a world reachable only through his gate, would have to come out through something. Ask what.

The parlour is the paying door, but it is not the only door. In the south they do without engines: the Green Lotus of the Great Erg opens the mind in a two-hour coma, and every dose gambles the soul slipping loose into the Dream World with no bell set — the flower does for a copper what my house did for ninety marks, the way a flood does the work of a ferry. In the deep Erg the nomads rope their camels away from dream-gates that do not open onto the desert, and a seer of Ulan-Tang sells the crossing to those who must; somewhere past those gates, in a prison built as a palace, the archmage Mubafer keeps the sweetest parlour of all — the pilots' rule for him is one sentence: drink nothing, envy nothing, and do not let him show you the library. And some need no door. Some are born arriving — awake behind their own eyes on the first shore as children; the trade calls them Dreamers, half in envy. A few learn to walk out of their own sleep into the common deep and home again, carrying — knowledge, mostly; some swear, small things that fit in a closed hand — and these are the Travellers, for whom the Permit Office's alphabet keeps no page, which tells you its whole opinion of them.

The Curator

For these doors from the waking side, see the desert chapter before this leaf, whose lore-keeper ends standing at the same threshold; for the parlours as their city sees them, the Crier's notice in Chapter VII. — the Curator

From the log of Descent 3,441, ninth of Serpent, 3502; client a College man, Permit C, question not stated; down at the third bell of night, waking-bell the sixth. We go deep. Past the last landfall I hold certain — the unlit Lighthouse, which every pilot knows and none has climbed — the water of sleep changes colour, and there is the garden. Hedges of black glass; no wind. At the centre a woman made of brass and porcelain sits with her own winding-key in her lap. She is weeping. The tears are crystal, and fall with the sound of very small bells, and the gravel is white with them — drifts of them, years of them. She says, to no one, in the voice of a public clock: "I am Vespertina. It is not yet paid." My client opens his mouth to ask what is not paid. I strike the bell myself. — In that log's incident-column my own hand has written: none. That was the one cowardice of my licensed life, and I mend it here, in a book instead. Every deep pilot knows the garden; no licensed log carries it. And the name is the name on the Peacemaker's monuments — a Grand Duchess four centuries and a half dead, who ended the Great War and gave this calm age its calm. What her name is doing on the lips of a weeping engine at the bottom of sleep I do not know; I know only that the men who might know have made very sure it is nobody's trade to ask.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The garden is real, and the log's "none" was the lie. Vespertina VonGryffen, the Peacemaker, ended the Great War and reconciled Armora with Letia — by statecraft, say the histories. Say instead: by contract. She went down, by the College's first engine, to treat with the far country's landlords for the calm of the world above; and the far shores take only the coin the pilot names — waking things. Her line got the age its peace. The dream got her: kept, wound, running down against a term. The country remembers her as a machine because that is what the bargain made of her — an engine of the calm, ticking. She weeps because she has read the lease; "it is not yet paid" is not a complaint but a countdown. The Veiled Hand calls this age's calm patience, and the patience not ours; ask whether the two are one instrument. What she bought, and in which year it falls due, are left open on purpose: a calm with a due-date is the best clock a campaign can keep.

Last, the island — and the island is why any of this is written. Sailors know Kyria as fifty leagues of fog that a full gale will not move; they sound it, find no gap, and stand away. Under the fog, the traders say, lies an island the old ruinations never touched, and one town, Thonis, where beast-faced folk — the shy Ur-Tanoth — keep lamps lit for nobody; and Thonis, though no chart says so, is the one waking anchorage the dream-country has: the single town on Ymir that stands in both weathers at once, ashore in the world and moored in the dream. I know both reports are right. I have been there. Once. Descent 1,119, twelfth of Unicorn, 3488, a calibration sitting, the house's own, no client aboard; and thirty years have not moved one grain of it.

The mist opens the way a hand opens. There is a town. Lamps in the mist, hung too kindly to be harbour-lights; streets of turf; a smell of wet grass and clean fur. The folk come out unhurried, cat-faced, grave, kind as aunts, and nobody asks my name, and I understand — the understanding of dreams, that arrives without a doorway — that I am being read: turned over gently, page by page, as a border-clerk turns papers, by the mist itself, which is in some fashion also the town. They give me tea. I sit among them the length of a childhood. At last the eldest of them — white-furred, with eyes the colour of the hour before dawn — takes my cup as though I had finished, and says, kindly, in my own mother's voice, the sentence I have spent thirty years contradicting: "Not you; not yet." And the mist folds me out of the town the way a mother folds a child's arms into a coat — and I am on my own couch at the Bell & Candle, weeping into the smell of rain on brass, and the waking-bell has not yet been struck.

I logged it — landfall; inhabited; turned back — and the College lifted the page within the month. No matter. I have it by heart, which is where they cannot file it.

The Curator

Compare Chapter V: three of the navigator's crew, asleep off this same fog, dreamed one town — lamps, mist, folk "kind as aunts" — in the very words this pilot chooses for her landing. Two trades, one border. I have altered neither. — the Curator

Thirty licensed years I steered for that fog — every deep sitting trimmed a little north-by-sleep, always with the bell set, always home to the minute — and I never raised it again. But mark what an old pilot marks. Thonis shelters refugees; on this every trader agrees. And no ship has ever landed there. By what road does a refugee reach an island the sea is not permitted to touch? There is one road only that does not mind water. They arrived asleep. Which means the island keeps some. Which means there is a list, and a door, and a doorkeeper; and I have met her; and she said not yet.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

All true, and truer than she dares. The fog is a veil of the Dreamlands, drawn over Kyria seventeen centuries into the age to hide the island from the Sovereign Demons: Kyria was folded halfway into the Dream World and has been kept there since — untouched, unaging, time within passing as the dream pleases. What the veil guards is the Dreaming Legion: a secret order of men, Tanoth, cloud-giants, and stranger volunteers, drilling for centuries toward the last battle against the Sovereigns, under Gildiss — a Dream Dragon, a follower of Topramesk, who can lift the shadow of Corrupted Essence out of a living soul. The veil reads every sleeper it touches because it is recruiting: the "refugees" of Thonis arrived exactly as the pilot reasons — asleep, chosen, kept. Wardens do not refuse; they file. "Not you; not yet" is a posting-order with the date left blank, because the last battle has not yet called for pilots. When it calls, the Legion will want someone who can bring a thousand sleepers down in good order and hold a bell so it does not ring. The fog knows where she lives. So, now, do you.

So I will confess the descent that is in none of the fifty-one volumes, since the Permit Office has already been paid its opinion of it. In my thirty-first year, on the fourth of Magpie, 3511, I let myself into my own house at the second bell of night, alone — no second pilot, no house wizard, no client, and (write it plainly, Vaneck) no waking-time. I set no bell. A bell calls you home, and I did not want to be called home. I went down past my grade and past my craft, to where the mist begins; and the mist read me again, gently, to the last page; and it sent me back anyway — saying, this time, nothing at all, which is worse. The house found me on the third day; time had run as it pleased. The College took my licence, and the Permit Office takes eleven marks a month for the rest of my life, which is the smaller debt. Since that night I do not dream. My sleep is one grey water with no shore in it, and somewhere off in the grey a lamp that does not come nearer. I am paying, you see, in the far country's own coin — waking things — and I keep the instalments punctually, every night. I was always punctual.

I am old now; I sleep more than I wake; and the trade taught me exactly one prayer, which is a waking-time. So let this leaf stand as my petition, filed where the fog can read it — for it reads; I have felt the thumb at the corner of the page. To whom it concerns, in Thonis or above Thonis: the references are enclosed, fifty-one volumes of them. Thirty years; every sleeper brought home to the minute but one, and the one was myself, and on that account I have paid nightly ever since. I am quiet. I am punctual. I know how to sit beside the sleeping, and how to hold a bell so it does not ring. And if the answer is still not yet, then file me — and there: the lamp is in the window. The stair is under my foot. The mist is opening the way a hand opens. I am not asleep, reader; I am early. Hold the bell.

The Curator

The crate bore no address; the carter's bill named a fishing village on the north-western coast, the last with sight of the fog-bank. I wrote once. The answer came in a neighbour's hand: the pilot sleeps longer each month, wakes cheerful, and asks that her waking-time be left unset. I have entered the leaf accordingly. — the Curator

XII·D · THE SPEAKER OF THE TALLY

the Belligerent Kingdoms, heard at last; a transcript

The words of Marghuz, speaker-of-the-tally, envoy of the tribute-court of the Khan of the Fourth Banner, taken down at the Crystal Seat on the ninth of Spider, 3517, through two sworn interpreters. Where the interpreters disagreed, both renderings stand in brackets, by rule of the Council's clerk.

The Curator

I will record the protocol and confine myself to it. When this Compendium began to gather, the Council at my request sent word east that the peoples' book would hold a leaf for the banners, should the banners want one. Three years passed without answer, which I am told is how the tribute-courts read a contract. Then an envoy came, under the Council's safe-conduct, sworn upon the count she carries. The delegation of Letia asked that its absence from the hall be entered in the minutes; it is entered. Two interpreters were sworn: Master Corvin Attaleo, ransom-factor of the border keeps, who learned the tally-speech in four years' captivity, and Mistress Brill Halfweight, Tal-Tanoth caravan-broker, who learned it in twenty years' trade. Where they differed I have kept both readings, in the order in which they spoke. The envoy declined the chair, asked how many stood in the hall, was answered ninety-one by the clerk's count, said the number was correct, and began. She spoke one hour by the glass, took no questions, and was gone before the sand was. — the Curator

I am Marghuz. I hold the tally of the Fourth Banner: the count is the Khan's, the mouth is mine. Your Council asked the banners for words. Words rot [the first interpreter renders "words rot"; the second insists the tally-word is "words desert"]. I brought the count. It is not the whole count. The whole count is iron, and iron stays home.

Entry the first: the Muster. Before the clans, the gate. The Counter counted us out of the iron gate in ranks of one hundred, gave the count into our hands, and did not come back [the first interpreter renders "did not come back"; the second insists the tense is wrong, and that the word says "has not yet come back"]. The banners are one hundred because the count was one hundred. That is the oldest entry. You have origin stories. We have an opening balance.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The oldest entry is the truest thing in the hour, and the crookedest. There was a gate; there was a Counter; there was a count of one hundred. The orcs are the makers' bio-soldiers of Gwart — soldier-stock, bred and drilled and shipped through the Adepts' own doors to wherever the empire wanted a war won — and what the tally-courts keep as scripture is the muster-roll remembered as liturgy: ranks of one hundred; an officer with no face and no name, only an office. An army does not remember its maker's face. It remembers being counted. Every made people's myth keeps its kernel truly in a crooked mirror — the dwarves their Ancestors, the Little Folk their courts — and this is the orcs' mirror: they were not created, they were issued. And note what the second interpreter heard in the tense. The Counter "has not yet come back." An age of running wild, a Horde, a law of scales — and under all of it, one hundred banners still formed up, still counted. Still waiting for inspection.

Entry: Uglush. Your books write him "the Supreme." The irons write him Uglush of the One Count [the first interpreter gives "the Supreme" nonetheless; the second insists the style means "he who made the many counts one"]. Before Uglush the clans starved by turns and raided by turns, and no one counted either. Entered: forty famine-winters in the hundred cycles before him. Entered: six in the hundred cycles after. In fifteen cycles — your 2015 to 2030 — he took the east of your empire and closed the ledger of famine-winters. Your chronicles say a scourge broke on the marches like weather. Weather is not counted. We were counted, every rank of us, going in. Where Uglush lies is not entered; a count needs no shrine. He is not worshipped. Worship rots a count. He is entered, and his entry is not closed while one banner still counts.

Entry: the marches. Your friar writes "burnt marches," and "held in captivity," and "remembered at every evening office." Marches burn in every conquest; yours burned in ours. From the west, the smoke was all that could be seen. We were east of the smoke, counting. Towns burned in the taking: 31. Towns standing this cycle: 44. Ask who built the thirteen. Letian folk under the banners at the last census — we census — 1,100,000. Their bells: standing, 60; melted in the taking, 9; the count of the melted is kept with the count of the standing [the first interpreter renders "kept"; the second insists the word means "owed"]. Tribute is taken. Tribute is counted, sealed, and receipted, and what is receipted cannot quietly grow. Ask your marchers what the tithe grew to in a bad year, who counted it, and to whose eye the count lay open. Our gates stand open westward to any Letian who walks. Walked west since the courts were set: 40,116. Walked back east: 6,554 [the first interpreter renders "walked back"; the second insists the word is "came home"].

Entry: the law. The count is the law. A death is an entry; an entry demands balance — a life, a weight, or a service, as the wronged banner elects; blood answers only when the scales refuse. Quarrels between banners in the last hundred cycles: 61. Settled by weighing: 49. Settled by blood: 12. The hundred tallies are summed at the Great Khan's court; the sum is the throne. A Khan who falsifies his tally is struck from the hundred. Struck in the last hundred cycles: three. Their names are no longer spoken [so the first interpreter; the second insists the word is harder: "their names are subtracted"]. You have hells. We have arithmetic.

Entry: One-Eye. Nine hundred and ninety-six cycles after Uglush closed his count, the red moon stood up in the sky. Ugrumuulsh One-Eye, Great Khan, read it with the eye he had, and armed the hundred. Your Theocrat read it and armed. Your Technocrat read it and armed. Three readers of one omen; only ours is written superstition in your chapters. Then your two churches, who will not share a cup, came against us under one banner. Entered: our dead of that war, 118,240. Entered also: their dead. They do not publish their number. We hold it. This is not spite. A dead man not entered is dead twice. We counted them [the first interpreter renders this "we counted them"; the second insists the tally-word means "we mourned them"; the clerk was instructed to keep both]. And after One-Eye came the Chaos, when the dead walked. Set down the banners' teaching from those cycles: a people that knows its dead by name and number is a people whose dead lie still [the interpreters disagreed here at length; neither rendering satisfied them; the clerk has kept the shorter]. In the hundred cycles after One-Eye, the horde became the courts. A horde is a count in motion. A kingdom is a count that holds still.

Entry: your gates. Letia's gates are closed to us by law; the law is written in this book, with its defence. We keep the entry from our side. Orcs hanged at those gates for testing the law, in the last hundred cycles: 77. It is an entry, not a grievance. A grievance is a count you have let someone else keep.

Entry the last. You ask why the Khan answers your book. Books burn. We have burned some; that also is entered. Iron keeps, but iron stays home, and a count kept in one place is a count that can be lost. So enter the banners: one hundred. Under them, at the last census, 9,040,000, counted each. I am told this book keeps margins. We have lived in your margins fifteen hundred cycles. Put us in the page. That is the tally. When you want the rest of the count, send someone who can read it.

[Here the envoy ended; and the interpreters, for the one time in the hour, agreed without being asked.]

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Two consequences, one hook. First: One-Eye read the red moon correctly — it was the Observer, and soldier-stock feels the makers' sky in its blood; of the three powers that armed in 3026, only the Khan armed for the right war. Second: a people bred to answer orders keeps, somewhere under its law of scales, the shape of obedience — and the Tide reopened the doors it was first marched through. Gwart is a place, and places can be walked to again. If ever a banner finds an iron gate standing open with a count of one hundred cut above it — or if something on the far side resumes the count — decide quickly what the hundred banners are: a people, or an army that has been awaiting orders since the Maelstrom was a city. The envoy, be sure, has a number ready for either answer.

The Curator

Procedure to the end. The transcript was read back to the envoy by both interpreters together; she corrected two figures and no words, and set her seal — a rank of five notches — beside the clerk's. For those who would weigh her account against another of the same country, the Seat's library holds the tribute-courts' own chronicle, Ztoria di Krande Imperum — "History of the Great Empire, by Mugraash the scribe" — which I am assured is mostly numbers, and which agrees with the Annalist of Solanthya on every date and on nothing else. — the Curator

Colophon. Leaf XII·D (late-bound). Narrator: Marghuz, speaker-of-the-tally (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md conventions), with the interpreters Corvin Attaleo and Brill Halfweight (invented). Canon on the orc lands is thin, and this colophon says so at length on purpose. [CANON]: the Great Horde's conquest of Letia's east under Uglush the Supreme, 2015–2030, creating the Belligerent Kingdoms (canon/history.md, canon/factions.md); Ugrumuulsh One-Eye, Great Khan in 3026, arming at the red moon alongside the Theocrat and the Technocrat (canon/history.md, canon/characters.md — the two warlords kept distinct per HIS-16/META-10); Letia's exclusion laws (canon/geography.md); the joined Letian–Armorite arms against the Khans (canon/history.md); the Belligerent Kingdoms as a region of the Letian landmass (canon/geography.md); the orc chronicle Ztoria di Krande Imperum by Mugraash (canon/items.md); and, in the margins, the Gwart bio-soldier origin with RAC-1 = A honoured — the making true and the myth's kernel true, in a crooked mirror (canon/secret-the-great-lie.md, canon/peoples.md). [INVENTED] — roughly seven parts in ten of the leaf, more than any other in this book: Marghuz herself, her office, her court, and the Khan of the Fourth Banner; the hundred banners as an institution and their number (canon gives only "the Khans" under a Great Khan); the tally-law entire (the count, the scales, the summing of the throne, the strikings); the census and every figure spoken in the hall; the Muster and the Counter (a crooked-mirror creation-myth built to RAC-1's rule — no source records an orc creation-myth); the session, its protocol, both interpreters, and all their disagreements; the margins' "resumed count" hook. The wars, the dates, the two warlords, and the laws made against this people are canon; nearly everything said by them here is not, because no source has yet let them speak — which is this leaf's reason for existing.

XIII · THE PEOPLES OF YMIR

Whom You Will Meet on the Road, and How Not to Be Killed by Them

As taken down from the table-talk of Pips Quondam, Tal-Tanoth of no fixed address — traveller, guide, and dealer in goods of variable legality; by her own styling, "One Who Has Borrowed From Most of Them."

The Curator

Scholars have written on the peoples of Ymir. I did not want a scholar. I wanted somebody who has slept in a Vorskgen longhouse, haggled with a Sohleugir through a veil, and been thrown out of Ost-en-Galad at least once — and who would talk if fed. Mistress Quondam dictated this over nine suppers at Elendor; her facts hold far more often than her manner suggests, and her slanders are her own. As for her trade: she says "recovery of mislaid property," and I have counted the spoons. — the Curator

You want to know about the peoples of the world, and you have asked a cat who has robbed— travelled with most of them, so you have asked correctly. I will tell you what they look like, what they eat, what insults them, and what they will pay for, because that is what keeps you alive on a road; theology keeps you alive nowhere. We are deep in the long calm, the New Equilibrium, and the roads are as safe as they have ever been. Which is not very. Attend.

Humans first, because you cannot avoid them. Every climate, every coast, every tavern, talking. Most are Common Men — the great warm majority, farmers and clerks and soldiers, decent if fed and terrifying in crowds. Sprinkled through them are the Mixed Men, the cosmopolitan sort — Cartosa is thick with them — who carry, they will tell you at length, a trace of the old blood.

And the old blood is real: the Primeval stock — the Hyperboreans, to give the first people their proper name — whom legend says the powers made from Fire itself, which is why humans do everything at a sprint and regret it after. The fire-folk thinned their blood down the long ages into the Common Men you meet everywhere; the lines that kept the old spirit pure are few, ferocious, and — this is the part they all share — absolutely certain they are the last real ones.

Three you may actually meet: the Vorskgen, hill-clans of the Nortmaar and Letia's cold borders — ancestor-worshipping berserkers with wills like anchor-chain, generous past sense to a guest and unforgiving past sense to a thief (I once spent a winter with the Vorskgen; I still owe them a goat, and the goat is how I know the second fact); the Ophiriti of the southern continent — tall, very black-skinned, old-blooded above all in their nobility, who keep the first people's arcane wisdom not in books but in songs, grandmother to daughter, a thousand deep (their singer Nyema has a chapter-leaf of her own now; read it, it is better company than I am); and the Pureblood Leti, the oldest noble houses of Letia, pure-line Hyperboreans to a genealogist's satisfaction — you will know one instantly by the regal bearing and the magnetism, and by the fact that he already assumed you knew.

Beyond these, men come in regional flavours — the Norsi of Helgedad's Winter Forest with their totem-spirits, Hillmen, Woodmen, High Men — each sure the next valley over eats babies. Feed a human, admire his children, never touch his hat.

The Curator

For the Vorskgen and the Norsi at length, see the skald in Chapter VIII; for the desert peoples, Chapter XII; for the Gaerwath, Chapter XI — whose author would be displeased to share a page with what follows here, which is one reason I have allowed it. — the Curator

The Xebechani. Now I lower my voice, and you will lower yours. The eldest people: reptile-blooded, milk-pale, double-jointed, cool to the touch and cooler in conversation; they see the heat of your body, so do not bother lying to one about whether you are afraid. Four castes, fixed at birth by the scale-patterns on the back: the Xudra, massive and most reptilian, more than half of all their kind, never met abroad because they never leave; the Xidhe, the silk-voiced travelling caste, the only Xebechani most folk ever see, with a gaze like a hook in warm honey; the Xion, the priests; and the Xattva, the ethereal nobles and mages — meet one abroad and you have met an exile, so be gentle. They do not marry, they do not breed; when a Xebechani dies the body goes into the Pools, and from the Pools, by the rite called the Rebirth, comes a newborn. And the Pools — keep your voice down — are failing. Nobody living knows how they work; some children come out wrong now, or do not come out. The eldest people on Ymir is quietly, politely dying, and it does not speak of it, and neither will you at their table. Even I do not joke here. Trade honestly, keep your hands folded, and never steal from a temple with Pools in it. I know of what I speak.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The cat lowers her voice; lower yours. The Pools are not mysteries, they are machines — the bioscience of the engineers the Xebechani name the Ancients, who are the Adepts of these margins, still running after millennia with no one alive who can read them. The Rebirth is a recycler's liturgy. The race is not cursed, not dying of sin; it is out of maintenance. When the last Pool fouls, the eldest people ends — of a broken machine, and only these margins will know the cause of death.

Dwarves. Short, wide, bearded, and richer than you. The mountain kind are the pattern: smiths and engineers wanted in every court, builders of steam-engines, sole holders of the secret of Black Powder — the one secret on Ymir nobody has managed to steal, and believe me, professionals have tried. Their cousins the Plains Dwarves went down among the humans for work; call one Laak-nama-Khazalim near a mountain dwarf and you will learn it is an insult, and then what dwarven dentistry costs. In the Cartosan jungles live the Arawak — dark-skinned, violet-eyed, working stone and bone and never metal, braiding gems into their hair to count their deeds, preferring death to capture; their mountains hold Shald, the magical glass, and the expeditions that go after it feed the jungle. Dwarves are suspicious of my kind on principle, which is hurtful and, in fairness, evidence-based. And a word said softly, because the dwarves themselves say it softly or not at all: the folk of the Wald are not the pattern any more. When the golden dragon broke their halls at the start of the Machine War, the survivors went down — deeper than mining goes, and for generations of the dark. What came back up trades honestly and shuts doors politely and sees you before you knock, and their eyes take lamplight the way a cat's do, which from me is professional respect and from a dwarf is a tragedy. They call themselves Wald-folk still. Their mountain kin call them nothing at all, which is dwarvish for grief. Pay a dwarf on time. There is no second tip.

Elves. Beautiful, tragic, and here by accident — they came through the world-doors in the Demon Wars, and stayed. Get the shape of them right, because they mind: three kinds — the High, high and haughty; the Sylvan, most of them, wood-folk; the Grey, who are the Sea-elves and the only ones who will reliably drink with you — and, cutting clean across the kinds, three creeds. The Light Elves wait in Alfheim behind the walls of Ost-en-Galad for the Last Messenger to lead them home to Niethel. The Illuminati — the other two say Renouncers, quitters — live scattered among us and hold that the Sacred Realm is inside each elf; the pleasant ones. The Dark Elves of the Gaerwath say the Sacred Realm is Ymir, all of it, and the rest of us furniture. Any kind can hold any creed; you cannot tell a Dark Elf by his face, only by his filing system: Elar, true elves; Nemyel, failed elves, outcast and rightless; Kelvar, everything else that breathes — you, me. I once smuggled a Nemyel out of the Gaerwath in a salt-fish barrel: she had failed their child-trial and been set to scraping Arvo, the magic-dust, for demon-dealers, and she wept the whole crossing, quietly, so the fish would not inform. I did not charge her. Put that in your book: once, the cat did not charge. And the sharpest gossip on the continent: on Ymir the elves die. Two hundred years, more or less, and out — and the priests of Letia still argue whether they have souls at all, which the elves find as charming as you would.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Roll-call, since she has now toured most of the stock. The dwarves were shaped on Omalgat, servant-work of the Omungus. The orcs are the makers' bio-soldiers of Gwart, run wild for an age. The Little Folk were seeded from Seelie. Shaped — but not hollow: each people's myth keeps its kernel of truth, for the making was done to living stock, and Durkaam and the Ancestors remember truly in a crooked mirror. The elves alone among the incomers were never made at all: native to Tchyo, hunted off it, they fled here through the makers' own doors during the Demon Wars. Niethel is no paradise lost to sin. It is Tchyo, before the hunt.

The Little Folk, smallest and maybe rarest of the pure peoples, out of the Silver Isles — and yes, their capital is Elendor, where the Council of Peoples sits: the smallest folk host the biggest table, and they think that is very funny. Two sorts, not to be confused. The Fae — they call themselves Tylweth Teg — come in both sexes, whatever the ballads claim, stand under three feet, build toy-perfect cities deep in the woods, distrust the Tall Folk, are honestly baffled by dwarves, and hate everything that smells of the Maelstrom with a hatred out of all proportion to their size. The Gnomes are not their menfolk — say that twice and you have insulted two peoples with one sentence — but a separate race entirely, whose defining trait is wanting to be left alone, including by other Gnomes. A Gnome's hospitality is a door that opens once. Take the once.

My own people next, and I will be fair, which is more than they deserve. We Tanoth come in four branches — the registers used to say two, until somebody finally asked a Tanoth. The Tal-Tanoth — myself — small cat-folk of the cities, needing our eight hours of sleep and not much else, living cheerfully off the humans, who call us a plague when angry and a nuisance when fond. We are the world's finest thieves, guides, wanderers, and finders of mislaid property, and if Ymir had halflings we would have eaten their lunch anyway. Men are big dolts; we love them the way you love a purse left open. And when the big folk mislaid a whole town in the Tide years, it was the Cats who recovered it: we retook Hagelbarn, the grandest piece of mislaid property my people ever returned to its rightful owner — which was us.

Then there are the Ur-Tanoth, our great cousins — man-height and half again, furred like panther or tiger or lion by tribe, and despite the look, the gentlest souls on this world: shy nomad hunters, peerless workers of wood, gatherers of healing herbs and furs and rarities no city-hand could find. They shun the towns; only we Tal can deal between them and you, so mind the tariff. In Ophiur's savanna they are something more — a whole roaming kingdom, Tarkur, that follows a power they call the Storm across the grass; ask the singer in the leaf before this one, she tells it properly.

Third, the Kor-Tanoth — the wolf-cousins, or jackal where the land runs hot: our height or a hand more, heavier-built, furred head to foot, ranging the central plains of Cartosa and Ophiur in hunter-tribes. Not warlike — but territorial the way a locked door is territorial, and most of the blood spilled over them was spilled by fools who could not read a boundary-stone. Walk around. It is never far. And fourth, the Ugra-Tanoth, the bear-cousins, biggest and most alone of all my kin: three metres of them at the least, strong enough to lift a man one-handed, and gentle enough that they almost never do. They keep dens — caves, mostly, in Helgedad's ice or Ophiur's deep green — and unlike the rest of us they stay: a lifetime within a few dozen kilometres, tending the land around the den, thinking long thoughts, meeting even their own kind but once a year at the great gatherings. Provoke one and you will learn why nothing else in that valley does; but mostly the size does the arguing. Once in a rare while an Ugra leaves the den — grief, usually, or fire — and goes wandering, and those road-Ugra warm to company like stones warm to the sun: slowly, and then wonderfully. I travelled two seasons with one. Best watch I ever slept under.

And hear me, because I stop joking exactly twice in this chapter and this is the second: an Ur-Tanoth caged is an Ur-Tanoth dead. They fear the loss of the open sky past all reason; pen one and it sickens and dies in a season, every time. And still the beast-shows buy them, and still the "collectors" pay. I have opened three of those cages with my own hands, and I will open every one I find. Buy Ur-Tanoth work at honest prices. Never buy an Ur-Tanoth.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The cat jokes that men are her cousins. Do not laugh yet. In the makers' registers each world is listed with its stock — dwarf-lines for Omalgat, soldier-lines for Gwart, fairy-lines for Seelie — and nowhere in the catalogue is there an entry for Man, nor for the Tanoth, because Ymir's own line reads only: homeworld of the Tanoth. The Adepts made much and remade more, but men and the Tanoth-kin they never made. These were already underfoot — the native stock of the makers' own seat, the unmade peoples on the workshop's doorstep. Whether Man is the Tanoth-line grown tall and bare, or the cat-folk his elder cousins gone to fur, the registers do not trouble to say: they are entered as one column. Every made people's myth remembers its maker in a mask. Man has no maker to remember — which is why his myths are the muddiest, and his claim to this world the only one no forge can dispute.

The Sohleugir, the lizard-folk of the southern seas, whose element is the water and whose manners are better than yours. Their cities of stone and coral climb in terraces above the waves and below, and their deep capital, Ssas'Kher, has coral walls never once breached — kingdoms of men should blush. They surface to trade pearls and coral, seldom for politics: the fairest merchants and the most frightening soldiers I know. They know their faces alarm the warm-bloods — some believe it themselves, an old curse in the blood, poor dears — so they go veiled and hooded among us out of courtesy, if you please. Lift a Sohleugir's veil and you deserve whatever arrives. They despise the Gratar and all corrupt races, and they pay in pearls, promptly, which is the whole of my religion.

The Sarradhi of the Sadhir — lizardfolk also, and if you call them Sohleugir's cousins to either party, enjoy the silence. Desert matriarchs, "Those Who Walk the Cycle": mystics and elemental mages who rule their Sultanate by the creed of the eternal Wheel of Life, in which everything returns, including — they will note, watching you — debts. The grandmothers run everything; charm the grandmother or leave. Under them serve the hermit-crab folk of the dried inland sea, guides who can take you where nothing else can. The desert chapter of this book is theirs by right; I only add — bring a gift with a history. They can tell.

Now the jungle south, and your life may hang on the difference. The Nkishi, the wild orcs of the deep Cartosan green, are the bravest things on legs: from childhood they let beasts bite them and wear the scars as a book of deeds, and their Bokkor shamans treat with the ancestors and the dark forest gods, the Orisha — the worst of whom, the Mayombe, are not named after sundown, and I notice it is sundown. Meeting Nkishi: stand still, do not flinch, show your own scars if you have them. Courage is the coin; cowards are cargo. The M'Bantha goblins of the jungle-edge are another animal entirely: they believe humans keep their magic — they say "Cargo" — inside their everyday things, so they steal the things to steal the power, and go jingling with trinkets hammered from stolen kettles. People call them stupid. People are wrong at spear-range: they are patient, clever ambushers. My survival kit for M'Bantha country is a bright brass compass that has never pointed north in its life, carried where it can be admired. Rich in Cargo, poor in loss. You are welcome.

The half-peoples. Where peoples meet, children happen, and the learned count the documented crossings: sixteen in the old registers — fifteen since the Ugra got promoted, which the registers will admit any decade now. Each has a technical name and a common name — the common one is usually the cruel one. You know the everyday sorts, the Yafysh half-elves and Ghzaak half-orcs, common as coppers in the Qi-Long. Of the rest, the vivid ones: the Changeling, fairy-blood in a human line, lovely and short-lived as a rumour; the Hobgoblin, goblin-and-orc, which breeds true and drills in ranks, worse luck; the Dwergar, the "foul dwarf," goblin-and-dwarf, disowned by both grandmothers and harder than either; the Gremlin, kobold-and-fairy, small, spiteful, the finest lockwrights I have ever been professionally embarrassed by; the Kappa of the warm shallows, lizard-blood gone wrong, that will drown a swimmer for etiquette; the sad Fish-Man, of a human and one of the Rahab of the deep. There are more — Tanoth-crossed lines among them, not discussed at supper — and one rule worth ink: the half-peoples catch stones from both sides of their parentage, so if one serves you loyally, you have hired the most loyal thing on the road. It has nowhere else to be.

Last, the touched and the twisted, and one more flash of temper. The Marked are not a people but a lottery: children touched in the womb by the Maelstrom, born with deformities and, sometimes, gifts that make magisters go pale. Most villages smother them in the cradle. I have guided two of the Marked over the mountains, and both were kinder company than the villages that wanted them dead; you may have my opinion of the custom without charge. The Hybrids — the Maelstrom's older children — are peoples of a sort: Centaurs on the steppes, goat-legged Satyrs who will drink you into a ditch and lament you from its edge, Tritons in the green water, Giants in the high waste with old elemental fire in some lines. Treat each as you find it; the category is a fence humans built, and the fence leaks. But the Vulfen and the Gratar — the degenerate races, bred-down servants of the Sovereign Demons — I have met only at spearpoint, and so has everyone who has met them twice. If a Gratar offers you terms, count your fingers, then run.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Why do the wise fear the Marked, when the village priest fears only a crooked child? Because the Maelstrom is not weather. It is the wound over drowned Ragash, and what bleeds from it is the Corrupted Essence — and a Marked child is a place where the wound has signed its name inside the world's own stock. The villages kill what looks demon-touched and are wrong about the demon. The wise count differently: each Marked cradle is the seam grown thinner, the Tide arriving early, one child at a time. Killing them does not thicken the seam. Nothing does.

There. Every people on this list will feed you, and every one will kill you, and the whole craft of the road is knowing which door you knocked on. Learn their food, their insults, and their prices, in that order. And if a small cat-shaped shadow shares your fire some night and asks what that shiny thing is — say it is Cargo, and hold it tighter.

The Curator

The reader will weigh an account of the world's peoples whose author confesses, within it, to smuggling, cage-breaking, and a standing debt of one (1) goat. I have weighed it. I trust her further than several archbishops of my acquaintance, and I have settled the goat from the Compendium's purse, against her next nine suppers. — the Curator

Colophon. Chapter XIII. XIV · A HUNTSMAN'S BOOK OF BEASTS

Of the beasts of Ymir, honest and otherwise; their sign, their seasons, their worth, and the ones you must not hunt

Being the field-book of Weck of the Long Road, huntsman-ranger sworn to Therìos, kept over forty years of walking, and bought by the Curator for the price of two good knives.

The Curator

The humblest book in the Compendium, and the cheapest: two good knives, from a trader in Wald who could not read it. Of Weck I know only what the pages tell — he walked most of the world, served the Hunter god, and was still alive when the writing stops. My colleagues catalogue creatures; this man survived them, which is the sounder scholarship. I have kept his manners. — the Curator

I write what I have seen, or what men I would trust at my back have seen. Nothing else. Therìos asks three things of a hunter: kill clean, waste nothing, know what you are killing. This book is the third thing. Read it before you need it.

HONEST BEASTS

Daretas. Southern highlands, Cartosa. Six legs, ox-sized, plated like an armadillo. Sign: broad prints in double rows, dust-wallows. Placid; carries half its weight up a scree slope and complains less than you do. Worth: meat tender as veal, hide that turns a sword — the Cartosans make shields of it. An honest animal. Treat it honestly.

Reptoids. Horse-sized riding reptiles, farmed across Cartosa; most southern leather is Reptoid. Steadier than horses near fire. Sign, where feral: claw-drag in mud, sunning-flats worn smooth. Worth: hide, and a live one broken to saddle is worth three dead.

The northern game. Elk, red deer, boar, hare, the grouse of the pine country. I will not insult them with entries. Take the old and the weak, leave the mothers in spring, thank the Hunter, and the forest will feed your grandchildren.

MAELSTROM-TOUCHED

Storms come off the world's middle that are not weather. What they touch, changes.

Yarhim. Crystal crabs of the Sadhir and Cartosan coasts, blue shell like sea-glass. The small ones span a man's arms. The great ones run to ten metres, and I have seen one open a fishing boat the way you open a mussel. Sign: furrows up from the tide-line, a wet-glass glitter at dusk. Hunt the small ones from land, with barbed lines and patience. Do not hunt the great ones. Worth: the shell — armourers pay a farm for a whole one. Legendary harness comes of it. So do widows.

Hybrids. The general name for what the Maelstrom's storms make of living things. No two alike. Two rules keep you alive: count the joints — too many, leave. Look at the eyes — wrong number, or wrong places, leave. Worth: nothing is worth it.

The Marked. Some fool asked me to put them in this book, and I will say this once: they are people, born of women, and they go in no bestiary of mine.

The Curator

I have let that sentence stand exactly as written, and commend it to certain rival compendia. For the Marked, see the chapter on the Peoples. — the Curator

DESERT KINDS — the Sadhir and the Great Erg

Glass-Stalkers. Crystal scorpions, near invisible. You will not see one; you will see where the sand dimples and the heat-shimmer walks against the wind. Travel at midday when their shadows betray them. Worth: the claws, to lens-grinders and to fools.

Mirajids. Things born of the heat haze — elemental, not flesh. They wear the shapes of water, lost friends, shade. Loose no arrow; you cannot skin a lie. Walk on, and answer nothing that speaks out of the shimmer.

Cindervine Serpents. Burrowers. They sleep under the hardpan and come up in the storms, when the moving sand hides their sign. In a desert storm, stand on rock or stand on nothing.

Tzil Beetles. Salt beetles, thumb-sized, harvested by the desert folk. Worth: alchemists buy them dried, by weight. An honest little trade — the desert has few.

Zaar'Qrak. In the Erg tongue, Rending Death, and the Erg tongue does not exaggerate. Taller than any man, six-limbed, black-bristled, plated in bone; it sees in full dark as you see at noon. It leaps out of the night into a caravan's middle, kills, and is gone with a body before the guards turn. Solitary, the Hunter be thanked; the worst thing in the Erg is a mother with cubs. Do not track it. Do not avenge anyone. Leave the country.

The Shrikes of the White Choir. Not beasts. Leavings — dead things kept walking as carrion spirits, singing over the dunes in white rags. Necromancy. Whoever first sang the Choir up broke all three of the Hunter's laws at once and should have been given to the Zaar'Qrak alive. Stop your ears and pass by dark.

COLD KINDS — Helgedad and the north

Troll. They infest the wild lands beyond the last farms. Sign: split game bones, dung full of hair, a smell like a flooded grave. Strong past reason, stupid past belief, and neither will comfort you at arm's length. Fire is your friend; so is daylight; so are eleven other men. Worth: the Nortmaar pay bounty by the head. Earn it in company.

The Great Eagles. Of the Mountains of Mist. Write this in your own book: they are allies, not game. They are wiser than half the men I have guided, and they hold those passes against worse than trolls. Never draw on one. Never go near the nests. If one stoops low over you and cries, something is on your trail. Thank it, and check.

The Frozen Waste. Goblins in the ruins, kobolds under them, and a third kind of track I found one grey morning — long, wrong, and melted into the ice as if the foot had been hot. I never stayed to learn what makes it. In the Waste, that is not cowardice. That is craft.

STRANGE KINDS

Fungalids. Fungoid flyers — a mouthless grey toad on dragonfly wings, walking on two barbed tentacles. Damp caves, rotting forest. The skin weeps acid; the claws carry a poison that stops your limbs while it eats; and they mend as fast as you cut — blades are an argument with mud. Oil and fire, from upwind, or leave them their cave. Worth: alchemists want the acid glands. Charge them accordingly.

Slashquark. Green pigs, wrinkle-necked, with a sucker mouth and two rows of fangs. Almost comical in the ferns. Then the neck goes out three metres, the first fangs hold you, and the second row rakes back and strips the flesh off the bone. They are named for the sound the victim makes. I will not write it plainer. Kill from beyond neck-reach or not at all.

Magres. Seven metres of grey, hairy, scaly wrong: a mouth of a thousand small teeth, piggish eyes, useless little hands, dozens of small feet like a nightmare's caterpillar. The learned say they were kobolds once, warped by the Chaos. The joke: it is a coward. Where the joke ends: when it panics — and everything makes it panic — it flees over whatever stands in the way. Farms. Herds. Companies of soldiers. Never wound one, never startle one uphill of a village. Move it as you would move a fire: from far off, toward nothing you love.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

"Warped by the Chaos," the learned say, and never ask what the Chaos is. It is the Maelstrom. The wound over drowned Ragash has bled a mutating stain into the world for three and a half thousand years — the Marked at birth, the Hybrids in the storms' path, and, where the bleed pooled long ago in the deep hills, the kobolds that swelled into Magres. One stain, at different distances from the wound. The huntsman's "Maelstrom-touched" and his "strange kinds" are the same chapter, and he half knows it.

The Tangle. The forest with the Heart of Chaos grown at its middle — nineteen centuries of it now, say the learned — and here is the whole of my entry: I do not enter the Tangle, and I have no bounty high enough to change that.

MADE THINGS

A thing with no smell has no honesty. I distrust every entry below.

Automata and golems. Armora builds its servants of brass and jar-lightning; the Dark Elves bind spirits into stone. Not game — no blood, no fear, no season. Sign: oil-drip, tracks in perfect stride that never wander, no scat, no browse. Nothing that walks and does not eat is walking for a good reason.

The war-mechs of Armora. Bigger, armed, and obedient, which is the worst of it — a beast serves its belly, and a belly can be predicted. These serve whoever holds the writ. I have scouted past them; I would rather pass a troll bridge. Stay out of the smoke.

Derelict. A dead war-construct that did not stay dead. Rusted armour walking, joints weeping oil like a wound, cold that climbs into your chest, and under the creak of it — voices. Battle-dead souls, welded in by necromancy, making war on everything that breathes. Weapons are a slow way to die near it. Burn it, then burn the ashes. Then tell a priest where the fire was.

Zortos. Made, but made of meat — the Cartosan mage-guild's hunting stock, bred from goblins and worse by a rite they do not publish. Waist-high, fox-headed, silver-blue fur that looks wet and is. Claws and fangs carry venom; the nose beats any hound I have run; they swim like otters, breathe water like air, and see by day, dark, and the heat of your blood. Sign: none. That is the sign. If the Blue Mantles have set Zortos on a man, the kindest thing you can do for him is not be standing near him. They ignore earth and ice, and hate fire and clean light — a torch buys you steps, not safety. I do not hunt what was built to hunt. Professional courtesy.

SPIRITS AND THE SUMMONED

I hunt none of these. I write them so you will know when to stop hunting and start praying.

Spirits. Everywhere, the way wind is everywhere. Mostly harmless; the wellspring of magic. But a spirit that stays too long in the world goes sour — Raging, the learned call it — and a raging spirit is weather with a grudge. Cold hollows where birds will not sing: camp elsewhere.

Elementals. From wisps you could cup in two hands, up rank by rank — servants, mounts, guardians, giants, the true ones — to the Elemental Lords, one lord to each element. The small ones run a mage's errands. The great ones are the weather taking a personal interest. There is no hunting entry for a thing that is also the river; make your grandmother's offering and cross somewhere else.

Djinn. Made things — a spirit given substance and fixed to a place, an object, or a task, so it cannot sour. Jann to Marid, weakest to greatest. Mannerly, if you are. They keep their word to the letter, and only to the letter. Say nothing near one that you would not sign.

Angels. Servants of the Good, they say, in three shining spheres. Never seen one. Keep it so. Men who see angels are standing where the war of the gods is being fought, and no hide from that country was ever worth the tanning.

The Night-Ones. Once. Forty years on the roads, and once. A man-shape of standing shadow, edges that would not hold still, two green lamps for eyes, watching me across my own fire — and my fire threw no light on it. It did not touch me. It was counting something; I do not know what. They walk by night or shade — sun vexes them but does not bite — and the shadows move to suit them. Those who claim to have spoken with one say they come from a place they call only Night, or Shadow, and are here about a Task they will not name, and that each talks as if it were all of them — one water in many jugs. I have no advice. I moved my camp at dawn, and the prints it left were mine.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The huntsman met one and was inventoried; be glad his ledger balanced. The Night-Ones' "Night" has a name in the makers' catalogue: Naxtis, the dark world of the old empire, whence the light-drinking Umbra is carried. They are that world's stock as the dwarves are Omalgat's — and alone among the made peoples they still speak of their Task in the present tense, as if the makers' last instruction were not yet complete. No one has established what the instruction was. Whoever does will want to sit down first.

Demons. Spirits corrupted whole and sworn to the Evil. Here they cannot well hide what they are — the greater the demon, the worse the shape fits. The learned rank them in six Circles, weakest first. A first-Circle thing dies to hard men, good spears, and a priest who does not run. By the fourth, you burn the village to save the parish. Of the sixth: I have seen ground where one stood, years after, and nothing grows there and nothing ever will. Above the six they say beyond the Circles, and mean the Sovereigns.

The Sovereign Demons. Nineteen names in the old lists, each with a title and a mark — the black dragon of Asmodeus, Lord of Tyranny; the spiderweb of Xardas, called the Puppeteer; the mask of Masak, Lord of Illusions; the coin, the dagger, the bull's skull, the rest. I list them as legend; legend keeps farmboys from drawing the marks for a dare. Most are dead. Ea of the Abysses was unmade long ago, Saxul of the Fevers after him, and when their prison cracked in the age of the Tide, the greater part of the rest died in the breaking — so the priests swear, and for once I hope they are right. Three walk: Asmodeus. Xardas. Masak. No sign to read, no season, no worth. If you meet one, your book is done. Give this one to someone who lived.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The prison is the Adepts' oldest cell — the pocket of Corrupted Essence men call Inferno — and its law was always: none cross unless summoned. The Tide broke that law once. When the isolation shattered, the crack hurled the Sovereigns at the crossing unsummoned, and the crossing killed most of them; a single exception, not a new rule, and the cell resealed around the lesser legions. The ledger now: the Circles still cross only by summoning; the dead Sovereigns are dead — save that a Sovereign only truly dies when its essence is unmade, and no one audited the essences — and three came through whole. Asmodeus, Xardas, and Masak have walked Ymir five centuries, and demons that cannot hide what they are have not been seen. Consider what that says about where they are standing.

DRAGONS LAST

Drakes, wyverns, and the lesser serpents. Dragonkind, but mortal about it. Huntable. Barely. A wyvern is winged malice with a sting; a drake is a forge with legs. Take them at the nest-cliff in moult, with ballistae, poisoned carcasses, and more men than your pride wants. Worth: hide, teeth, and glands enough to pay off a farm — fitting, as the beast has usually eaten one.

The True Dragons. There are twenty. This is not a hunting entry. This is a warning.

I have tracked everything that walks, and forty years taught me this: the True Dragons do not track like animals. An animal wants food, a mate, its own ground, and you can read all three in the mud. A True Dragon wants something I have no word for. They watch. Nothing honest watches like that. Gwalgamaur the Golden sleeps in Wald, and the Young Kingdoms pay him the courtesy of silence. Nihoggr the Solitary lies under the Helgedad ice, and the Nortmaar do not fish certain lakes. In the east the Qi-Long kneel to six of them as Dragon-Gods, gods in dragon flesh — and I will not argue the word. Above them all the eastern priests set Ouroboros, the True Dragon-God, who in the age of the Tide rose and unmade Alfheim entire. A country. In days.

I crossed a valley below Wald where the Golden One had once been thorough. Sixty years before, they told me. The riverbed stone was glass; the soil, ash under a hand's-width of new dirt; no tree older than the fire. There was game sign at the rim and none — none — below: sixty years, and the animals still would not go down. An old man on the ridge farm had seen it as a boy; he spoke of it only in a whisper, as if the sky kept accounts. Learn from the beasts, as a hunter should. Stay out of the valley.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The huntsman's instinct is exactly right: the True Dragons are not animals. They are the Adepts' living superweapons — twenty engineered custodians set to guard Ymir's secrets, charged to watch for any people climbing too near the Truth, and to burn the ladder. That is the wanting he has no word for. Most drifted from the charge into agendas of their own; only the six Dragon-Gods of Qi-Long kept the faith. Above loyalist and rogue alike stands Ouroboros — the apex of the design, "Leviathan" his element, not a twenty-first dragon — and Alfheim died because the wardens' king judged that someone there had climbed too high. When a True Dragon watches you, an old protocol is deciding whether you are prey. Prey is the better outcome.

That is the book. If a beast is not in it, I did not meet it — or I met it, and it is why the writing stops. Kill clean. Waste nothing. Know what you are killing. Therìos walk the Long Road with you.

Colophon. Chapter XIV. Narrator: Weck of the Long Road, huntsman of Therìos (invented framing voice; NARRATORS.md — the two-knives provenance, the burned valley of Wald, and the hunting-craft of sign, season, and price are [INVENTED] colour). Creatures and rosters from canon/bestiary.md (all fauna and constructs; the Sovereign Demons and their post-Tide status; the twenty True Dragons, Gwalgamaur, Nihoggr, the Dragon-Gods, Ouroboros/Alfheim) and canon/cosmology.md (the Mark and the Hybrids; Spirits, Elementals to the Elemental Lords, Djinn, Angels, the six Circles); the Tangle and the Heart of Chaos at its centre (1600 d.M.) from canon/geography.md + canon/history.md — the huntsman's refusal to enter is [INVENTED] colour; margins from canon/secret-the-great-lie.md (Adept-engineered dragon-custodians; the demon prison; the Maelstrom as the Chaos-bleed). Dragon roster per sources/extracted/wiki/creatures-dragons.md. Decisions applied: MAG-2 (one demon hierarchy — Sovereigns Beyond the Circles over the six Circles), MAG-12/MAG-13 (Ouroboros above the loyal/rogue split; "Leviathan" his type, not a twenty-first dragon), MAG-17 (the Magres' "Chaos" = the Maelstrom's mutation), MAG-18 (the prison-crack as a mass involuntary manifestation — the stated exception to "none cross unsummoned").

XIV·B · FIGURES OF RENOWN

being, in Part One, extracts of the Register of Figures of Renown kept by the Letian Inquisition, with the Verdicts of the Holy Office; and in Part Two, the Companies of the Turning Age, wherein the narrators of this book name the adventurers of their own lands

As compiled for the use of the Inquisition of the Clericate of Mithra by Frater Remigius Falx, Canon-Historian of the Holy Office, in the registry at Solanthya, in the year of the Maelstrom 3518, under the present High Theocrat, whom Mithra preserve.

The Curator

The Inquisition of Letia keeps a register of the world's renowned, so that its examiners may know by whom the folk swear before hearing how they pray. It is recopied each generation and the superseded copy burned; the under-censor charged with the last burning retired instead — suddenly, and to Cartosa — and this is the copy he did not burn, bought at two removes. I have bound it behind the huntsman's book of beasts, it being a bestiary of another kind. One caution before the verdicts, reader: they are measurements of piety, not of truth, and the instrument is calibrated at Solanthya. — the Curator

PART ONE · THE REGISTER OF THE HOLY OFFICE

the renowned of the ancient and settled world, as the Letian Inquisition files them

To the Reverend Fathers Examiners; and to my successor upon this shelf, whomever Mithra afflicts with the appointment.

Renown is a road, and roads carry pilgrims or plague; the Office must know the traffic. The common man swears by these names, christens his sons with them, and takes doctrine from a dead hero sooner than from a living homilist. Hence this Register: first what the charters attest; then what common report adds, marked as report; last the Verdict of the Office, which is the purpose of the exercise. And let my successor mark it early: the Office burns less than the world believes. The lowest shelves are locked, not empty. What follows is the shelf we show. The entries stand in the order of their floruit, so far as the calendars of the fallen world allow; where the elves are concerned no order was obtainable, and I have ceased to ask.

I. Topramesk, called the Yellow, and by the charitable the Mad — archmage of the last Adepts; the Ancient Days. The first name in the Register and the oldest file in the house. He led the remnant of the sorcerers' empire into the Academy at the heart of fallen Ragash and wrought the ritual that ended the Demon Wars — and the land with them: where the Splendent City stood, the Maelstrom turns to this day. The doctors read the Catastrophe as judgement upon pride; the Office sees no cause to distinguish the sentence from the culprit's handiwork. Report in the east will have it that he is not done dying. Report is not evidence. Verdict of the Office: BLASPHEMER OF THE FIRST MAGNITUDE; author, under Heaven, of the world's wound. He predates the Office and every court but one. The file remains open, on principle.

II. Inanna, called the Splendent — queen of the Light Elves; her date beyond our reckoning.
