The legendary queen of Alfheim, who in the deeps of elven time drove the demon-binding Dark Elves from that realm into their Gaerwath exile, where they remain, and remain a lesson. Her line ruled the Shining City until the Serpent unmade it in the Tide, and a queen of her blood replants the splinter now. The exiles curse her house and lay their fall from paradise upon her ancestor — which is, the Office observes, what exiles always say. Verdict of the Office: BEYOND THE WRIT — an elf, and no subject of Mithra's law; her war upon the demonolaters is nonetheless entered with an approval the Office is not, strictly, entitled to feel.

III. Aslund, called the Swordless — legionary of the Tenth; Helgedad, in the Empire's day. Builder of the wall that bears his name: four hundred kilometres of stone from sea to sea across the Kelmark, raised against the northern barbarians while Helgedad was yet of the Empire — the year itself the northern rolls do not condescend to give. The north says he laid his own sword in the foundation-course, whence the byname. The Office has no doctrine of buried swords; it has a doctrine of works, and the Wall of Aslund is the largest single prayer in the world's north. Verdict of the Office: PIOUS, after the frontier fashion; a wall being a psalm in stone, he is entered among the devout — the more readily as he can no longer be examined on particulars.

IV. Uglush, called the Supreme — warlord of the Great Orc Horde; 2015–2030 d.M. The scourge of the east. In fifteen years of fire his Horde carved from the Empire's living body the realms now called Belligerent, where the Khans squabble upon Letian bones. Of his end the chronicles are silent, orcs keeping no registry that Mithra would audit. Anathema was pronounced while the marches still burned and is renewed at every synod since; my predecessors note, with the honesty this Register requires, that the anathema has to date recovered no province. Verdict of the Office: BLASPHEMER; ANATHEMA, pronounced and perpetually renewed. See the caution at entry X below, and let no novice merge the files.

V. Morannagul, styled the Eternal — unifier of the Gaerwath; 2550 d.M. to this present writing. In 2550 a young Dark-Elf lord unified the Gaerwath; in 3518 the same lord rules it still, unaged, over an empire that keeps our kind for slaves and binds demons for statecraft. This registry holds nine hundred years of findings against him; he has outlived every hand that wrote them, and the anathema, renewed by Theocrat after Theocrat, he is nowhere reported to have noticed. Verdict of the Office: DEMONOLATER; ANATHEMA, many times renewed and demonstrably not inconvenienced. The Office persists. So, regrettably, does he.

VI. Valdis Nemertine — last heir of the throne of Letia; fell 2563 d.M. In that year the Sovereign Demon Saxul, Lord of All Fevers and Pestilence, manifested in the imperial capital, and at the Battle of the Broken Land was destroyed — the second of his kind ever slain — Valdis giving her life in the destroying. The crown ascended with her; the crozier, in God's season, took up what the crown had laid down. Her cause of sainthood is the largest file in this registry. Of the Sword of Valdis: three shrines display a sword apiece; the Office has authenticated none and — the pilgrims being devout and the offerings regular — has closed none. Verdict of the Office: MARTYRDOM CONFIRMED; SAINTHOOD PENDING, these nine hundred and fifty years. Heaven is not slow. Heaven is thorough.

VII. The house of Carrillo — princes of Cartosa; 1906 d.M. to this present. The princely line of the daughter-colony since a governor was raised to Prince in 1906. Of their deeds: Rodriguez XI reduced Meridian and annexed it in the War of the Red Sea, 2817–2830; Alberto V took the throne in the Year of the Black Sun, 3017, having — so every southern telling insists — held his own palace stair against the Black Dawn. Cartosa's heralds cannot even agree in which years the thirteenth Rodriguez reigned and the fifth Alberto succeeded; the Office files both accounts and trusts neither. The house tithes chiefly to Orum, invokes Mithra at coronations, and remits to Solanthya with commendable punctuality. Verdict of the Office: LAX, the line entire — the sea-god at table, the sun-god on feast-days; funds however accepted.

VIII. Berthrant Vaelaer — champion; Helgedad, 3015–3020 d.M. When the army of the Wolves took Nortislong in a single day and was as suddenly gone, it was Berthrant Vaelaer who would not let the leavings live: five years he hunted the remnant, sworn to scour Evil from that island root and stone, with a muster at his back that the north still sings. Helgedad left the Empire's fold in 2600, and its creeds are Thurms's and colder; the man himself is not recorded to have spared one servant of the Dark in five winters, which is more orthodoxy than several archbishops have shown. Verdict of the Office: SCHISMATIC BY GEOGRAPHY; RIGHTEOUS BY WORKS. Entered as PIOUS, if lamentably armed, and lamentably elsewhere.

IX. Tobias — captain of the Black Dawn; Cartosa, 3017 d.M. Master of the cult called the Black Dawn — Ahriman's, and the Sovereign Demons', its seal a sun eclipsed — and author of the failed coup that gave the Year of the Black Sun its name. The coup broke; the cult burned. The Office's experience of burned cults may be summarised: cults do not burn, they cure, like ill-cut timber. No body was produced, and the Register does not close a demonolater's file on the strength of smoke. Verdict of the Office: DEMONOLATER; UNREPENTANT, twice invited. The invitations stand cancelled. The watch does not.

X. Ugrumuulsh, called One-Eye — Great Khan of the orcs; fl. 3026 d.M. Great Khan of the Belligerent Kingdoms in the year the red moon rose, when he read the omen as the Theocrat and the Technocrat read it, and armed as they armed — the single recorded instance of doctrinal agreement between Solanthya and an orc. CAUTION FOR EXAMINERS, in my own hand and underscored: this is not Uglush the Supreme, who burned the east a thousand years before him. Two warlords; two files; one smell. The files have been merged three times by novices and unmerged three times by me. Verdict of the Office: BLASPHEMER, in his own right and not his predecessor's. The distinction is doctrine. See that it stays seen.

XI. Of the Heresiarch of Ulan-Tang — the name struck from this and every roll, by decree; fl. 3026 d.M. He was ours. The Register is commanded to forget him and commanded to warn of him, and I have written this entry seven times to make it do both. ████████ ██████ was received into the Office in ████; served as inquisitor, commendations twice entered and twice struck; fell — the manner of his falling is struck; and fled. And in 3026 d.M., before the ancient portal at Ulan-Tang, being by then a lich and a cultist of the outer dark, he wrought the abomination that drew down upon the world the red moon that owned no calendar — the heathens have a name for it, which I will not copy. That moon was two hundred years and more in the withdrawing. The Theocrat of that age struck his name from the rolls of the Clericate; the striking binds this Register, and me. Verdict of the Office: HERESIARCH; DEGRADED; STRUCK. The blank is the sentence, and the sentence is being served.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The struck name was Heinrich Kramer, inquisitor of this same Office, and the striking was tidier than the truth. Nothing at Ulan-Tang is recorded destroyed: a lich ends at its phylactery, and no account claims one was found. His workings remain around a gate that has already admitted a god's attention once. And on the lowest shelf — locked, not empty — proscribed pages give a name for what the lich intends to become, M'Raker, and describe a war the world has not yet had: dead armies, Armora besieged, the gods barred from the sky. Prophecy, forgery, or a nightmare with good handwriting — no other record says. If you open that door at your table, know what you are arming.

XII. Obnubia — a seer; the years of the Tide. A seer of the Tide's eve, whose sight — common report insists, and sober captains confirm — found true where the Obscuring should have blinded her. Doctrine is settled that no honest divination reaches far under the Maelstrom; Obnubia's did not trouble to be dishonest, merely correct. The Register records, because it must, that the Office itself put questions to her twice in that generation. The answers are filed on the lowest shelf, and both came true, which is why the shelf is the lowest. Verdict of the Office: NEITHER PIOUS NOR IMPIOUS BUT INCONVENIENT; not to be consulted; consulted twice; see the previous clause, and do not see it again.

XIII. Tofango — axeman; bearer of Oblivion; the years of the Tide. The greatest axeman of his age, and the saddest file in the house. In his hands was Oblivion, a two-handed axe of purest Demon Steel, within which a mighty demon lies imbued and trapped; the thing's will gnawed him by inches — madness, loss of governance, deeds his companions would not repeat under seal — until the man who had been the terror of the dark went up a remote mountain alone and ended his days a hermit upon it. The body was found. The axe was not. The colleges teach his case in a southern doctor's words: corruption is of the instrument and the handling, not of the man. Our theologians disputed forty years whether such affliction is sin, and concluded, for once, in mercy. Verdict of the Office: AFFLICTED, NOT APOSTATE; his madness absolved, his axe not. The axe being at large, the mercy is under review.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The verdict is kinder than the Office knows. Oblivion was forged at Hagelbarn by the alchemist Hans Schlecht, vowed to Ahriman, its demon set to wake as the bearer's corruption deepened — and the commission was placed, and paid, by an inquisitor of this very Office: the name struck two entries above. Built as a lure for the paladin Urikal, it found Tofango instead; the register that absolves him was written in the house that ordered the axe. It is on a mountain somewhere still, patient, waiting for hands.

XIV. Surya — bearer of the Masamune; the Machines' war, from 3025 d.M. An easterner, named among the heroes of the great war of the Young Kingdoms, and bearer of the blade the east calls the Masamune — which the doctors of magic identify with Meletskar, one of the six Relics of the histories. The bearer is reported brave, temperate, and wholly unbaptised. Verdict of the Office: PAGAN; INSTRUMENT, notwithstanding, OF PROVIDENCE. The Office does not canonise foreigners, and declines to say how near it came.

XV. Isidoro VonGraven — of Armora; the Machines' war. A hero of the same war, of the Armorite line VonGraven — and here the clerk's pen must slow: VonGraven, not VonGryffen; the ducal house is the other; those files too have been married and annulled twice. Of Isidoro the attested part is valour. The reported part is stranger: that he was not born but made, in the likeness of the lord Sigurd VonGraven before him; and that something not himself sometimes looked out of him, which report calls a spirit of light and the Office declines to call anything on hearsay. Verdict of the Office: SCHISMATIC (Armorite); also, per report, TWICE-MADE. A man may be made twice; he is christened once; the Office was consulted on neither occasion, and remembers.

XVI. Leonard — adversary of the Machines' war. The great adversary of that war after the dragon itself: a lord among the undead — a vampire, the sober accounts agree — who moved behind the Black Sorcerers and behind worse. He was destroyed. The Office would enjoy ending the entry there. But reports of his destruction have been filed more than once, which is once more than the arithmetic of destruction allows. Verdict of the Office: DAMNED; DESTROYED, per the accounts; the accounts, per the Office, PENDING. The file is chained open.

XVII. Vespertina VonGryffen, the Peacemaker — Grand Duchess of Armora; after the Tide. Successor to Leonius, who brought the wayward daughter back to Letia's side against the Khans — a treaty of equals, her chancery insists; a return, our pulpits prefer; the orcs, who were the occasion, were not consulted on the wording. Her line rules Armora still, and its church remains its own, unrepented. The Office notes that she kept every article of the treaty, attended the joint thanksgiving, and declined — thrice, and graciously — to convert. Verdict of the Office: SCHISMATIC, LAMENTABLY; FAITHFUL TO HER WORD, UNDENIABLY. The Office prays for her house, accepts her garrisons, and keeps the file in the drawer marked BOTH.

XVIII. Roberto Rodrigo, called the Armorite — pirate; the southern seas. The pirate legend of the Cartosan waters: northern-named, southern-crewed, and by every account unhanged. Two lives of him circulate — The Legend, which is affectionate, and The True History, compiled by a bard of Cartosa, which is meticulous and reads, examiners will note, less like a biography than like a bestiary. The Office draws no conclusion in the body of a register, and underlines the observation. A life of perfect laxity in either telling: no mass, no tithe, no oath unbroken — save, both lives agree, his word once given over cards. Verdict of the Office: PIRATE; APOSTATE; LAX BEYOND THE REACH OF EXAMPLE; condemned in absentia, the absentia persisting. If his second biographer is right, not baptisable in any case, which would simplify the file wonderfully.

XIX. Dwayne Soylen, called Saint Dwayne the Builder — paladin of Thurms. A paladin of the thunder-god who raised temples the way other soldiers raise camps, and whom the south has canonised by acclamation — there is a written Life, and there are shrines — without reference to any calendar of ours. The Office has ruled three times that there is no Saint Dwayne; the shrines have been rebuilt three times. The folk bring their roof-beams to be blessed there, and the roofs, my correspondents report with regret, hold. Verdict of the Office: SAINT BY ACCLAMATION, IN A COMMUNION NOT OURS; which is to say, NOT A SAINT; see the previous clause. Suppression pending; funding for suppression pending longer.

XX. The Two of the Tide — names wanting; Year 0 of the Age of the Tide. And last, the entry that shames the registry. Two adventurers, in the years about 3025 to 3027, took up the six Divine Relics and stripped the seals from the Runes of Power; the isolation of the Maelstrom shattered, every door of the ancients opened, and this age began. Every consequence in the world's ledger since descends from two pairs of hands, and no record on Ymir yields the names. The accounts of those years name heroes and monsters in abundance — bearers, companions, captains, the damned — and at the hinge itself go silent, as if the ink declined. My predecessor ruled a blank space for the names, and his predecessor before him. I have measured the space. I have left it. It is the only humility this Register contains, and it was not voluntary. Verdict of the Office: VERDICT SUSPENDED, FOR WANT OF AN ACCUSED. The deed is condemned and absolved daily, in every pulpit, according to the preacher. The Office waits for a defendant. It has waited four hundred and ninety years. The Office is patient.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The Office may be waiting for a defendant it has already tried. Strip the accounts to what they attest, and the candidates for the two names reduce to these: the company that gathered the six Relics — whose bearers this register names without knowing it — or the vampire Leonard, his file chained open above; or one of each. No record decides, and each answer poisons a different sermon. The blank the frater measured is a door left open at the exact centre of the age; whoever walks two names through it decides what the era was for. No prize in this book is larger.

Here end the extracts. My successor will add names; the world does not stop minting them. Let him keep the files distinct, the verdicts dry, and the lowest shelf locked; and let him remember that our duty is not to admire the light of renown but to record what it consumes. Mithra keep the Register; the Register, God willing, will help keep Mithra's house.

The Curator

The attentive reader will have noticed that the Office's writ ends exactly where its approval begins, and its files open exactly where its fears do. Read the verdicts, then, as I warned: piety, in Letian units. But read the blank at the register's end twice. A house kept an empty space ruled and waiting for five hundred years rather than guess at two names; nothing in this book says better how history feels from inside an institution that intends to outlast it. — the Curator

❧

PART TWO · THE COMPANIES OF THE TURNING AGE

being an account of the adventuring bands whose hands turned the age, gathered by the Curator from the narrators of this book

The Curator

The Curator resumes. Here Frater Falx's extracts end, and here I must confess the register's quiet failure — which is not the frater's fault, but his office's. The Inquisition of Letia keeps the renowned that Letia can see: the ancient, the sainted, the anathematised, the safely dead. But the age we live in was not turned by the safely dead. It was turned, in the space of a single troubled decade about the Tide, by companies — bands of the living and the reckless, who gathered the Relics, opened the gate at Ulan-Tang, broke a fever-demon, sailed after a pirate's ghost — and of these the frater's register holds only splinters: a bearer here, a heresiarch there, and at the hinge of the whole age a ruled blank space.

So I did what a Letian register could not. I asked the narrators of this book — the ones you have already learned to read — to name the companies of their own countries, each in the voice you know them by; and where the Office deigned to rule upon one of these figures, I have let Frater Falx's verdict stand beside the telling, that you may watch renown measured twice over: once by those who loved it, and once by those who policed it. What follows is not tidy. Companies are not tidy. But read here, and you will see the turning age from the inside of the hands that turned it. — the Curator

§ The Company of the Gate — northern Letia to Ulan-Tang, 3026 d.M.

Frater Remigius Falx, Canon-Historian of the Holy Office, continuing.

The Curator bids me name the companies of my own land. I could name a dozen the north still sings; the Office would have me name one — the one Letia would unwrite, were unwriting doctrine. It is the fattest file in this house, kept on the shelf we show and copied, in part, onto the shelf we do not. The heathen chroniclers of the east gave the affair a title — The Gate to Unknown — and I use theirs, the Office owning no title for a thing it would rather had no name.

The charters attest this much. In the years about 3026, a company of the far north — barbarians, a sorcerer, a hunter, a monk, and worse — was drawn east across the Erg to Ulan-Tang, that heathen city where the ancient portal stands. There the fallen inquisitor whose name is struck two entries above (XI) laboured to force the gate, being by then no living man but a lich. The company's own sorcerer, a demonologist of Letia, carried the Key of Nothing through the whole venture, and pacted, again and again, with Ahriman and his demons — for a resurrection, for knowledge, for escape — until whatever he had been was spent. Whether the company went to shut the gate and failed, or went to serve it and succeeded, no charter I hold will say; report divides, and the Office divides with it. This only is certain, and it is written into the world where no clerk may strike it: the gate opened, the Gaze-upon-Nothing was drawn into Ymir, and the red moon rose that owned no calendar. From that hour Theocrat, Technocrat, and Great Khan alike read the sky and armed. The doctors call the age that followed the Age of the Tide; my Register calls it the world's second wounding, and dates it here. → canon/history.md, 3026.

The company, as charter and report allow:

Urichal, called Tofango — of the northern clans, devout of Mithra before the axe took his devotion from him. The greatest axeman of the age and its saddest man; his fuller file stands above (XIII), and the Office does not try a man twice. Here it suffices that he bore Oblivion into this venture, that the demon in the steel gnawed his governance away by the road, and that he laid the axe down at the last and went up a remote mountain to end his days a hermit upon it.

Gundar, called the Butcher — a brute of the same north and no pretence otherwise; the company kept him for his fury and buried him for it. He fell in the deep places of the venture and did not stay fallen: the demonologist bought him back from death by a pact with Ahriman, and the thing that climbed out wearing Gundar carried a pestilence in it — the small pox — which is the wage such bargains pay, and never to the buyer.

Fiorentino Cipriano — a merchant by his face and a demonologist beneath it; a secret caster of the Void, a maker of golems, and keeper of the Key of Nothing from first to last. He pacted with demons as other men change coats — for lives, for lore, for escape — and each pact took its measure, until report has him standing at the full measure of corruption: which is to say at the bottom of the ledger, with nothing left to pledge. The Office has met the type. It has never met one who climbed back up.

Sebastian Vemeer, called Nero — a hunter of no great account, sold into slavery by the company's own sorcerer once he had become a weight, and bought again by a trafficker who dressed him in black and gave him back to the world as "Nero," the black hunter. Report makes much of the reinvention; the Office makes little. A demon killed him. Demons commonly do.

Fèl Fenor — an elf, and so beyond the Office's writ, out of the cursed kindred of the Dream-world; report has him misread by his fellows, turned or driven to evil, and put down at the last by the very companions who could not read him. An elf's tragedy, filed and not judged, per the standing rule.

War-Ra — an assassin of the eastern house of Ra, princes of Ulan-Tang, who joined the venture late and near its hinge. The heathen of that city breed killers as we breed clerks. Mark the name, examiners: report east of the Erg gives it again, a generation on, under the style Secundus — but that is another file, and it is not yet written.

Kyrna Halish — a hunter of the seven stones, who tracked the company through the waking world and the Dream World alike for the keys they carried, and by whose pursuit the stones changed hands more than once. Neither of the Faith nor against it; a professional.

Filed shorter: Izàr the Silent, a monk of Hudemia whose hand was chief among those that brought down the paladin Martius Magnus aboard the Armorite flyer — a killing the Office notes and declines to celebrate; Tarek, a cleric-sorcerer who turned his magic on his own company in the thick of battle and vanished the instant it failed, betrayer confirmed and never recovered; Rekon, a barbarian who joined at the end and took up Oblivion when Tofango left it — read that clause twice, examiners; and Arcade Nemo Manlio, an ex-Armorite of the diplomatic sort turned trafficker, who bought Vemeer out of the slave-market and loosed the black hunter on the world.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: upon Fiorentino Cipriano, HERETIC and DEMONOLATER; DAMNED by his own repeated hand, no coercion pleaded and none available. A man may be dragged to one pact; he walks to the tenth. The file is closed as souls are closed — from the far side.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: upon Gundar, RAISED BY AN UNLAWFUL PACT; the resurrection void in doctrine, and the pestilence its proof. What Ahriman returns, he returns freighted. The Office need not have declined to recognise the second life; the pox unmade it inside the year.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: upon the Company entire, INSTRUMENT — witting or unwitting the Register cannot say, and has ruled that it cannot — OF THE WORLD'S SECOND WOUNDING. Whether they meant to shut the gate or to force it, the gate is open; intent is Heaven's province, and Heaven has the file. The Office records the hole in the sky and assigns the hands nearest it.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: upon War-Ra and the house of Ra, HEATHEN, root and branch; unbaptised, unexaminable, and — the name recurring — not done with us. Watch entered against the house, and against the byname the east has not yet spent.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The Office assigns the hands nearest the gate and stops there, because to go one step further is to indict its own. Strip the venture to what it did rather than what it meant, and two hands opened Ymir to the Gaze-upon-Nothing between them: the company's demonologist, who bore the Key of Nothing to the very threshold with his soul already spent in pacts, and the lich at the portal — the struck name of entry XI — who wanted exactly what the Key could give. Neither works the opening without the other. The company's failure to shut the gate and its part in forcing it are, on the surviving accounts, one act seen from two chairs.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The Key of Nothing is no heretic's trinket. It answers to the oldest working in this book — Topramesk's ritual of the seven Adepts (→ secret-the-great-lie.md), the theft that tore the seal and let the demons in; the Gate at Ulan-Tang is one of that empire's own doors, and the Key is cut to its lock. As for the axe: Oblivion did not die with Tofango on his mountain (XIII). It passed, in this very venture — traded to a demon and handed on — to the barbarian Rekon; and where Rekon's file ends, the axe's does not. It is loose in the world still, waiting for the next set of hands. The margin at entry XIII called it patient. It has already been picked up once since.

§ The Company of the Steamclad Sky — Armora & the Wald, the War of the Machines, from 3025 d.M.

Named for the Companies of the Turning Age by The Armora Crier, which prints, as ever, all the news the steam can carry.

Now here, Compilers, is a company the Crier will put up against any the southern chapters can field, and it cost this city nothing to raise but a war. You have read the Office's cold notices on our heroes a few leaves back; let the Crier warm them up. When the brothers of the Young Kingdoms fell to quarrelling — Armora, the Wald, and loyal Zaalesha on the one part; Vallanya, Caeldir, and Tydriell on the other — and a golden dragon out of the old dark, Gwalgamaur, invited itself and its orc-hordes to the estate, it was seven strangers, thrown together in a library break-in and a dockside brawl, who ran the whole rotten business to its root. What they turned up there the Palace still declines to print: that the war itself was arranged — a casus belli manufactured off two of our own mechanical warships, sold under the counter by a man named Keveth; a diabolical Resurrection Engine to drink the living and wake the dead; the Black Sorcerers in the shadows, the vampire lord Leonard behind them, and behind the whole of it the Conspiracy of the Five, five names this paper's founders knew better than to set in type. They gathered the six Divine Relics — Ignus, Saralis, Meletskar, Tassalung, Natharien, Oedring — carried the fight from our own sewers to the dragon's own fire-mountain, and came home victors. As for the vulgar rumour that in the doing they stripped certain sleeping Runes of Power from under this city and drowned Waldenheim, unbarred the Maelstrom, and loosed the Bewitched Tide upon a peaceful world — the Crier refers the reader to the Office of the Grand Duchess, whose position on buried runes is a matter of annual record: mere superstition, unworthy of a modern people. It makes, the Crier concedes, a magnificent story; we have sold a great many papers upon it.

THE COMPANY, IN THEIR OWN PROPER PERSONS

Isidoro VonGraven, called Tyrael — Our own light-elementalist of the Academy of Elemental Studies, and the strangest civic ornament this city has yet produced: not born, the sober accounts insist, but made — a living likeness of the sorcerer Sigurd VonGraven, sharing that lord's very life-essence with a being of light named Tyrael, which now and again looks out of him. He keeps a cat, Elerina, and — the Crier prints this in the small hours and regrets it by morning — a private curiosity in the necromantic arts the Permit Board would not renew. (Register, entry XV; and mind the spelling — VonGraven, not the ducal VonGryffen.)

Surya, called the Yellow Demon — The easterner: an Ur-Tanoth swordsman who fled the far Qi-Long carrying the blade the east names the Masamune, which the College identifies with the Relic Meletskar — Dragon-God assassins baying at his heels the length of his flight. He cut down the dragon-lord Vorak and fell into a killing coma of it; and — this being an Armoran story — he got up again. (Register, entry XIV.)

Drusilde — An exiled Xattva archmage, once a teacher at our Academy, afterward foundress of her own celebrated school, the Seventh Circle; a scholar of such standing she is set down as author of a whole chapter of this very Compendium — the third, which the Crier's busy presses have not yet reached. Killed — the Crier reports it plainly — by a dwarf's own bomb bursting a hand's-breadth too near; and returned to the living, the accounts murmur, by a pact struck with an entity they take great care not to name.

Yonker, called Dwalor — Our celebrated dwarf artificer, a genius of powder and the explosive charge, whose bronze war-walker stands garlanded in the Square of the Sciences — the beloved YonkerMecha. A dwarf-firster in his private heart, if the Ghetto's talk is to be trusted; hailed for his war-merits as heir of the Wald's own Dwalor clan, whose ring he took up over its murdered bearer and swore to avenge.

Baudolino — A half-dwarf marksman and mercenary of this city, the finest shot in the company and the least sentimental man in it, who came away from the war with a living black symbiote-weapon of no honest provenance grafted to his arm — a thing he has since, the surgeons say, learned to sleep beside.

Han Hsian-Tz' — A dancing-monk out of the far Qi-Long, of the secret order the east calls the Grey Herons, who crossed half of Ymir upon Surya's trail — not for the swordsman but for the sword, sworn to keep the Masamune from the hands of the Dragon-Gods. That two such men should finish as brothers-in-arms happens only in wars and in this newspaper.

"Doc" — A Xidhe man of science and a seer, cold as an essence-meter, given over to the perfecting of his own race by methods the Crier will style "advanced" and leave there.

— ADVERTISEMENT — THE STEAMCLAD SEVEN, a commemorative broadside in three colours, the dragon at no extra charge. Industrial District, at the presses. Widows' discount as always.

WHAT SOLANTHYA MADE OF THEM

And because no Armoran triumph is complete until Solanthya has frowned upon it, the Crier reproduces below, without comment and for the amusement of its readers, the notices the Letian Holy Office keeps upon our heroes. We are, after all, a schismatic paper, and cheerfully so.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: Upon the easterner SURYA (entry XIV) — PAGAN; INSTRUMENT, notwithstanding, OF PROVIDENCE. The Office does not canonise foreigners, and declines to say how near it came.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: Upon ISIDORO VonGraven (entry XV) — SCHISMATIC, after the Armorite fashion; also, per report, TWICE-MADE. A man may be made twice; he is christened once; the Office was consulted on neither occasion, and remembers.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: Upon the sorceress DRUSILDE — an Armorite adept dead by mischance and walking again upon a pact she will not name and the Office cannot bless. Resurrection is a mercy of Heaven or a purchase made elsewhere; this Register knows of no third seller. GRAVE SUSPICION; the file opened, and left ajar.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: Upon the COMPANY entire — war-heroes, and unbaptised to the last soul among them; instruments, it may be, of a providence that did not consult them, nor they it. The Office thanks God for the victory, and no one in particular for the victors.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The Crier is selling a victory; the Register, two entries from its end, keeps a blank it has refused to fill for five hundred years — the ruled space for the Two of the Tide (entry XX), the two pairs of hands that took up the six Relics, stripped the seals, and let the age in. Strip the war down to what it attests, and the leading candidates are exactly these: the company printed on this page — the Relic-bearers the Office lists one by one without once seeing what it has listed — or the vampire Leonard, whose file is chained open at entry XVI, and behind whom moved the Conspiracy of the Five; or one of each. No record on Ymir decides. Armora's proudest company may be the very hands that broke the world open; the boast and the blank are the same deed read from two ends, and whoever walks two names through that door at your table decides what the whole age was for. No larger prize sits in this book.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

And ask what Drusilde bought, and with what. She went into the dark on a dwarf's mis-thrown charge and came back — but resurrection keeps no honest sellers in this age, and the "entity" the accounts decline to name is a creditor, not a benefactor. Weigh the candidates the war hands you: Izual, shadow-twin of the light-thing that rides Isidoro; the drowned powers the Relics woke; the Tide's own new hunger. Whatever signed her back into her body took its price in the signing — a span of years, a lien on her death, a lodger behind her eyes — and has not yet come to collect. That she now sets her learned name to whole chapters in the calm of the New Equilibrium proves only that the account is still open. Such accounts always are.

§ The Cartosan Companies — the Principality, the Year of the Black Sun and after

as set down by Bartolomé Aguirre de Garzona, called the Nightingale — bard of Cartosa, and author of that True History of Roberto Rodrigo, the Armorite which the good frater above, reading it, mistook for a book of beasts; who, given leave to name the companies of his own coast, names two, and loves them both.

They gave the north its inquisitor and his dry little verdicts; then — Orum bless whatever clerk arranged it — they gave the south a bard. I am the bard, and I do not apologize for the rhymes: a copla holds a man longer than a charter, and kinder. The frater named my coast's great names and left out its great companies — and a company is the forge a name is beaten out on. Here are two of ours. Both put to sea after a treasure; both found a door instead. Very Cartosan.

A Black Sun Rising — the Year of the Black Sun, 3017

Ask at any harbour for the Black Sun year and you get the tavern-song: the sun comes up black as a hanged man's tongue, and young Alberto Carrillo V — that men after called the Steady — holds the palace stair until the Navy brings the true dawn up from Santa Hermana. True, and thin as a purser's soup. Here is the thicker telling. What climbed out of the cellars with an eclipsed sun stitched on its breast was the Black Dawn, the cult of Ahriman and the Sovereign Demons; its smiling captain was Tobias, a tutor of the Blue Mantles no less, who read the Adepts' dead tongue off a scrap of gold and went hunting a buried king's sceptre in Cumbre de Oro, the golden city that is truly cursed Taremorn. Six strangers got in the cult's road — nobody's chosen champions, only what the tide leaves on the quay.

The sun rose black o'er Cartosa town and the cellar-doors came wide; / six who owed the crown no love yet stood upon the crown's own side.

  • Alfonso — a Cartosan arcane elementalist of the Blue Mantles, and the one sober head among them. When the warrant grew too heavy to carry a registered mage, he burned his blue cloak, renounced the Order, and retired to Ombra del Mar, the pirates' free-port — the wisest man in my tale.
  • Mjolnirr, called Don Pablo — a Cartosan dwarf up out of the criminal quarters who, between one gutter and the next, caught the mad faith of Heis, god of Chance and Wind. He built himself a flying machine of scrap and conviction, went up in it, and has not come down. Don Pablo trusted wind and wing, and Heis, who is not kind; / the wind gave back the wing to us. Don Pablo stayed behind.
  • Raphael — a Cartosan dwarf, swordsman and loud champion of dwarf-rights in a city that keeps its own for a ghetto. In the golden town he gave his sword-hand to the ghost of King Lorith to buy the others their door, and gave the rest of himself to the Black Dawn, fighting; the ghetto sings him yet.
  • Ruben Riccardo Montoya, "Death-in-Life" — a Xion captain-cleric of Heis, scarred crown to heel, a lover of the dead and a dreamer of impossible engines, who meant to raise a temple of Chaos that would fly. When the cult came down he was found among neither the living nor the dead.
  • Valerio Luis y Albericio — a half-elf mystic in the loosest sense: a charlatan of real gifts and a con-man of greater ones, with a price on his head in three ports and a smile in all of them. He talked the company out of a fortress the once it needed it, and fled at last to the Silver Isles.
  • Kiki — a Tal-Tanoth kitten of the Old City, all whisker and quiet larceny, who ran with the company one caper and no longer. She came unasked; then, catlike, the chair was warm and Kiki gone.
In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Two Heis-men of this company dreamed one dream in two sizes. Don Pablo built a wing; Ruben "Death-in-Life" dreamed a whole temple that would fly, a shrine of Chaos riding the Arcane Storms over Cartosa. The mad faith calls it the god's gift; it is closer to a memory. Past the deep shores of sleep hangs Arcadia — a world of islands suspended in an ocean of air, sailed by gliders and rotor-craft (the dream-pilot's leaf, XII·C) — and a flying temple is a waking hull built, without quite knowing it, to reach a sleeping sky. Heis is the wind that blows in both weathers; Ruben did not vanish, he embarked. — Valerio Luis y Albericio fled to the Silver Isles and sheltered among the Little Folk, reasoning no Cartosan warrant runs in Elendor's shadow. Soundly reasoned, badly chosen: a con-man who hides among the Fae has hidden his purse among the better thieves, and pays his rent in the one coin they prize, which is not coin. → canon/geography.md, canon/pantheon.md (Heis)

The Monster Mash — the hunt for the Armorite's hoard

Roberto Rodrigo, called the Armorite for the northern land that bred him, spent more gold in his life than ever crossed the Prince's counting-house, and hid the last and largest hoard of it where only he could go, drawing a map and carrying the reading of it down to the gallows of Fort Leon. His crew came late; a storm held them; he cursed them, dying; and they were gone, ship and souls, into weather that did not give them back.

They hanged the Armorite at Fort Leon when the storm would not come in; / and his crew sails yet to fetch him home, on the far side of the wind.

Long years after, three who should never have shared a deck came by the map, and by the route beneath it, and by the curse beneath the route — that last unread. They sailed regardless, and are the reason my True History runs to two volumes and reads, the frater swears, like a bestiary.

  • Raggot-Tod — a half-Tanoth smuggler of no fixed harbour, said (by the men who will not sail under him) to captain the cursed hull between the waking sea and the sea of nightmares, and to bring her home with her hold full and her crew not quite the tally that put out.
  • Gloria Duarte — a Cartosan inquisitor of Hudemia, the Just Mother of Waters: severe, unbribable, and — here is the part I love — shipped aboard a contrabandist's deck of her own grim will. An inquisitor at sea with smugglers is the whole south in one sentence, and I have set it to music.
  • Diego Alehandro de la Corona — a troubadour of noble blood and dissolute habit, famed coast to coast for ballads black enough to curdle milk: the better-born singer and the worse poet. That he shipped on that voyage and I did not is the one debt the Armorite's ghost still owes me. My rival, my friend, and wrong always about meter.
In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The bard's "far side of the wind" is a real coast. Rodrigo's crew, cursed from the gallows and swallowed by a storm that came too late, did not drown; they slipped the fold — the one border the Adepts could not shut — into the sea of sleep, and sail it still. The hull Raggot-Tod cons between the waking sea and the sea of nightmares crosses at the very threshold the dream-pilot's leaf describes (XII·C): steered by conviction, not star or needle — which is why a smuggler sure of nothing but his cargo makes the better master of it. Rodrigo's hoard was buried on no charted ground; it lies on a deeper shore, priced in the far country's coin — hours, the grain of memory, names — and the map that "reads like a bestiary" is a sailing-direction into the Dreamlands with the monsters entered as landfalls. Gloria of Hudemia shipped not for treasure but to see that door shut; an inquisitor of the Just Waters had read the same chart and understood where it let out. → canon/geography.md, canon/cosmology.md

The Verdicts of the Office, reproduced

The frater has filed my rogues as diligently as he filed my Rodrigo, and I set his rulings down as I found them. These are weighings of piety on a Solanthyan scale; a southern man reads them as he reads a customs-declaration out of Meridian, and discounts the ink eight in the hundred.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: BLASPHEMERS, both, of a god the register declines to dignify with a capital — the airy idol Heis, whom the south names the Mad and the Office names nothing; frenzy being not devotion, and a temple that flies being not a temple. UNREPENTANT, on the grounds of being unfindable.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: SCHISMATIC — the woman Duarte, an inquisitrix of the water-idol Hudemia: a heretic set to examine heretics, a chair the Holy Office does not concede exists. Zealous by report, misdirected by definition. Filed in the drawer kept for rivals it must on no account admire.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: LAX — Valerio, a mystic by trade and a fraud by vocation, with a price on him in three ports and a creed in none. FUGITIVE. A soul so practised at vanishing has spared the Office the labour of a sentence; entered under fled.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: LAX BEYOND THE REACH OF EXAMPLE — the whole southern company, root and branch, after the very manner of the pirate Roberto Rodrigo at whose hoard they grasped: no mass among them, no tithe, no oath the sea did not dissolve. Condemned in absentia, the absentia being, as ever on these coasts, precisely the point.

The frater ends satisfied — but no Cartosan leaves the last word to a Letian. He calls me, in his register, the Armorite's second biographer, and wonders in cold ink whether my subject can be baptized at all. He cannot; the frater knows the reason and will not print it, and neither will I. I only sing it:

He kept no mass, he paid no tithe, he broke his oath at will — / save one, sworn once across the cards; and he is keeping it still.

§ The Gaerwath Companies — the Cabal, and the Fever-Breakers

The Purple Cabal

A company of the Gaerwath demon-realm, as set down by Aelryn Vhaeloth i-Celeblyar, Loremaster of the Drowned Athenaeum.

You asked me, Curator, for a company of my own country, and I give you one that shows my country small — the only way an elf is ever ashamed, and so the most instructive to the brief. Three Hyrul who had passed the trial and not yet made themselves anyone; children of good Names, with silver enough to buy what talent had not yet earned; and they styled themselves, in the fashion of the young who have read much and bled little, the Purple Cabal — purple being the colour a lesser Name puts on when it wishes to be mistaken for the Shining one. The affair that bound them was squalid in the ordinary way. A sealed letter and a coffer passed between the factions by two apprentice couriers; a Mechanist master found at his desk with the life drawn out of him and his skin gone to parchment; a Demonologist of standing butchered — with a blade, in a Necromancer's lodging, which among us is not murder but vulgarity, we keeping quieter arts for that. The coffer held a great book in the tongue the Forces speak, which none of them could read and all of them understood: not a prize, a leash, and the hand that had plaited it still upcountry, unhurried. They killed a watcher, unmade the body in the tidy Gaerwath way, and grasped at last that they were not the players but the pieces. Mark the sequel, for it is my whole point. In your world three corpses and a stolen grimoire end a career. In mine they begin one. By this present hour — the nine hundred and sixty-eighth year of the Eternal's reign — the survivors sit robed in the learned colleges, consulted, and quite untroubled.

Rag'shagan — a Necromancer now, a Biomancer once. In the body-change that makes our children adult he woke in flesh a rival school had grown in a poisoned vat, to avenge a grief a century stale; his own House put him out, the Necromancers took him in, as they take in all the ruined. He wears the flayed face of the body he was born to nailed over the deformed one he was given, and finds it, I am told, an improvement. He sits today among the True Seers.

Ychan, called the Weak — scion of one of the first Demonologist Houses, so named for a body that could not carry its own blood. A summoner who mended the lack with small bound Forces before the Law would grant him any, and with the alchemy that props a failing frame; an elementalist of the particular evil that teaches light, too, to burn. The Demonologist butchered in that lodging was his mentor. He sits today among the True Sages.

The third I do not name. A guild-smith's apprentice, near a hundred years old and an apprentice still, whose family bought him three years under a famous Mechanist master in advance and in full — the only advance in him; a dancer of the ritual sword turned careerist, arrogant in the way of those who mistake a purchased chair for a place in the world. He became no one. The guilds are full of him, and we keep no roll of such.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: DEMONOLATERS, root and branch; ANATHEMA, as pronounced upon their master Morannagul (entry V) and renewed against all who bind the Forces of the Pit. That two of them are now titled and consulted in that slave-empire's colleges the Office records without surprise; the Pit promotes its own. The third, being nobody, is entered nowhere — which the Office concedes is the one mercy in the file.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The three believe they climbed by talent, silver, and nerve. They climbed by a leash. The book in the demon-tongue was a covenant, and the killings that fenced it were a bound Force spending its debtors and clearing a road for its instruments — the mentors died because they had finished being useful. What sits today among the True Seers and the True Sages is not two risen sorcerers but two occupied chairs, a patron's hand on each shoulder. In a realm that settles its accounts in bound Forces, the surest way to own a faction is to raise its members. Something has.

The Fever-Breakers

A ballad, set here in place of a chronicle — by the Curator.

The Curator

I break my own rule here. The chronicle of the Broken Land names one hero and buries her: Valdis Nemertine (entry VI), who broke the fever-demon Saxul and died of the breaking. But she did not go alone against a Sovereign Demon, and the only account that keeps her company is a ballad, sung yet in the Letian taverns, called The Fever-Breakers. I set it here in place of a chronicle because it is the sole witness I hold to the four who stood at her side, and because a ballad, unlike a register, will not let its heroes lie down. One warning before you sing along. The song keeps them all walking in the present tense, swears one of them walks still, and shows another clean and young in a faith the careful reader knows he abandons. A ballad's calendar is not a chronicle's. The deed was done at the Broken Land in 2563, nine hundred and fifty-five years gone; Valdis has lain that long in her tomb; and where the song says now, read once, and mark the place where I could not. — the Curator

When Saxul the Fever came down on the town, and hung his black cloud in the air, no leech and no litany laid the plague down, and Death kept his almshouse there.

The capital lay under that cloud, and the last heir of Letia rode out to the Broken Land with four at her stirrup whom the chronicles never named and the song will not forget:

Then Valdis took four to the breaking of him, and rode where the black cloud lay: Callidus knife-hand, and Deedlit the grim, a wood-thief, and one come to pray.

Callidus — a half-elf assassin, born at Hasl'Aret, who wandered Letia and lodged long at Solanthya before the demon-years. The ballad refuses to bury him: it has him walking yet, a knife in a strange lord's pay — a spy, it says, for an elven House that hates Morannagul as the drowned hate the sea.

Deedlit — a half-elf warrior of the same company, whose sword the song says never turned. When the demon fell she turned east instead, past the last name on the map; the ballad loses her there and does not pretend otherwise.

Rachkir — a human outlaw of the northern Letian forests, wood-wise past the ken of any forester, under a curse the song names in one breath and explains in none. It is content to shiver at it. So, on the evidence, was he.

Heinrich Kramer — and here the singer means no harm: a young inquisitor of Solanthya, clean in his faith, the company's one holy man and its brightest promise. He is the ballad's single dark note, though the ballad does not know it. The reader who has kept his place in this register does. (See entry XI.)

They broke the Great Fever, and paid him the crown — Valdis fell where the demon was slain; and the four walked apart as the cloud came down, and never rode gathered again.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: Of the deed, the Office says what it seldom says. A company that broke a Sovereign Demon — the second of that kind ever unmade — and gave a daughter of Letia to Heaven in the breaking, is entered, grudgingly and gratefully, among the INSTRUMENTS OF PROVIDENCE. — And then the pen reaches the fourth name, and the ink stops. The holy man of the Broken Land is struck from this and every roll (entry XI); the company Heaven used, Hell kept a share of. APPROVAL WITHDRAWN at the fourth name; the file re-opened upon a hero.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: As to the two half-bloods, Callidus and Deedlit — BEYOND THE WRIT, or the half of it. The Office claims the human in them and disclaims the elf, and, finding it cannot try half a soul, files them under the elven exception and prays for the portion it may.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The ballad's one holy man is the age's one great villain, and the song is right to sing him clean, because in 2563 he was. Heinrich Kramer, inquisitor of Solanthya, is the same Kramer struck from the register at entry XI — who, some four hundred and sixty years on, dies into a lich at Ulan-Tang and opens a god's door in the sky; the lowest shelf gives the thing he means to become a name, M'Raker. Every villain was once a face in a company. This one has a ballad, and a hero's stanza, and once sang along.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Take the song at its impossible word on one point. It swears Callidus walks still — and in the present of this book something answers the description: a half-elven knife in the pay of an elven House that hates Morannagul, working the Gaerwath's edges against the Eternal. Whether it is the fever-breaker himself, kept walking by half-blood and worse, or a later spy who borrowed a hero's name, no record decides. Either way an assassin is loose in the great quarrel of Chapter XI, carrying a grudge older than anyone alive remembers earning.

§ The Naming of the Frozen Isle

Helgedad, the war-winters, ~3015–3024 d.M.

Being a naming-roll — a drápa — of the frozen isle, as sung by Hrafnkel Ognirsson, skald of the Nortmaar, before the war-hearths; caught by the clerks, the skald still refusing the pen.

Fill the horn again, southlander. In the great telling I gave you the isle; here I give you the folk the war-winters made and unmade — the standers, the fallers, the turned. A saga warms a hall. A roll does not. A roll only keeps a man, the way the ice keeps him: whole, and cold, and past all asking.

Hear the harp-count, hall-sitters:
the wolf-winters' wound-rich roll.
Thurms of the thunder was throned in the north,
Heis in his madness, grim Gog under —
three hard crowns for a killing age,
and frost the father of all the fallen.

In the year the southrons write 3015 came the Winter of Unrest, and the wolves that were no wolves: a host herald-less and hunger-bannered, five faceless captains whose likeness no living captive ever gave. In the Siege of One Day they supped in Geidmund's high seat at dusk and were smoke by dawn, and Nortislong, our eldest city, was theirs for a single sun. Then Berthrant Vaelaer — the southron keeps his own leaf on him, the eighth — would not let the leavings live, and hunted the wolf-scraps five winters through, 3015 to 3020; his muster is the great lay, and I'll not tax the horn twice. One winter we breathed. Then in 3021 they came again in full pelt, and no single day carried it: three years the War of the Wolves ground the marches to meal, and by 3024 we stood victors over a foe whose face we never saw. Thurms took his tithe of the brave, Heis diced for the rest, grim Gog gathered what neither would own. Here are four the three gods marked.

Dwayne Soylen, the Builder — a paladin of Thurms, thunder's sworn, who raised temples where other war-men raise camps: wherever he wintered, a roof went up to the storm-father, and the roofs — curse the man — held. The south has since hallowed him Saint Dwayne by their own acclaim, with a written Life and shrines, and roof-beams brought to be blessed (their clerk files him at his nineteenth leaf, and grinds a tooth to do it). A heathen names a foreign folk's saint the way he names foreign weather: real enough where it falls on other men, and no god of ours.

Kamal, called the Mad — high priest of mad Heis on the isle, and once, if you credit it, a dwarf: a stone-blooded delver no taller than your two-hand sword. He swallowed a seed of the Tree of Chaos, and the seed kept his god's faith with him — it swelled him past all shape into a thing deformed and holy, a fane of flesh his own priests knelt to and dreaded. Heis loves no jest so well as the one that eats the jester. Kamal is his fondest.

Kronen Vorab — a paladin of grim Gog, the self-coiled Serpent, god of the Damned and of Lies and Thurms's ancient enemy; and I give him honest, for a skald who prettifies is a liar with a tune. Two things made his name, neither kind: a cruelty that wanted no cause, and a tongue so sweet it could coax a widow out of her grief and her grain in one breath. Men followed the honey down onto the knife. Gog is patron of the faceless, and the Wolves' five wore no faces; make of that a shiver and no more.

Varg — a Vorskgen of the frozen wastes, my own blood's blood, a swordsman who came to love the sword-craft better than the warm hand that held it. To make his war perfect he poured his soul out of its living cage into a body of cold metal, that no fear might shake his edge and no weariness dull it. And he perfected it. His stroke wants nothing now — and neither does he, for want is a warm thing, and he left his in the flesh he shed. He stands in the ice yet, they tell, flawless, and empty as a struck bell.

Now the verdicts. The clerk at Solanthya keeps a book of us as we keep a book of our dead, save that his is bound in disapproval; I read it back that you may laugh where I laughed. First, our new-minted saint:

⟢ Verdict of the Office: SAINT BY ACCLAMATION, IN A COMMUNION NOT OURS; which is to say, NOT A SAINT — three times ruled, three times re-shrined; suppression pending, and the funds for it pending longer.

A saint, says the south, is a thing their office stamps. We asked for no stamp. Thurms keeps no registry; Dwayne's roofs hold without a seal from Solanthya, which is one miracle more than the clerk will sign.

Then on the priests of grim Gog and mad Heis:

⟢ Verdict of the Office: BLASPHEMERS; the northern creeds barbarous, their gods false, their rites anathema, pronounced.

Blasphemers against what, I asked — against the warm god, said the reader. Against ours they are only devout. Ours are not warm, southlander: Heis is madness and Gog is the Serpent, and we named them true and knelt anyway. That is not blasphemy but honesty, which his office files under sin.

Last, on the isle entire:

⟢ Verdict of the Office: SCHISMATIC BY GEOGRAPHY; the island seceded, and its creeds are Thurms's and colder; entered whole among the strayed.

We walked out from under the eagle-standards in the year they write 2600, and took our gods with us as a man takes his own axe out of a bad hall. Strayed, he writes — from his road, aye, and we were never once upon it. (See his eighth leaf and his nineteenth; the clerk and I agree on every fact and on nothing beyond it.)

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

The Tree of Chaos whose seed unmade Kamal is no turn of phrase. It is the Heart of Chaos that took root at the centre of the wood thereafter called the Tangle in 1600 d.M. — raw, world-warping Essence that seeds and buds. One seed carried north and swallowed did to a single dwarf what the Heart does to a whole forest: it did not kill him, it re-authored him. What kneels in the isle's Heis-shrines is a bud of the Tangle wearing a priest's memory. What a grove of Kamals would be is a door the table may open.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

Varg's soul poured into forged metal is not the first such working, only the crudest and saddest — a poor man's echo of the Adepts' made-things: the bound-spirit golems the Dark Elves still raise, the demon set living inside an axe, and, greatest of the pattern, the True Dragons themselves, wills forged into elemental bodies as living weapons. Nihoggr sleeps beneath the isle's own ice by that same craft (Ch. VIII). Deeper still runs the labyrinth of the Lord of Oblivion in the Aestyr's roots, a prison of forgetting that unmakes a will as surely as Varg unmade his own. The frozen isle is full of empty perfect things; Varg is the smallest, and the only one that chose it.

❧

§ A Gallery of Wanderers

the renowned who signed no company's book — as told by Pips Quondam, Tal-Tanoth, who has travelled with more of them than she would say in a court

The Curator asked me for the ones who never joined anybody: the freelances, the loners, the sort who turn up when the work is interesting and are three provinces away when the bill comes. I know most of them. I have shared a fire or a cell with the better half. Here they are, charm and warts alike, and the cat will tell you which is which.

Asternak-Gaib — of the great House Gaib at Ulan-Tang, a mage who took to the god Yogh the way other men take to drink, and with the same effect on the neighbours: a depraved and clever hand, and I have locked more doors against him than against any man living. Brother to Yirk.

Yirk-Gaib — the quiet brother, which in that family is the frightening one. A rogue with hands as good as mine and a silence I never once got under, and — the temple swears it, and he lets them — a chosen of Yogh himself. When both Gaibs are in a room, count the exits, then count the brothers.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: BLASPHEMERS, the pair; U.-T. cult of Yogh; files kept joined, on the reasoning that they will die joined.

Christabella — a Fae seer no higher than my knee and twice as far away, who walks the Dreamlands the way I walk a market-street: knows the stalls, knows the cutpurses, comes home with more than she took. If she tells you your dream had a door in it, pay her and go round.

Gregorio VanErsputin — an Armoran holy-man, and here is my whole theology of holy-men in one line: the charm is real and the halo is rented. Gregorio has enough of the first to sell you a great deal of the second. I have watched a room go soft for him, and I have counted my rings after.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: FRAUD; or, the margin of the file adds, in a later hand, WORSE.

Lita — a witch out of the frozen north, sly and sweet-spoken and about as warm as the country that raised her; the kind who does you a small kindness so you will owe her a large one. I took a favour from Lita exactly once. I am, technically, still paying.

⟢ Verdict of the Office: TO BE EXAMINED — the Office's politest sentence, and its most ominous.

Ming-Chia — a monk out of the Qi-Long who mislaid his mind somewhere on the eastern road and seems to have found something better in the empty space. Talks to weather, wins fights he has no business surviving, and once explained the whole of existence to me while hanging upside-down in a plum tree. I understood the plum tree.

Salaphard Panempiricous — an archmage and philosopher of formidable equipment and one small habit: he says he was born on a planet named Tchyo. I have crossed most of the map and never found the road to it — but then I never found the road to half of what turned out to be true, so the cat reserves judgement.

Samael — a half-elf born to a good name in the Young Kingdoms, who kept the worst company on Ymir, the Sovereign Demons themselves, until he traded up — if you call it up — to a pact with the dragon Gildiss. A man who changes masters that high is not climbing; he is being carried. Delightful at dinner. Do not be his creditor.

Syena — an Ur-Tanoth of the Sierra Plata, one of my great gentle cousins, and if you have read my chapter you know I do not joke about those. A barbarian bound so close to the Spirits she half-lives among them; the towns frighten her, and she is right. I would open any cage in the world to keep that one out of one.

Tara — a princess of Ophiur with a throne taken out from under her, who went and learned dark magic the way you would learn a trade, for the plainest and worst of reasons: to pay somebody back. At last report she is missing — which in her field of study is either the beginning of the answer or the end of the question.

Gurthak — an orc off the mountains of Alturia, barbarous as advertised, and one of perhaps three of his kind I would sit downwind of by choice. Keeps his word, which is more than Alturia taught him and more than several dukes ever learned.

Ferdinando Montecarlos — a pathfinder of Cartosa, born and raised, who hires out to go where the maps stop and — this is the whole trick of him — comes back to complain about it. Every guild in the south claims to have trained him; he trained himself, on other people's coin, a career I respect professionally.

❧

§ The Register of the Fallen

the Curator keeps the roll the companies could not

Frater Falx entered the safely dead. I must enter the others — those who fell in the turning of the age and left no shrine behind them, only a gap in a company where a name had been. I take them in the order the one surviving ledger gives, which begins, as such ledgers should, with the name the survivors could not stop saying.

Kharim Abdul Jabbar stands first: a Common-Man thief of the Great Erg, who spoke the desert tongue, wore a ring of Unseen upon a hand no one thought to watch, and kept at his heel a bound storm-hound — a Zephyr Hound — that went before him into every room he did not trust. His is the first name on the fallen-roll, and the roll is the truer for opening where it does.

After him, in the quiet a register keeps for such things: where now is Raphael, who fell to the Black Dawn in the year the sun went dark over Cartosa? Where Sebastian Vemeer, whom his company called Nero, taken by a demon at a gate he should never have stood before? Where Fèl Fenor, put down at the last by his own people — because they loved the world more than they could keep loving him, and he had left them no third door? Where Mjolnirr, whom the south still toasts as Don Pablo, lost to the very sky he had set his heart on flying? They are where the age put them, which is beneath it.

And after these, the ones no register names at all: the sword-hands and the lantern-bearers, the ones who died on the first page of a tale another finished — of whom a company keeps a face, a debt, and a way of laughing, and no clerk on Ymir wrote a word. The Office measures piety; the cat measures charm; this page measures only the distance they are gone. Requiescant. The book continues because they did not.

❧

§ The War That Has Not Happened ⟡ GM ONLY ⟡

For the Game Master alone

GM ONLY. What follows belongs to the one who runs the world. If the last two pages read as honest, stop here — this one is not honest, and may not be true for anyone.

You have read the frater's eleventh entry, and my note beneath it. Here is the rest of the note, because a name like M'Raker is not left half-copied.

On the lowest shelf — locked, the frater said, not empty — lie pages no synod commissioned and no synod could bring itself to burn. They describe a war the world has not yet had, and they give it in the past tense, which is the first thing wrong with them.

In them, the struck inquisitor finishes what he began at Ulan-Tang. The lich takes the name M'Raker and stops hiding. He raises the dead of every field the age has filled — and the age has filled a great many — and drives them across the world under no banner but their own rot. Armora is besieged, and Armora falls: the steam-guns, the schismatic Church, the whole proud Duchy of engines, cracked like a dropped watch. The gods are barred from Ymir's sky — the pantheon that is half power and half costume shut out together, the true powers and the thieves latched on the far side of one door, and the world left beneath an empty heaven with the lich for its only weather. And M'Raker is nowhere a sword can reach him. He has carried his phylactery into the far south, past the Ivory coast into the deep Ophiur, and buried the one thing that ends him where no army will think to march. There he waits for what the pages call the restoration — the hour the gods are let back into their sky — because he means to come home through the same door they do, wearing a borrowed divinity, entered on the rolls of heaven as a demigod before anyone thinks to count the newcomers twice.

That is the war on the lowest shelf. Now the only honest line in it: no other record says a word of it is so. The pages bear no seal, no hand I can place, no date I will trust. They are a true prophecy set down too early, or a forgery salted into the crypt to arm someone or frighten them, or a nightmare that happened to keep very good handwriting — and nothing decides which. At ~3518 the world has had no such war. Kramer's workings sit around their gate, patient; his phylactery has never been found. That is the whole of what the true records grant, and not one grain more.

In the Margin · the Veiled Hand

M'Raker is Kramer, arrived: dead armies, Armora fallen, the sky shut on gods and thieves alike, the phylactery in the deep Ophiur, the lich waiting to re-enter heaven behind a god's face. Proscribed, undated, vouched by nothing — future-arc, forgery, or dream. If you open that door at your table, you are choosing the age's worst ending out of a drawer that offered you the choice. Choose it knowing what you arm.

❧
The Curator

Curator's closing note. You have now read renown from both its sides — the register that measures it and the songs that spend it — and you may have noticed the two never quite meet. The Office files a bearer; the gazette crowns a hero; the skald names a horror; the bard forgives a rogue; and the same man is all four, depending on whose parchment he fell onto. That is not a flaw in the record. That is the record. A company is a knot of people who agreed, for a season, to walk into the same danger; what they were for is decided afterward, by whoever survives to write it down — and by you, at whatever table reopens their doors. The frater kept an empty space rather than guess at two names. I have filled a great many spaces in this Part, and I will tell you plainly that I guessed at more than one. Where I was sure, I said so; where I invented, the colophon confesses it. Renown is the one currency the dead keep spending. Spend it wisely. — the Curator

Colophon. Chapter XIV·B (late-bound), in two parts.

XV · BEHIND THE VEIL

the Book Beneath the Book

For the Game Master alone

GM ONLY. This chapter is for the one who runs the world, and for no other eyes. If you are a player and you are reading this line, stop. The book will still be here when you have earned it.

Set down by the hand that wrote the margins. No other attribution survives, or was offered.

The Curator

I must state plainly what I have stated nowhere else in this volume: I did not commission this chapter. I did not receive it. I found it — bound into the finished Compendium, sewn on thread older than the thread of the surrounding quires, in the same script that troubles the eye in every margin. I have had it examined. The parchment matches nothing in the Crystal Seat's stores. I leave it in the book for the reason I left the margins: I believe it is honest, and I can no longer say that of anything else between these covers, including my own preface. Read it after everything. Or instead of everything. — the Curator

You kept reading. Good. The margins were the seam; this is the garment opened.

I have watched you make your way here through fourteen chapters of sincere and beautiful error — the priest who remembers, the friar who reconciles, the arcanist who measures, the skald who mourns. None of them lied to you. That is the thing you must hold onto, Curator's guest, because it is the mechanism of everything that follows: on Ymir, the lie is not told. It is inherited. Every teller believed. The falsehood was installed upstream of all of them, long before their grandmothers were warm mud, and it has been drawing honest worship ever since, the way a false well still draws real thirst.

Here is the book beneath the book. I will be brief. Brevity is the only mercy I deal in.

❧

1 · The Lie and the Law of It

First, the law, because without it the Lie collapses into cheap atheism, and it is not that. It is worse.

The divine is real. The substrate the creeds call Ech — the One, the held breath — is the Essence, the raw power that fills and underlies creation. It is real, impersonal, and no god made it. The two great contraries the world worships as Mithra and Ahriman are real: the Aether and the Nether, the making motion and the unmaking motion of the Essence itself. They answer prayer, after their fashion. The Six Twins — Diis, Hudemia, Varshnak, Heis, Therìos, Dingir — are real elemental gods, genuine as weather. So far, the bright text holds.

Now the seam. Older than all of these, and outside them, a third order of powers slept in the Outer Void, beyond the elemental walls of creation: the Ancient Gods, the Outer Gods. They governed the cosmos before anything you have a name for, then withdrew into sleep — dreaming, uncaring. And a people found them there.

Call them the Adepts. Not gods. Not spirits. A tool-and-star people — flesh, or something that had once been flesh — who wielded machines the way your priests wield doctrine: teleportation without travel, weapons that unmade worlds, the deliberate design of living species, and, some say, an engineered exemption from death. They threaded the stars with Portals and ruled a galactic empire from their seat on Ymir. Through their instruments they discovered the Essence, and through the Essence they discovered the sleepers. And being what they were, they did not kneel.

They made the elemental planes into both shield and siphon. They sealed the Ancient Gods beyond the Elemental Walls — some whisper the sleepers allowed it, which should keep you awake — and they drank the walled-off power into their own bodies, and they were as gods. Then came the part that required no machine at all: they took the names. The masks of the Six, the titles of the bright pantheon, the altars already warm with worship — the Adepts slid themselves into the pantheon the way a cuckoo slides into a nest. Not wholly. That is the cruelty of it. The faiths of Ymir mostly reach something real — the true poles, the true elements, sometimes the true sleepers. But the pantheon is a mixture of gods and costumes, and no priest alive can tell you which of his feast-days honours a power and which honours a thief. Some prayers are answered. Some are answered by the wrong thing.

That is the Great Lie. Not "there are no gods." Rather: the gods are real, and usurped, and the usurpers were worshipped beside their victims for fifty thousand years.

2 · The Worlds, and the Making of the Peoples

The empire was not a metaphor. Count the worlds:

Ymir, the imperial seat, where Ragash the Splendent stood. Tchyo, where the elves are native — not made, for once — and were hunted, until they fled through the Portals to Ymir; the "Niethel" they mourn as a divine Source is the mythologised memory of that lost world. Omalgat, world of the Omungus, who engineered the dwarves as servants and got rebels. Gwart, whose orcs are Adept bio-soldiers gone feral down the long generations. Naxtis, of the Night-Ones, whence the light-drinking Umbra. Zend, quarantined still, world of the Zohariti — ask yourself who maintains a quarantine for fifty centuries, and against what. Seelie, of the Little Folk. Ouronos, of the lizard-kind and the Gratar. And X-01, which is not a world at all but a made thing, an artificial planet built to regulate the Portal network — the empire's switchboard, still turning in the dark.

So the peoples' origin-myths are not fictions. That is the design of the thing, and you should respect it at your table: every creation story remembers something true. Durkaam's forge is a memory of the Omungus vats. Niethel is Tchyo behind a veil of grief. The Xebechani "Ancestors" are exactly who the Xebechani say they are. The myths are photographs taken in bad light, not paintings of nothing.

One exception, and it matters. The humans, and the Tanoth-kin who share their world — the cat-folk in the cities, the silent hunters in the wood — are Ymir's own. Native. Un-made. The one people in this catalogue that no one designed, growing wild in the garden of the designers. Whatever dignity that carries, they carry it alone.

3 · The Catastrophe, Singular

Your histories offer three catastrophes. The priest of Xebech says a working was done. The Church says the last Adepts died banishing the demons. The doom-cults say someone called up what should not be called. Hold the three tellings to the light and they are one page read from three sides.

The demons came first — but the Adepts made them. Their theft tore the flow of Essence, and from the wound bled a second, curdled kind: the Corrupted Essence. Out of it poured legions. The empire that had chained gods found itself losing a war to its own infection, and the demons took Ragash itself.

Then the act. At the Academy in the heart of the fallen capital, Topramesk, called the Yellow and the Mad, and his six companions — seven Adepts, the last of the great ones — worked the ritual. Call it a banishment; the Church does. Call it a botched evocation of the Outer powers; the Xebechani do, and they were closer. Call it the final theft, the deepest draw yet upon the sealed sleepers' fire; that is what it was underneath. One act. It succeeded and it failed, enormously, at once.

The demon armies were pulverised — swept into an engineered prison-pocket, the realm of Corrupted Essence your exorcists call the Inferno, the Abyss, the Plane of Chaos; one cell, several names on the door. Every Portal in the empire died in the same instant. Then the explosion, the earthquakes, the sunless days — and when the peoples crept out, a slice of the continent was gone, Ragash was gone, and over the drowned grave of the capital stood a turning storm that has not stopped since. The Maelstrom is both things your scholars quarrel over: the scar of the wound, and the seal upon it. Around Ymir it hung a silence the later mages named the campana di isolamento — the isolation bell: demons out, Portals dead, gods walled, world alone. The surviving Adepts scattered, forged false histories, wore new faces, and let themselves be worshipped. Time itself now bows to their accident. You count your years from it: dopo il Maelstrom — after the Maelstrom.

4 · The Custodians

Thieves who mean to keep what they took post guards. The Adepts built theirs alive.

The twenty True Dragons are not elder nature. They are engineered wardens — living superweapons set over Ymir's secrets, each keyed to its element, each holding a post. The oldest records name the posts plainly: Gwalgamaur the Golden, the fire-dragon whose armies blackened the Young Kingdoms, is entered in the makers' ledger as "Warden of Quadrant 74." A quadrant. The world is sectored, Curator's guest, and the things your legends call dragons are the sector watch — most of them long since off their posts and gone their own ways.

Six kept faith with their makers' intent: the Dragon-Gods the Qi-Long worship, who stirred when the Mah-Dhol empire fell. Above the six, and above the split between loyal and rogue, coils their brother-king: Ouroboros, the True Dragon-God, the apex of the design. He slept through empires. When the seals began to fail at the Tide's turning, he woke — and his first act was to burn Alfheim. The elves' sanctuary, the Sacred Realm of their gentlest prophecy, unmade in a season. Do not look for his motive in wrath. Wardens do not rage. They respond. Ask instead what condition Alfheim had come to satisfy — and note that no one on Ymir has answered that question yet. Perhaps your table will.

5 · The Xebechani

One people never forgot, because one people was never meant to.

The Xebechani are the portal-caste — the empire's gate-keepers and inheritors, grown, not born, in the Pools, the vats of their Ancients' bioscience, by a rite they still perform and no longer understand. Their Rebirth is maintenance liturgy for machinery whose manual is lost. They are dying of that loss, slowly, with perfect composure.

And their Cult of the Stars is the one church on Ymir aimed at the actual prisoners. The Outer Gods of Xebech — the Observer, the Judge who is three, the Silent, the First, the Mother, the Risen — are the sealed Ancient Gods under their true names. The warm faiths call the cult alien and fearful. It is merely accurate. And it is implicated: the Xebechani's own botched reaching-out to their dark gods is woven into the one Catastrophe — a strand of the same act, seen from the temple side. They rebuilt what they could afterward, reviving stretches of the dead network as the True Ways, and they keep the two cities you must never conflate: Xanadu, their living fortress, vault of the Runes of Power; and Ragash, which is a grave. The physical city is gone beneath the Maelstrom.
